<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--4</p>
<p>Introduction:.</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>In  barbaric  worlds  of  fantasy  and  far-flung </p>
<p>galaxies,  great  heroes  battle  for  gold,  glory, </p>
<p>justice, or mere survival. Some wear mithril armor </p>
<p>and wield massive swords glowing with magical </p>
<p>energy.  Others  are  commandos  in  the  latest </p>
<p>ballistic vests spraying lead from their submachine </p>
<p>guns. Some aren’t even human.</p>
<p>But  they  are  all  heroes,  and  their  epic  tales </p>
<p>inspire those who read them. This game system </p>
<p>attempts  to  simulate  these  incredible  tales—at </p>
<p>least in our imaginations—giving form, structure, </p>
<p>and  challenge  to  the  heroes  and  the  savage </p>
<p>worlds they walk in with rules that are simple on </p>
<p>the surface and comprehensive in their depth.</p>
<p>The  game  focuses  on  the  action  rather  than </p>
<p>statistics  and  bookkeeping,  allowing  the  Game </p>
<p>Master to concentrate on the player characters, </p>
<p>their foes, and the fantastic settings they battle in.</p>
<p>For players, <i>Savage Worlds</i> has an extremely </p>
<p>rich  advancement  system  that  lets  you  create </p>
<p>everything  from  a  swashbuckling  rogue  to  a </p>
<p>charismatic investigative reporter.</p>
<p>Some “generic” rules systems come up lacking </p>
<p>in  certain  settings. <i> Savage  Worlds</i>  avoids  this </p>
<p>with Setting Rules (you’ll find a list of common </p>
<p>Setting  Rules  on  page  108). These  allow  the </p>
<p>Game Master to fundamentally alter the feel of </p>
<p>the game without changing the basic mechanics. </p>
<p>Players can explore haunted space hulks in power </p>
<p>armor, battle dragons, or surf the matrix of a virtual </p>
<p>computer world without having to learn all-new </p>
<p>rules. But throw in something like Righteous Rage </p>
<p>from Solomon Kane®—which makes a hero far </p>
<p>more deadly in his most desperate moments—and </p>
<p>the entire feel of the game changes in an instant.</p>
<p>What’s.New?</p>
<p>This deluxe version of the <i>Savage Worlds</i> rules </p>
<p>system is the culmination of a decade’s play with </p>
<p>suggestions  and  comments  gathered  from  our </p>
<p>own campaigns and the thousands of adventures </p>
<p>we’ve run at game stores and conventions across </p>
<p>the  globe.  For  those  who  have  already  played </p>
<p>we think you’ll find this the most complete and </p>
<p>comprehensive  edition  of  the  rules  we’ve  ever </p>
<p>published.  We’ve  compiled  the  best  material </p>
<p>from our setting books and added in web updates </p>
<p>like  gritty  damage  rules  and  interludes.  Races </p>
<p>and vehicles are back from the first edition, as </p>
<p>well as the race creation rules from the Fantasy </p>
<p>Companion and a consolidated list of all the best </p>
<p>powers from our many expansions.</p>
<p>Veterans of our game will also find a number </p>
<p>of Design Notes scattered throughout the book. </p>
<p>These are insights into why a particular rule is </p>
<p>written the way it is and the thinking that went </p>
<p>into it.</p>
<p>For  those  who  are  new,  we  welcome  you  to </p>
<p>the  “Savages,”  and  encourage  you  to  join  one </p>
<p>of  the  most  friendly,  helpful,  and  enthusiastic </p>
<p>communities in gaming on our forums and mailing </p>
<p>lists.</p>
<p>And if you already own Savage Worlds and want </p>
<p>a list of all the changes or additions we’ve made </p>
<p>here <b>completely free, </b>just go to our website at </p>
<p>www.peginc.com!</p>
<p>Now let your imagination wander to worlds of </p>
<p>danger and adventure, where heroes are larger </p>
<p>than life and monsters stalk the land. These are </p>
<p>savage  worlds—and  those  who  survive  them </p>
<p>become legends...</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--5</p>
<p>Getting.Started</p>
<p>You’ll need a few other items besides this book </p>
<p>to begin your journey:</p>
<p>Dice</p>
<p><i>Savage  Worlds</i>  uses  traditional  gaming  dice: </p>
<p>4-sided,  6-sided,  8-sided,  10-sided,  12-sided, </p>
<p>and some settings use a 20-sided die as well. As </p>
<p>a player, you’ll also want a “Wild Die,” a d6 of a </p>
<p>different color which we’ll explain in Chapter One. </p>
<p>Dice are available from your favorite local gaming </p>
<p>store, or online directly from Pinnacle.</p>
<p>We abbreviate the different dice as d4, d6, d8, </p>
<p>d10,  d12,  and  d20.  If  you  see  something  like </p>
<p>2d6+1, that means to roll two six-sided dice, add </p>
<p>the two together, then add 1 to the total.</p>
<p>Players need only one set of dice. The Game </p>
<p>Master might want a couple of sets so she can </p>
<p>roll attacks for several villains at once.</p>
<p>The.Action.Deck</p>
<p><i>Savage Worlds</i> uses a standard deck of playing </p>
<p>cards with the Jokers left in. Cards are used for </p>
<p>initiative in combat and to help keep things moving </p>
<p>fast and furious. You’ll even find an official set of </p>
<p><i>Savage Worlds </i>cards where you found this book!</p>
<p>A.Setting</p>
<p>Will  you  and  your  friends  explore  post-</p>
<p>apocalyptic  ruins<i>?</i>  Lead  a  rugged  warband  in </p>
<p>your favorite fantasy world? Take on the role of </p>
<p>vampire lords? Or perhaps fight evil in the many </p>
<p>theaters of <i>Weird Wars?</i></p>
<p>Pick up the book for your favorite game setting </p>
<p>at  your  local  game  store,  or  create  your  own. </p>
<p>Official Savage Settings<b> </b>include new Edges and </p>
<p>Hindrances, Setting Rules, spells, weapons, gear, </p>
<p>monsters, and more! We talk more about some of </p>
<p>our settings on pages 7 through 15.</p>
<p>The.Adventure.Deck</p>
<p>We also make an exciting Adventure Deck that </p>
<p>adds an additional aspect of player control to the </p>
<p>game. Your hero might find a new romance, make </p>
<p>a lifelong enemy, or do additional damage in that </p>
<p>critical fight. Look for it on our website or in better </p>
<p>game and hobby stores.</p>
<p>Using.Miniatures</p>
<p>The rules are written for the table-top because </p>
<p>that  requires  exact  measurements  and  precise </p>
<p>rules.  That’s  why  all  the  weapon  ranges  and </p>
<p>movement values are listed in inches (rather than </p>
<p>yards or some other unit). Using miniatures and </p>
<p>terrain or a battle-mat can really help your players </p>
<p>understand the tactical situation and better interact </p>
<p>with their environment, and we highly recommend </p>
<p>this style of play for most games.</p>
<p>But  miniatures  certainly  aren’t  required,  and </p>
<p>you’ll find rules for “guesstimating” ranges, how </p>
<p>many foes are caught in a blast radius, and other </p>
<p>issues on page 73.</p>
<p>If you do decide to use miniatures, check the </p>
<p>Pinnacle  website  for  metal  miniatures  for  our </p>
<p>games  as  well  as  cardboard  and  cardstock </p>
<p>variants.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--6</p>
<p>Savage.Settings</p>
<p>Pinnacle has created many Savage Settings, from </p>
<p>our award-winning Deadlands and its spin-offs Hell </p>
<p>on Earth and Lost Colony to Rippers and Necessary </p>
<p>Evil and Weird Wars. We’ve also licensed some of </p>
<p>our favorite worlds, such as Frank Chadwick’s Space </p>
<p>1889® and The Savage World of Solomon Kane®. </p>
<p>A few of our settings, such as Andy Hopp’s brilliant </p>
<p>Low Life, are published by Pinnacle but owned by </p>
<p>their creators.</p>
<p>You’ll find a sneak peek at a few of these settings </p>
<p>on the following pages.</p>
<p>Plot.Point.Campaigns</p>
<p>Like our rules system, our settings are designed </p>
<p>to be easy for the Game Master to run. That’s why </p>
<p>many  of  our  Savage  Settings  feature  “Plot  Point </p>
<p>Campaigns,”  a  backstory  to  the  campaign  world </p>
<p>and a set of adventures that resolve the main plot. </p>
<p>Interspersed between these Plot Points are Savage </p>
<p>Tales—short adventures that can be run depending </p>
<p>on where the party goes and what they do. Of course </p>
<p>a Game Master can also insert his own adventures </p>
<p>into the campaign, including those based around the </p>
<p>player characters’ backstories.</p>
<p>The idea is to communicate a big backstory—like </p>
<p>the flooding of the world in 50 Fathoms—but still give </p>
<p>Game Masters the freedom to construct and run their </p>
<p>own stories in the foreground. In this game, the heroes </p>
<p>are a crew of explorers, traders, or even pirates sailing </p>
<p>about a drowning world. They can seek out whatever </p>
<p>destiny they choose, but will become entangled in the </p>
<p>fight against the Sea Hags—the creatures destroying </p>
<p>Caribdus—as well.</p>
<p>Savage.Tales</p>
<p>The adventures in our books are called “Savage </p>
<p>Tales,” short and direct adventure outlines the Game </p>
<p>Master can run with only a few minutes of preparation. </p>
<p>We do this by cutting to the  most  important  details </p>
<p>of an encounter.</p>
<p>Ideally, </p>
<p>a </p>
<p>Game </p>
<p>Master  can  find  out </p>
<p>what  his  group  is </p>
<p>interested in doing that </p>
<p>session, read no more </p>
<p>than a page or so, and </p>
<p>be  ready  to  run  the </p>
<p>game. He can always </p>
<p>put  more into it if he </p>
<p>wishes,  but  all  the </p>
<p>crucial ingredients for </p>
<p>running  a  fun  game </p>
<p>are there in detail.</p>
<p>Savagery</p>
<p>If you decide you like Savage Worlds, you might </p>
<p>want  to  check  out  some  of  our  settings  and  other </p>
<p>resources. Some basic information is found below, </p>
<p>and you can find more detail, free downloads, and </p>
<p>support materials at our website: www.peginc.com.</p>
<p>Settings</p>
<p>From space opera to Western to Puritan adventures, </p>
<p>we make a lot of exciting settings. Our philosophy </p>
<p>has always been to do things a little differently than </p>
<p>everyone  else.  Most  of  our  worlds  have  a  unique </p>
<p>twist  we  think  makes  them  special.  For  example, </p>
<p>Evernight begins in a typical fantasy world of elves </p>
<p>and dwarves—but quickly changes as strange spikes </p>
<p>drop  from  the  heavens  and  spill  forth  something </p>
<p>terrible from beyond the stars.</p>
<p>Some of our largest game lines, such as Deadlands, </p>
<p>are open settings with multiple Plot Point Campaigns </p>
<p>presented  separately.  For  example,  The  Flood </p>
<p>revolves around fighting Reverend Ezekiah Grimme </p>
<p>in what’s left of California after the Great Quake—the </p>
<p>Maze. Stone and a Hard Place is all about the game’s </p>
<p>deadliest  gunslinger  and  killer  of  heroes:  Stone. </p>
<p>Stand-alone  adventures  such  as  Coffin  Rock,  or </p>
<p>add-ons like the Smith &amp; Robards Catalog, featuring </p>
<p>dozens of steampunk inventions, are also available.</p>
<p>Companions</p>
<p>Our  Companions  focus  on  new  Edges  and </p>
<p>Hindrances, powers, monsters, and Setting Rules for </p>
<p>particular genres such as Fantasy, Horror, Science </p>
<p>Fiction, and more.</p>
<p>Adventures</p>
<p>Full-length adventures are available for most of our </p>
<p>settings, as well as unique tales set in worlds of their </p>
<p>own, such as Zombie Run, a survivor adventure set </p>
<p>in America after a worldwide outbreak of undead.</p>
<p>Accessories</p>
<p>We’ve  already  mentioned  our  official Action  &amp; </p>
<p>Adventure  Decks.  We  also  manufacture  custom </p>
<p>Bennies for each of our lines—custom poker chips </p>
<p>with images specific to the setting. Licensees also </p>
<p>make  plastic  templates,  dice,  and  other  specialty </p>
<p>items. Check our webstore at www.peginc.com for </p>
<p>the latest.</p>
<p>Forums</p>
<p>We’re very proud of our forums, and think we have </p>
<p>the best community in all of gaming. If you have a </p>
<p>question, visit the forums and the other Savages, or </p>
<p>our own writers, will happily answer it for you. You can </p>
<p>also find new world, campaign, or adventure ideas, </p>
<p>groups to play with, and Game Mastering advice, all </p>
<p>from a friendly and welcoming community that loves </p>
<p>to game.</p>

<p><b>technology  of  a  long-dead  race  to  threaten  to  repel  the </b></p>
<p><b>“alien” invaders forever.</b></p>

<p>Weird.Wars:.Weird.War.Two</p>

<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>The.Savage.World.of.Solomon.Kane®</p>
<p><i><b>A landless man. A Wanderer. A Puritan.</b></i></p>

<p><b>Kane punishes the wicked. Hundreds have fallen before his saber and pistols.</b></p>
<p><b>But rarely, Kane finds one who wants to atone—a tainted soul who realizes the evil he’s done </b></p>
<p><b>in his life. These hopeful few are tasked to wander the earth as Kane does, searching out the </b></p>

<p><b>will find redemption. A few will become heroes.</b></p>
<p><b>All will walk the Path of Kane.</b></p>

<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Rippers</p>
<p><i><b>Stalk the creatures of the night—or become one!</b></i></p>
<p><b>Rippers are a secret cabal of scholars, warriors, sages, and heroes who battle legendary </b></p>
<p><b>horrors around the globe. But willpower, science, technology, and steel are not their only </b></p>
<p><b>weapons. Abraham Van Helsing and “Dr. Jack” have shown the Rippers how to extract the </b></p>
<p><b>essences or natural tools of the creatures they battle—and implant it in themselves.</b></p>
<p><b>Those  who  survive  the  process  may  become  fearsome </b></p>
<p><b>warriors, fighting with the claws of a werewolf or the teeth of a </b></p>
<p><b>piranha-man. Others go mad from the procedure and become </b></p>
<p><b>that which they once hunted.</b></p>
<p><b>And at least one has become the greatest traitor the Rippers </b></p>
<p><b>have ever known…</b></p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--15</p>
<p>Chapter.Goes.Here</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--16</p>
<p>Chapter.One:.</p>
<p>Characters</p>
<p>Great  heroes  are  more  than  a  collection  of </p>
<p>statistics  and  numbers,  but  in  a  game  system </p>
<p>this is certainly where they begin. To make your </p>
<p>hero, download a <i>Savage Worlds</i> character sheet </p>
<p>from our website (www.peginc.com) or copy the </p>
<p>one found at the back of this book and follow the </p>
<p>steps below.</p>
<p>1..Race</p>
<p>Humans are the most common characters, but </p>
<p>some settings<b> </b>may feature bizarre aliens, graceful </p>
<p>elves, or other exotic races—some examples of </p>
<p>which you’ll find on page 20. You can choose to </p>
<p>play any race available in your particular setting.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Humans  are  the  standard  race  in <i> Savage </i></p>
<p><i>Worlds, </i>and start play with one free Edge </p>
<p>(see Step 3).</p>
<p>2..Traits</p>
<p>Characters are defined by attributes and skills, </p>
<p>collectively called “Traits,” and both work in exactly </p>
<p>the same way. Attributes and skills are ranked by </p>
<p>die types, from a d4 to a d12, with d6 being the </p>
<p>average for adult humans. Higher is better!</p>
<p>Attributes</p>
<p>Every character starts with a d4 in each attribute, </p>
<p>and has 5 points with which to raise them. Raising </p>
<p>a d4 to a d6, for example, costs 1 point. You’re free </p>
<p>to spend these points however you want with one </p>
<p>exception: no attribute may be raised above a d12.</p>
<p><b>• </b></p>
<p><b>Agility </b>is your hero’s nimbleness, quickness, </p>
<p>and dexterity.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p><b>Smarts</b>  is  a  measure  of  how  well  your </p>
<p>character knows his world and culture, how </p>
<p>well he thinks on his feet, and mental agility.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p><b>Spirit </b>reflects inner wisdom and willpower. </p>
<p>Spirit  is  very  important  as  it  helps  your </p>
<p>character recover from being Shaken (see </p>
<p>page 77).</p>
<p>• </p>
<p><b>Strength</b> is raw physical power and general </p>
<p>fitness.  Strength  is  also  used  to  generate </p>
<p>your  warrior’s  damage  in  hand-to-hand </p>
<p>combat.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p><b>Vigor</b> represents endurance, resistance to </p>
<p>disease, poison, or toxins, and how much </p>
<p>pain and physical damage a hero can shake </p>
<p>off.</p>
<p>Skills</p>
<p>Skills  are  learned  abilities  such  as  Shooting, </p>
<p>Fighting,  scientific  knowledge,  professional </p>
<p>aptitudes,  and  so  on. These  are  very  general </p>
<p>descriptions  which  cover  all  related  aspects. </p>
<p>Shooting, for example, covers all types of guns, </p>
<p>bows,  rocket  launchers,  and  other  ranged </p>
<p>weapons.</p>
<p>You  have  15  skill  points  to  distribute  among </p>
<p>your skills. Each die type costs 1 point (starting </p>
<p>at d4) as long as the skill is equal to or less than </p>
<p>the attribute it’s linked to (listed beside the skill in </p>
<p>parentheses). If you exceed the attribute, the cost </p>
<p>becomes 2 points per die type.</p>
<p>As  with  attributes,  no  skill  may  be  increased </p>
<p>above d12.</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b>  Fighting  is  linked  to Agility. A </i></p>
<p><i>character with a d8 Agility can buy Fighting </i></p>
<p><i>for one point per die type to d8. Buying a </i></p>
<p><i>d10 costs 2 points, and a d12 costs another </i></p>
<p><i>2 points.</i></p>
<p>Derived.Statistics</p>
<p>Your  character  sheet  contains  a  few  other </p>
<p>statistics you need to fill in, described below.</p>
<p><b>Charisma </b> is  a  measure  of  your  character’s </p>
<p>appearance,  manner,  and  general  likability.  It’s </p>
<p>0  unless  you  have  Edges  or  Hindrances  that </p>
<p>modify it. Charisma is added to Persuasion and </p>
<p>Streetwise rolls, and is used by the GM to figure </p>
<p>out how nonplayer characters react to your hero.</p>
<p><b>Pace </b> is  how  fast  your  character  moves  in  a </p>
<p>standard  combat  round.  Humans  walk  6”  in  a </p>
<p>round and can move an additional 1d6” if they run. </p>
<p>Write “6” on your character sheet beside the word </p>
<p>Pace. This is 6” on the table-top—every inch there </p>
<p>represents 2 yards in the “real world.”</p>
<p><b>Parry </b>is equal to 2 plus half your character’s </p>
<p>Fighting (2 if a character does not have Fighting), </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--17</p>
<p>plus any bonuses for shields or certain weapons. </p>
<p>This is the Target Number (TN) to hit your hero in </p>
<p>hand-to-hand combat.</p>
<p>For  stats  such  as  d12+1,  add  half  the  fixed </p>
<p>modifier, rounded down. For instance, a Fighting </p>
<p>skill of d12+1 grants a Parry of 8 (2+half of d12), </p>
<p>whereas a d12+2 gives a Parry of 9 (2+half of </p>
<p>d12+2).</p>
<p><b>Toughness</b> is your hero’s damage threshold. </p>
<p>Anything  over  this  causes  him  to  be  rattled  or </p>
<p>worse. Toughness is 2 plus half your hero’s Vigor, </p>
<p>plus Armor  (use  the  armor  worn  on  his  torso). </p>
<p>Vigor over a d12 is calculated just like Parry.</p>
<p>3..Edges.&amp;.Hindrances</p>
<p>Great heroes are far more than a collection of </p>
<p>skills and attributes. It’s their unique gifts, special </p>
<p>powers,  and  tragic  flaws  that  truly  make  them </p>
<p>interesting characters.</p>
<p>Characters can take Edges by balancing them </p>
<p>out with Hindrances. You’ll find a complete list of </p>
<p>Edges and Hindrances later in this chapter. Look </p>
<p>for more in our Savage Settings.</p>
<p>You  can  take  one  Major  Hindrance  and  two </p>
<p>Minor Hindrances. A Major Hindrance is worth 2 </p>
<p>points, and a Minor Hindrance is worth 1 point.</p>
<p><b>For 2 points you can:</b></p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Raise an attribute one die type, or</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Choose an Edge</p>
<p><b>For 1 point you can:</b></p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Gain another skill point, or</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Gain additional money equal to your starting </p>
<p>funds  (if  you  start  with  $500,  you  gain  an </p>
<p>additional $500)</p>
<p>4..Gear</p>
<p>Next you need to purchase equipment. Some </p>
<p>settings may provide your hero with all the gear he </p>
<p>needs. In others, you may be assigned a certain </p>
<p>amount  of  money  with  which  to purchase your </p>
<p>starting gear. A list of some common gear and </p>
<p>weapons can be found in Chapter Two.</p>
<p>Unless your setting book or GM says otherwise, </p>
<p>the standard starting amount is $500.</p>
<p>5..Background.Details</p>
<p>Finish your character by filling in any history or </p>
<p>background you care to. Ask yourself why your </p>
<p>hero is where she is and what her goals are. Or </p>
<p>you can just start playing and fill in these details </p>
<p>as they become important.</p>
<p>You might also want to talk to the other players. </p>
<p>Maybe  your  characters  know  each  other  right </p>
<p>from the start. Or you might collectively decide to </p>
<p>optimize your group a bit and ensure you’ve got a </p>
<p>good assortment of skills and abilities. If so, make </p>
<p>sure you’re playing what you want to play. There’s </p>
<p>no point in being the party’s magical healer, for </p>
<p>example, if that’s not a role you’re interested in.</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.Roleplaying</p>
<p><i>Most of us tend to play a particular character </i></p>
<p><i>type and stick with it regardless of genre or </i></p>
<p><i>game  system.  There’s  certainly  nothing </i></p>
<p><i>wrong with that, and you should absolutely </i></p>
<p><i>play what you enjoy most of the time.</i></p>
<p><i>It’s  also  great  to  occasionally  break  out </i></p>
<p><i>of  your  usual  mold  and  try  something </i></p>
<p><i>different.  Challenge  yourself  to  play  a </i></p>
<p><i>different character type now and then. Maybe </i></p>
<p><i>you always play big tough guys and like being </i></p>
<p><i>the “brick” of the party. Next time your friend </i></p>
<p><i>runs  a  swords  &amp;  sorcery  game,  try  being </i></p>
<p><i>the  mage. You  may  find  you  love  being  a </i></p>
<p><i>spellcaster.</i></p>
<p><i>Game  Masters  are  also  encouraged  to </i></p>
<p><i>try new things now and then. Some of the </i></p>
<p><i>most  incredible  sessions  we’ve  played  in </i></p>
<p><i>have featured very strange character types, </i></p>
<p><i>such as kobold slaves, the elderly, and even </i></p>
<p><i>intelligent  animals. And  nothing  beats  the </i></p>
<p><i>bizarre and amazing world of Andy Hopp’s </i></p>
<p>Low Life.</p>
<p><i>We  encourage </i></p>
<p><i>y o u  </i></p>
<p><i>t o  </i></p>
<p><i>t r y </i></p>
<p><i>something  new </i></p>
<p><i>once in  a while. </i></p>
<p><i>You can always </i></p>
<p><i>slip  right  back </i></p>
<p><i>into  that  very </i></p>
<p><i>comfortable </i></p>
<p><i>and  familiar </i></p>
<p><i>r o l e  </i></p>
<p><i>y o u </i></p>
<p><i>already know </i></p>
<p><i>y o u  </i></p>
<p><i>e n j o y </i></p>
<p><i>next time out.</i></p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--18</p>
<p>Archetypes</p>
<p>Sometimes you may want to quickly create a </p>
<p>character for a one-shot or convention adventure. </p>
<p>For  those  occasions,  an Archetype  might  be  a </p>
<p>good choice. Add a name, Hindrances, and gear, </p>
<p>spend any remaining skill points, and you’re ready </p>
<p>to play!</p>
<p>The Archetypes below are all Novice humans </p>
<p>with one free Edge.</p>

<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>

<p>Character.Creation</p>
<p>Martial.Artist</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d8,  Smarts  d6,  Spirit  d6, </p>
<p>Strength d8, Vigor d6</p>
<p><b>Skills:</b> Fighting d10, Intimidation d6, Notice d6, </p>
<p>+6 additional skill points</p>
<p><b>Charisma:</b> –; <b>Pace:</b> 6; <b>Parry: </b>7; <b>Toughness:</b> 5</p>
<p><b>Hindrances:</b> One Major, two Minor</p>
<p><b>Edges:</b> Martial Artist</p>
<p>Pilot</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d8,  Smarts  d6,  Spirit  d6, </p>

<p>Pistoleer</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d8,  Smarts  d6,  Spirit  d6, </p>

<p>d6, +4 additional skill points</p>
<p><b>Charisma:</b> –; <b>Pace:</b> 6; <b>Parry: </b>5; <b>Toughness:</b> 5</p>
<p><b>Hindrances:</b> One Major, two Minor</p>
<p><b>Edges:</b> Ambidextrous, Two-Fisted</p>
<p>Priest,.Healer</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d6,  Smarts  d6,  Spirit  d8, </p>
<p>Strength d6, Vigor d6</p>
<p><b>Skills:</b> Faith d8, Fighting d6, Healing d8, Notice </p>

<p><b>Hindrances:</b> One Major, two Minor</p>
<p><b>Edges:</b> Arcane Background (Miracles), Healer</p>
<p>Rogue</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d8,  Smarts  d6,  Spirit  d6, </p>
<p>Strength d6, Vigor d6</p>
<p><b>Skills:</b> Climbing d6, Fighting d6, Lockpicking d6, </p>
<p>Notice d6, Stealth d8, Streetwise d6, Taunt d6</p>
<p><b>Charisma:</b> –; <b>Pace:</b> 6; <b>Parry: </b>5; <b>Toughness:</b> 5</p>
<p><b>Hindrances:</b> One Major, two Minor</p>
<p><b>Edges:</b> Assassin, Thief</p>
<p>Scientist</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d4,  Smarts  d10,  Spirit  d6, </p>
<p>Strength d4, Vigor d6</p>
<p><b>Skills:</b>  Investigation  d6,  Knowledge  (Science) </p>
<p>d10, Knowledge (Other) d10, Notice d8, Repair d6</p>
<p><b>Charisma:</b> –; <b>Pace:</b> 6; <b>Parry: </b>2; <b>Toughness:</b> 5</p>
<p><b>Hindrances:</b> One Major, two Minor</p>
<p><b>Edges:</b> Jack-of-All-Trades, McGyver, Scholar</p>
<p>Scientist,.Weird</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d6,  Smarts  d8,  Spirit  d6, </p>
<p>Strength d4, Vigor d6</p>
<p><b>Skills:</b>  Knowledge  (Science)  d8,  Knowledge </p>
<p>(Other) d6, Notice d6, Repair d8, Shooting d6, </p>
<p>Weird Science d10</p>
<p><b>Charisma:</b> –; <b>Pace:</b> 6; <b>Parry: </b>2; <b>Toughness:</b> 5</p>
<p><b>Hindrances:</b> One Major, two Minor</p>
<p><b>Edges:</b> Arcane  Background  (Weird  Science), </p>
<p>Gadgeteer</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.Archetypes</p>
<p><i>Making  characters  in  Savage  Worlds  is </i></p>
<p><i>easy, but it still takes a while for everyone </i></p>
<p><i>to figure out what they want, make it, and </i></p>
<p><i>look through the rulebook and the particular </i></p>
<p><i>setting book to get it done.</i></p>
<p><i>These Archetypes let you jump right in with </i></p>
<p><i>little effort. They’re also a great tool to use with </i></p>
<p><i>younger or more inexperienced roleplayers </i></p>
<p><i>who  may  be  more  interested  in </i></p>
<p><i>hopping  in  and  playing </i></p>
<p><i>than making characters. </i></p>
<p><i>Use the Archetypes as a </i></p>
<p><i>base, then let the player </i></p>
<p><i>fill in the more creative </i></p>
<p><i>blanks—such  as </i></p>
<p><i>their hero’s name, </i></p>
<p><i>where  he  comes </i></p>
<p><i>from,  and  what’s </i></p>
<p><i>special  about </i></p>
<p><i>him.</i></p>
<p><i>  If  you’re  the </i></p>
<p><i>Game  Master </i></p>
<p><i>and you want </i></p>
<p><i>to  introduce </i></p>
<p><i>new  players </i></p>
<p><i>to  the  game,  use </i></p>
<p><i>these  to  quickly </i></p>
<p><i>fill  out  character </i></p>
<p><i>sheets,  then  add </i></p>
<p><i>some background </i></p>
<p><i>and  appropriate </i></p>
<p><i>H indra n c es  </i></p>
<p><i>to </i></p>
<p><i>make  them  shine </i></p>
<p><i>and you’re all set.</i></p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--20</p>
<p>Races</p>
<p>Not every hero is human. Below are sample races </p>
<p>common to many science fiction and fantasy settings. </p>
<p>Use  them  as  they  are  or  modify  them  to  fit  your </p>
<p>particular  world.  Immediately  after  you’ll  also  find </p>
<p>guidelines on how to create your own races.</p>
<p>Android</p>
<p>Androids are sentient machines with a variety of </p>
<p>appearances depending on the setting. Some appear </p>
<p>almost  human,  some  are  purely  mechanical. The </p>
<p>android presented here is a basic version with normal </p>
<p>human knowledge and emotions. Particular settings </p>
<p>may alter, remove, or add other abilities based on </p>
<p>their role and function in that world.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Asimov  Circuits:</b>  The  android  cannot  harm,  or  by </p>
<p>inaction bring harm to sentient beings. This gives him </p>
<p>the Pacifist Hindrance (Major).</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Construct:</b> Androids  add  +2  to  recover  from  being </p>
<p>Shaken, don’t suffer wound modifiers, and are immune </p>
<p>to poison and disease. Androids cannot heal naturally. </p>
<p>To heal an android requires the Repair skill—which is </p>
<p>used like the Healing skill only with no “Golden Hour.”</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Outsider:</b> Organic races often mistrust or misunderstand </p>
<p>androids. They  subtract  2  from  their  Charisma  when </p>
<p>dealing with races other than their own.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Programming:</b> Androids begin with a free d6 in one skill, </p>
<p>representing their original programmed role. </p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recharge:</b> During character creation, the player must </p>
<p>determine the  android’s  power source. If the android </p>
<p>cannot  access  his  power  source  at  least  once  per </p>
<p>day,  he’s  automatically  Fatigued  each  day  until  he’s </p>
<p>Incapacitated. The day after that, he goes “off-line” and </p>
<p>must be revived with a Repair roll and a four-hour charge </p>
<p>of energy. The power source replaces the need for food </p>
<p>and water, unless they are the chosen power source.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Unnatural:</b> Arcane  powers,  both  detrimental  and </p>
<p>beneficial, suffer a –2 penalty to affect androids. This </p>
<p>has no effect on damaging powers, which affect them </p>
<p>normally.</p>
<p>Atlantean</p>
<p>From  the  crushing  depths  come  the  mysterious </p>
<p>folk known as Atlanteans. They are thick and sturdy </p>
<p>beneath the waves but often vulnerable in the dry air </p>
<p>or searing heat. Their civilization is advanced, and </p>
<p>Atlantean science is a wonder to behold.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Advanced Civilization:</b> Atlanteans are generally more </p>
<p>intelligent than the other races of their world. They start </p>
<p>with a d6 in Smarts rather than a d4.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Aquatic:</b> Atlanteans  live  in  and  breathe  water. They </p>
<p>cannot drown in water, move at full Swimming skill, and </p>
<p>get a free d6 Swimming.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Dehydration:</b> Atlanteans must immerse themselves in </p>
<p>water one hour out of every 24 or become automatically </p>
<p>Fatigued each day until they are Incapacitated. The day </p>
<p>after that, they perish.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Tough:</b>  The  pressure  of  their  deep  homes  make </p>
<p>Atlanteans tougher than most. Increase Toughness by 1.</p>
<p>Avion</p>
<p>Avions are any basically human race with wings. They </p>
<p>tend to be very slight of build owing to their hollow bones.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Flight:</b> Avions can fly at their basic Pace and even “run” </p>
<p>while flying. It costs 2” of Pace to gain 1” of height.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Hollow-boned:</b> Avions have –1 Toughness.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Mostly Human:</b> Avions may choose one free Edge at </p>
<p>character creation as long as they meet all requirements.</p>
<p>Dwarves</p>
<p>Dwarves are short, stout, hardy people who come </p>
<p>from massive caverns in the high mountains. They are </p>
<p>a proud, warlike race, usually made so by frequent </p>
<p>contact with savage races such as orcs and goblins.</p>
<p>Dwarves usually live upwards of 200 years. In most </p>
<p>fantasy  campaigns,  they  have  ruddy  skin  and  all </p>
<p>human hair colors.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Low Light Vision:</b> Dwarven eyes are accustomed to the </p>
<p>dark of the underearth. They ignore attack penalties for </p>
<p>Dim and Dark lighting.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Slow:</b> Dwarves have a Pace of 5”.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Tough:</b> Dwarves are stout and tough. They start with a </p>
<p>d6 Vigor instead of a d4.</p>
<p>Elves</p>
<p>Elves are tall, thin souls with pointed ears and deep-</p>
<p>set eyes of various colors. Whether they hail from </p>
<p>the forests or hidden valleys, they are all born more </p>
<p>graceful  than  humans,  though  somewhat  slighter. </p>
<p>Most elves live upwards of 300 years. They have fair </p>
<p>skin and  their  hair includes  all human colors,  plus </p>
<p>shades of silver and blue.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Agile:</b> Elves are graceful and agile. They start with a d6 </p>
<p>in Agility instead of a d4.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>All Thumbs:</b> Elves have an inbred dislike of mechanical </p>
<p>objects, and thus have the All Thumbs Hindrance. They </p>
<p>shun most mechanical items and designs.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Low Light Vision:</b> Elven eyes amplify light like a cat’s, </p>
<p>allowing them to see in the dark. Elves ignores attack </p>
<p>penalties for Dim and Dark lighting.</p>
<p>Half-Elves</p>
<p>Half-elves  are  usually  a  solid  mix  of  their  two </p>
<p>parents. They gain the elves’ grace but none of their </p>
<p>elegant  frailty.  Most  half-elves  are  well-adjusted, </p>
<p>but some are shunned by one side of the family or </p>
<p>the other and grow resentful. Others may even be </p>
<p>mistreated. Their lifespans are closer to their human </p>
<p>parent than those of their elven kin. Most half-elves </p>
<p>live only to about 100 years.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Heritage:</b>  Some  half-elves  retain  the  grace  of  their </p>
<p>elven parent. Others gain the adaptability of their human </p>
<p>ancestry. A half-elf may either start with a free Edge of </p>
<p>his choice (as a human), or a d6 in Agility instead of a d4.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Low Light Vision:</b> The character’s eyes amplify light </p>
<p>like a cat’s, allowing him to see in the dark. He ignores </p>
<p>attack penalties for Dim and Dark lighting.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Outsider:</b> Half-elves aren’t true outsiders (as per the </p>
<p>Hindrance of the same name), but neither are they ever </p>
<p>quite comfortable around humans or elves as one of their </p>
<p>own, so the effect is the same.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--21</p>
<p>Character.Creation</p>
<p>Half-Folk</p>
<p>Half-folk are small, nimble creatures with fuzzy brown or </p>
<p>black hair. Though they are frail compared to most other </p>
<p>races, their cheerful optimism (or wily cunning) gives them a </p>
<p>“never say die” attitude that makes them more than a match </p>
<p>for creatures twice their size.</p>
<p>Half-folk see no reason to invite trouble, and tend to live in </p>
<p>their own little communities far off the beaten path.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Fortunate:</b>  Half-folk  draw  one  additional  Benny  per </p>
<p>game session. This may be combined with the Luck and </p>
<p>Great Luck Edges.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Short:</b> Half-folk average only about 4’ tall. This gives </p>
<p>them a Size of –1 and subtracts 1 from their Toughness. </p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Spirited:</b> Half-folk are generally optimistic beings. They </p>
<p>start with a d6 Spirit instead of a d4.</p>
<p>Half-Orcs</p>
<p>Half-orcs are the offspring of either a human and </p>
<p>an orc or an orc and another half-orc. Rarely is such </p>
<p>a mating willingly accepted, so the character’s </p>
<p>“family  tree”  is  likely  more  than  a  little </p>
<p>troublesome to him or her.</p>
<p>Half-orcs are usually accepted by </p>
<p>orcish communities, but are shunned </p>
<p>by  most  other  races,  including </p>
<p>humans,  elves,  and  dwarves.  Some </p>
<p>half-orcs  choose  to  join  the  “civilized” </p>
<p>races, turning their backs on their barbaric </p>
<p>roots,  and  are  often  looking  to  redeem </p>
<p>themselves. Many are heroic souls trying </p>
<p>to prove their worth.</p>
<p>Half-orcs  have  light-colored  human </p>
<p>skin  with  just  a  tinge  of  orcish </p>
<p>coloration,  with  black  hair  and </p>
<p>small  eyes. Their  features  are </p>
<p>harsh  and  angular,  like  that  of </p>
<p>orcs. Their natural life-span is the </p>
<p>same  as  humans,  though  it  is  rare </p>
<p>when one dies of old age.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Infravision:</b>  Half-orcs  can  see  in </p>
<p>the  infrared  spectrum,  halving  attack </p>
<p>penalties (round down) for bad lighting.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Outsider:</b> Half-orcs aren’t trusted by most </p>
<p>other  civilized  races,  and  so  subtract  2 </p>
<p>from their Charisma.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Strong:</b> Half-orcs have some of the strength </p>
<p>of their ancestry. They start with a d6 </p>
<p>Strength attribute instead of a d4.</p>
<p>Humans</p>
<p>Humans  in  most  settings  should  get </p>
<p>the usual benefit — one free Edge of their choice. </p>
<p>This option reflects their versatility and adaptability </p>
<p>compared to other races.</p>
<p>If you like more variety, you might also give humans </p>
<p>abilities  based  on  culture  rather  than  race.  For </p>
<p>instance, a seafaring human culture may start with </p>
<p>Boating and Swimming at d6. Cultural templates are </p>
<p>designed just like making new races (see page 22).</p>
<p>Rakashans</p>
<p>Rakashans  have  the  form  of  humans  with  the </p>
<p>features  of  felines. They  come  in  a  wide  variety: </p>
<p>the  bright  colors  of  tigers,  the  speckled  hides  of </p>
<p>leopards, and the exotic look of Siamese cats are all </p>
<p>appropriate. They have sharp claws and teeth and a </p>
<p>cruel nature when it comes to dealing with their prey.</p>
<p>Rakashans can be found in their own remote and </p>
<p>exotic cities or as fringe elements of normal society. </p>
<p>While they are too beautiful to be shunned, they are </p>
<p>too foreign to be easily accepted.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Agile:</b>  Rakashans  have  the  feline  grace  of  their </p>
<p>ancestors. They start with a d6 Agility attribute instead </p>
<p>of a d4.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Bloodthirsty:</b>  Rakashans  can  be  cruel  to  their  foes, </p>
<p>often  toying  with  them  for  simple  amusement. They </p>
<p>rarely take prisoners and feel little compunction about </p>
<p>punishing captured foes. This causes a –4 Charisma </p>
<p>penalty among more “civilized” types.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Racial  Enemy:</b>  Rakashan  society  rose  at  the </p>
<p>expense of another. Pick a common race in your setting. </p>
<p>Members of each culture suffer a –4 Charisma when </p>
<p>dealing with each other. Unless fettered by other </p>
<p>authorities or common goals, individuals of the two </p>
<p>races typically attack each other on sight.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Claws:</b>  Rakashans  have  retractable </p>
<p>claws  that  do  Str+d6  damage  and  grant  +2 </p>
<p>to Climbing rolls on all but completely sheer </p>
<p>surfaces.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Low  Light  Vision:</b>  Rakashan  eyes </p>
<p>amplify light. They can see in the dark </p>
<p>and ignore attack penalties for Dim and </p>
<p>Dark lighting.</p>
<p>Saurians</p>
<p>Lizard  men  typically  come  from </p>
<p>steaming  jungles  or  deep </p>
<p>deserts where they have unique </p>
<p>civilizations  unknown  to  other </p>
<p>sentient races.</p>
<p>Few outsiders have penetrated </p>
<p>their  society,  and  persistent </p>
<p>rumors  that  Saurian  religion </p>
<p>requires  sentient  sacrifices  remain </p>
<p>unconfirmed.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Outsider:</b>  Most  races  distrust  the </p>
<p>unblinking saurians. Their habit of eating </p>
<p>their meat still squirming is also less than </p>
<p>appetizing.  They  suffer  a  –2  Charisma </p>
<p>penalty.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Natural  Weapons:</b> The  tails,  claws, </p>
<p>and teeth of saurians allow them to tail slap, claw, </p>
<p>or bite in combat for Str+d4 damage.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Saurian  Senses:</b>  Saurians’  lizard </p>
<p>tongues can “taste” the air, giving them +2 to Notice </p>
<p>rolls. They are always considered active guards for </p>
<p>Stealth checks.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Warm Natured:</b> Though not truly cold-blooded, </p>
<p>saurians are not comfortable in cold environments. </p>
<p>They suffer a –4 penalty to resist cold environmental </p>
<p>effects.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--22</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Making.Races</p>
<p>The following system allows you to make new races </p>
<p>or cultural templates like those found on the previous </p>
<p>pages. The Game Master can use this to create pre-</p>
<p>built races for his world, or allow players to do so if </p>
<p>the setting allows a great number of racial variations.</p>
<p>If you’re the Game Master, decide on the maximum </p>
<p>number  of  Racial  Edge  points  allowed  (usually </p>
<p>between 2 and 4). This provides enough scope to </p>
<p>make an interesting race or culture without unduly </p>
<p>unbalancing the game.</p>
<p>Races and cultures begin with a free +2 Racial </p>
<p>Ability. This is equivalent to a human’s Free Edge. </p>
<p>Additional positive abilities must be countered with </p>
<p>an  equal  value  of  negative  ones. A  +2  ability,  for </p>
<p>example, may be countered by a single –2 ability or </p>
<p>two –1 abilities. </p>
<p>Give each ability a suitable name. If you want your </p>
<p>horse nomads to have Riding skill at d6, call it Born to </p>
<p>the Saddle. It’s all about creating a rich flavor rather </p>
<p>than just giving long lists of abilities your players have </p>
<p>to keep up with. Short and flavorful is far preferable </p>
<p>to long and comprehensive.</p>
<p>If you want to include an ability we haven’t listed </p>
<p>here, simply assign it a value based on the examples </p>
<p>here.</p>
<p>+3.Abilities</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Begin with a d8 in one attribute and may raise it to a </p>
<p>d12+2 via normal advancement; the Expert and Master </p>
<p>Edges may raise it to a d12+4</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>F r e e  </p>
<p>S e a s o n e d  </p>
<p>E d g e  </p>
<p>( r e g a r d l e s s  </p>
<p>o f </p>
<p>requirements — except those which require other Edges)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Hardy (a second Shaken result in combat does not </p>
<p>cause a Wound)</p>
<p>+2.Abilities</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+1 Parry</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+1 Size</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+1 Toughness</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+2 Armor (negated by AP weapons)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+2 Charisma</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+4 bonus to resist all negative environmental effects </p>
<p>(heat, cold, pressure, etc.)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+10 Power Points for one specific Arcane Background </p>
<p>(Magic or Miracles)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Aquatic  (cannot  drown  in  water,  moves  at  full </p>
<p>Swimming skill, free d6 Swimming)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Base Pace 10</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Construct (see page 152)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Free Novice Edge (regardless of requirements  —  except </p>
<p>for those that require other Edges)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Multiple  limbs  (one  extra  non-movement  action </p>
<p>per  limb,  incurs  no  multi-action  penalty,  price  is  per </p>
<p>additional limb)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Poison (victims that suffer a Shaken result from your </p>
<p>natural weapons must make a Vigor roll or be paralyzed </p>
<p>for 2d6 rounds)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Start with a d6 in one attribute</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>The Flight ability (at base Pace; may “run”); see the </p>
<p>monstrous ability on page 153</p>
<p>+1.Abilities</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+1 Reach</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+2 bonus to resist all negative environmental effects </p>
<p>(heat, cold, pressure, etc.)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+4 bonus to resist any single negative environmental </p>
<p>effect (e.g., heat or cold)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>+5  Power  Points  for  use  with  a  specific Arcane </p>
<p>Background (cannot be same AB as +2 Ability described </p>
<p>above)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Burrowing, Wall Walker, or similar</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Free d6 in a skill common to that race</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Immune to poison or disease</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Keen Sense (+2 to Notice when using one sense)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Low light or infravision; see monstrous abilities on </p>
<p>page 154</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Natural Weapons such as claws that cause Str+d6 </p>
<p>damage</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Potent Poison (must have Poison, each level gives </p>
<p>victims a –1 penalty to their Vigor roll)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Semi-aquatic (gain Fatigue level every 15 minutes he </p>
<p>holds his breath; on reaching Incapacitated, must make </p>
<p>Vigor roll every minute or drown; Fatigue recovers one </p>
<p>level per 15 minutes back in air)</p>
<p>–3.Abilities</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>One attribute can never advance beyond a d6</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>One attribute requires two points per step to raise </p>
<p>during  character  generation  and  the  character  must </p>
<p>dedicate two Advances to raising the attribute during </p>
<p>game play</p>
<p>–2.Abilities</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>–1 Parry</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>–1 Toughness</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>–4 penalty to resist all negative environmental effects </p>
<p>(heat, cold, pressure, etc.)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>One attribute requires two points per step to raise </p>
<p>during character generation</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Dehydration  (the  creature  must  immerse  itself  in </p>
<p>water one hour out of every 24 or become automatically </p>
<p>Fatigued each day until they are Incapacitated; the day </p>
<p>after that, they perish)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Major Hindrance (or equivalent effect)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Pace 3 or less (d4 running die)</p>
<p>–1.Abilities</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>–2 Charisma</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>–4 penalty to resist any single negative environmental </p>
<p>effect (e.g., heat or cold)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Minor Hindrance (or equivalent effect)</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Pace 5</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p>Racial Enemy (–4 Charisma when dealing with one </p>
<p>other race)</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--23</p>
<p>Skills</p>
<p>On the following pages are skills available in </p>
<p>most  Savage  Settings.  Normal  use  of  a  skill—</p>
<p>guiding a boat in and out of a dock, repairing an </p>
<p>engine with plenty of time and the proper tools, or </p>
<p>riding a horse across a prairie—shouldn’t require a </p>
<p>skill roll. Only when a character is under pressure </p>
<p>to  perform  a  task  quickly,  or  has  a  significant </p>
<p>chance of failure and can’t just keep trying until </p>
<p>he’s successful, should his player be asked to roll.</p>
<p>Familiarization</p>
<p>Skills  are  very  broad  for  ease  of  play,  but </p>
<p>sometimes  it’s  dramatically  appropriate  to </p>
<p>emphasize when a character is out of his element.</p>
<p>When  a  hero  finds  himself  using  a  skill  in  a </p>
<p>dramatically different way than he’s used to, he </p>
<p>suffers a  –2  penalty  to  his  rolls. How  long this </p>
<p>lasts depends on the skill, but should typically be </p>
<p>at least a few days of casual use, or a few hours </p>
<p>of more intensive use.</p>
<p>What  a  character  is  familiar  with  should  be </p>
<p>based  on  his  background  and  any  character </p>
<p>history the player has written up, with the Game </p>
<p>Master having the final say.</p>
<p>If you want more in-depth rules, see the Skill </p>
<p>Specialization Setting Rules on page 110.</p>
<p>Common.Knowledge</p>
<p>Instead of asking characters to have dozens of </p>
<p>background skills they rarely need in actual play, </p>
<p>we use the concept of “Common Knowledge.” Your </p>
<p>hero knows the basic history of his land, common </p>
<p>etiquette, how to get around geographically, how </p>
<p>to  operate  common  machinery  or  equipment </p>
<p>native to his time period and location, and who </p>
<p>the major players in his locality are. A Common </p>
<p>Knowledge roll is made by rolling the adventurer’s </p>
<p>Smarts attribute.</p>
<p>If a character’s background suggests he should </p>
<p>know  something  about  a  subject  and  he  must </p>
<p>make a roll, add +2 if most in his area or profession </p>
<p>would know the answer. If the subject is foreign </p>
<p>to a character, subtract 2 or more from the roll. </p>
<p>Everyone else breaks even and gets no modifier.</p>
<p>If  it  becomes  important  to  know  how  well  a </p>
<p>character performs a common task, the GM can </p>
<p>ask  for  whatever  roll  is  appropriate.  Knowing </p>
<p>how to do a dance, for example, is a Common </p>
<p>Knowledge  roll.  If  it  becomes  important  to  see </p>
<p>how  well  a  character  performs  the  dance,  the </p>
<p>GM might ask for an Agility roll (with no bonuses </p>
<p>or penalties).</p>
<p>For example, an adventure might read: “Anyone </p>
<p>who makes a Common Knowledge roll detects </p>
<p>that this cavern was carved by civilized hands, </p>
<p>not formed naturally.” A dwarf knows more about </p>
<p>stonework than an elf, so give the dwarf a +2 on </p>
<p>the roll. A human has about average knowledge, </p>
<p>so  no  bonus  is  granted. An  elf  who  has  spent </p>
<p>most of his life in a forest won’t know much about </p>
<p>stonework, so the roll is at a –2 penalty.</p>
<p>Specific.Knowledge</p>
<p>The  Knowledge  skill  represents  deeper </p>
<p>specialization in a chosen subject. A dwarf with </p>
<p>Knowledge  (Stonework),  for  example,  not  only </p>
<p>knows the dungeon was carved, but might just </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--24</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>know what race did it and the era in which it was </p>
<p>first excavated. He might also be able to locate </p>
<p>stress points that can cause a cave-in! Knowledge </p>
<p>of a particular region is also handy. In a fantasy </p>
<p>campaign,  for  example,  locals  might  know  the </p>
<p>Dread  Mountains  are  home  to  vicious  harpies. </p>
<p>A  hero  with  Knowledge  (Dread  Mountains) </p>
<p>might know a safe route or the specific peak the </p>
<p>creatures’ aerie is in.</p>
<p>Knowledge skills should be used for subjects </p>
<p>that have a significant impact during the game. If </p>
<p>the subject comes up  very rarely, use Common </p>
<p>Knowledge instead.</p>
<p>Skill.List</p>
<p>Boating.(Agility)</p>
<p>Characters with this skill can handle most any </p>
<p>boat or ship common to their setting and character </p>
<p>background. They generally know how to handle </p>
<p>common tasks associated with their vessels as </p>
<p>well (tying knots, rigging sails, etc.).</p>
<p>Climbing.(Strength)</p>
<p>Characters may sometimes have to climb tall </p>
<p>objects under duress, perhaps to scale a cliff to </p>
<p>attack  archers  stationed  above,  or  to  evade  a </p>
<p>terrifying creature on the ground below!</p>
<p>No  roll  is  usually  needed  to  ascend  ladders, </p>
<p>ropes, or trees with lots of limbs unless the GM feels </p>
<p>there’s a good reason (being chased, wounded, </p>
<p>etc.).  In  more  stressful  situations,  a  character </p>
<p>makes  a  Climbing  roll  and  checks  the  results </p>
<p>below.  Remember  that  these  measurements </p>
<p>are  listed  in  table-top  inches,  with  each  inch </p>
<p>representing two yards in the real world.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Fail: </b> The  character  makes  no  progress.  If  the </p>
<p>Climbing roll is a total of 1 or less, he falls to the </p>
<p>next level below—whatever that may be. See Falling </p>
<p>damage on page 101. If the hero was secured by a </p>
<p>rope or other restraint, he falls half the length of the </p>
<p>restraint and suffers a Fatigue level instead.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Success:</b> The hero ascends a number of vertical </p>
<p>inches on the table-top equal to half his Strength. A </p>
<p>hero with a d6 Strength, for example, can climb 3” </p>
<p>in a round if he makes his Climbing roll.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Raise:</b> As  Success,  above,  but  the  character </p>
<p>moves an additional 2”.</p>
<p>Climbing.Modifiers</p>
<p><b>  Modifier </b></p>
<p><b>Situation</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>Antique  or  medieval  climbing </p>
<p>equipment</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+4 </p>
<p>Modern climbing equipment</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Scarce or thin handholds</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Wet or slippery surface</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Falling Damage:</b> See page 101.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Prepared Climbs:</b> Break lengthy ascents into </p>
<p>three roughly even sections. Failing a Climbing roll </p>
<p>after a break point typically means the hero falls </p>
<p>that distance if free-climbing.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Ropes:</b> Those secured with a rope typically </p>
<p>only  suffer  a  level  of  Fatigue  from  Bumps  and </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--25</p>
<p>Character.Creation</p>
<p>Bruises  (see  page  99).  Of  course  the  Game </p>
<p>Master might decide the rope has a chance of </p>
<p>breaking under a sudden strain. This is rare in </p>
<p>reality, but for dramatic effect, roll a d6. On a 1, </p>
<p>the rope breaks and the character falls the entire </p>
<p>length of the last section climbed. </p>
<p>Driving.(Agility)</p>
<p>Driving allows your hero to control ground and </p>
<p>hover vehicles common to his setting. Rules for </p>
<p>handling vehicles on the table-top can be found </p>
<p>on page 113. Driving is also frequently used with </p>
<p>the Chase rules, described on page 94.</p>
<p>Fighting.(Agility)</p>
<p>Fighting  covers  all  hand-to-hand  (melee) </p>
<p>attacks, whether it’s with fists, axes, laser swords, </p>
<p>or martial arts. The TN to hit an opponent is his </p>
<p>Parry (2 plus half his Fighting). See Chapter Three </p>
<p>for the combat rules and numerous maneuvers </p>
<p>your warrior might attempt.</p>
<p>Gambling.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Gambling  is  useful  from  the  saloons  of  the </p>
<p>Old West to the barracks of most armies. Here’s </p>
<p>a  quick  way  to  simulate  about  a  half-hour  of </p>
<p>gambling without having to roll for every single </p>
<p>toss of the dice or hand of cards.</p>
<p>First have everyone agree on the stakes, such </p>
<p>as $10, 10 gold coins, etc. Now have everyone in </p>
<p>the game make a Gambling roll. The lowest total </p>
<p>pays  the  highest  total  the  difference  times  the </p>
<p>stake. The next lowest pays the second highest </p>
<p>the difference times the stake, and so on. If there’s </p>
<p>an odd man left in the middle, he breaks even.</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b> Kali rolls highest with a 10 and </i></p>
<p><i>Yuri rolls lowest with a 4. The difference is 6, </i></p>
<p><i>so Yuri pays Kali 6 x the stake of $10, or $60.</i></p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Cheating: </b>A character who cheats adds +2 to </p>
<p>his roll. The GM may raise or lower this modifier </p>
<p>depending on the particulars of the game or the </p>
<p>method of cheating. If the player ever rolls a 1 </p>
<p>on his skill die (regardless of his Wild Die), he’s </p>
<p>caught. The consequences of this depend on the </p>
<p>setting, but are usually quite harsh.</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.Guts</p>
<p><i>Where did the Guts skill go? We removed </i></p>
<p><i>it. It’s important to some settings (and you’ll </i></p>
<p><i>find it as a Setting Rule when it is), but rare </i></p>
<p><i>in others. Where it’s rare, however, even a </i></p>
<p><i>mighty barbarian has to spend valuable skill </i></p>
<p><i>points to prove his mettle with the Guts skill, </i></p>
<p><i>and that really doesn’t make much sense.</i></p>
<p><i>In  the  core  rules  and  most  settings </i></p>
<p><i>(especially  fantasy  and  sci-fi)  characters </i></p>
<p><i>now use Spirit to resist the effects of Fear. If </i></p>
<p><i>a character in a previously published setting </i></p>
<p><i>has Guts, ignore it. If you’re using a published </i></p>
<p><i>adventure that requires a Guts roll, use Spirit </i></p>
<p><i>instead. </i></p>
<p><i>In a few settings (primarily horrific ones, </i></p>
<p><i>like </i>Deadlands<i>), Guts is a Setting Rule that </i></p>
<p><i>reflects  the  particular  nature  of  the  game </i></p>
<p><i>world and remains intact.</i></p>
<p>Healing.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Healing  is  the  art  of  stopping  wounds  and </p>
<p>treating existing injuries. In general, every success </p>
<p>and raise on a Healing roll eliminates a wound. </p>
<p>The healer must subtract not only his own wounds </p>
<p>from the roll as usual, but those of his patient as </p>
<p>well.</p>
<p>See the Healing rules on page 87 for specific </p>
<p>information.</p>
<p>Intimidation.(Spirit)</p>
<p>Intimidation is the art of frightening an opponent </p>
<p>with sheer force of will, veiled or overt threats, </p>
<p>or  sometimes  just  really  big  guns.  This  is  an </p>
<p>opposed roll between the hero’s Intimidation and </p>
<p>his opponent’s Spirit. See Tests of Will on page </p>
<p>86 for game effects.</p>
<p>Investigation.(Smarts)</p>
<p>A character skilled in Investigation knows how to </p>
<p>make good use of libraries, newspaper morgues, </p>
<p>the internet, or other written sources of information. </p>
<p>To get information from people rather than books </p>
<p>and computers, use the Streetwise skill.</p>
<p>Knowledge.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Knowledge is a catch-all skill that must have a </p>
<p>focus of some sort, such as Knowledge (Occult) </p>
<p>or Knowledge (Science). The player can choose </p>
<p>the  focus  of  his  character’s  knowledge,  which </p>
<p>might  reflect  his  background  and  education. </p>
<p>The  skill  can  be  taken  multiple  times  with </p>
<p>different  focuses  to  reflect  different  areas  of </p>
<p>expertise. An archaeologist, for example, should </p>
<p>have  Knowledge  (History)  and  Knowledge </p>
<p>(Archaeology).</p>
<p>General focuses such as Knowledge (Science) </p>
<p>are acceptable, but the GM should give a bonus </p>
<p>to a character who has a focus more relevant to a </p>
<p>particular task, such as using Knowledge (Biology) </p>
<p>to identify a plant or animal.</p>
<p>Some Common Knowledge focuses are: Area </p>
<p>Knowledge,  Battle  (used  in  Mass  Battles,  see </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--26</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>page  106),  Computers,  Electronics,  History, </p>
<p>Journalism,  various  languages,  Law,  Medicine </p>
<p>(though actually caring for someone is the Healing </p>
<p>skill), or Science.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Common  Knowledge: </b> Characters  don’t </p>
<p>need a Knowledge skill to know something about </p>
<p>a  particular  field.  Basic  information  should  be </p>
<p>covered by Common Knowledge (see page 26) </p>
<p>assuming  it  makes  sense  within  a  character’s </p>
<p>background. A  mercenary  with  a  penchant  for </p>
<p>reading or a young history student, for example, </p>
<p>doesn’t  necessarily  need  Knowledge  (History). </p>
<p>If tasked with a historical question, however, the </p>
<p>two might roll normally while those without such </p>
<p>a background roll at a penalty.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Languages: </b>Knowledge can also be used to </p>
<p>reflect knowing a language other than one’s own. </p>
<p>The higher the level, the better the character can </p>
<p>speak  and  mimic  regional  dialects,  as  shown </p>
<p>below.</p>
<p>In  settings  where  inhabitants  typically  speak </p>
<p>many languages, see the Languages Setting Rule </p>
<p>on page 109.</p>
<p>Knowledge.(Language).Table</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>Skill </b></p>
<p><b>Ability</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>d4 </p>
<p>The  character  can  read,  write,  and </p>
<p>speak common words and phrases</p>
<p> </p>
<p>d6 </p>
<p>The speaker can carry on a prolonged </p>
<p>but occasionally halting conversation</p>
<p> </p>
<p>d8 </p>
<p>The character can speak fluently</p>
<p> </p>
<p>d10 </p>
<p>The  hero  can  mimic  other  dialects </p>
<p>within the language</p>
<p> </p>
<p>d12 </p>
<p>The  speaker  can  masterfully  recite </p>
<p>important literary or oral works</p>
<p>Lockpicking.(Agility)</p>
<p>Lockpicking is the ability to bypass mechanical </p>
<p>and electronic locks. Lockpicking is also used to </p>
<p>disarm the catches and triggers on traps, unless </p>
<p>a  more  relevant  skill  seems  appropriate  for  a </p>
<p>particular trap.</p>
<p>Notice.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Notice is a hero’s general alertness and ability to </p>
<p>search for items or clues. This covers hearing rolls, </p>
<p>detecting  ambushes,  spotting  hidden  weapons </p>
<p>and even scrutinizing other characters to see if </p>
<p>they’re  lying,  frightened,  and  so  on. The  more </p>
<p>raises a character gets on a Notice roll, the more </p>
<p>information the Game Master should reveal.</p>
<p>Persuasion.(Spirit)</p>
<p>Persuasion is the ability to convince others to </p>
<p>do what you want them to do.</p>
<p>Nonplayer characters start at one of five different </p>
<p>attitudes:  Hostile,  Uncooperative,  Neutral, </p>
<p>Friendly, or Helpful. A successful Persuasion roll </p>
<p>improves the Extra’s attitude one step, or two with </p>
<p>a raise. Failure, on the other hand, decreases the </p>
<p>character’s attitude by a step, or two if a 1 is rolled </p>
<p>on the Persuasion die (regardless of the Wild Die). </p>
<p>Most  Extras  won’t  change  their  reaction  more </p>
<p>than one or two levels during a single exchange, </p>
<p>but that’s entirely up to the Game Master and the </p>
<p>situation.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Charisma: </b>Persuasion is always modified by </p>
<p>a character’s Charisma. </p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Player Characters:</b> Persuasion should never </p>
<p>be used on other player characters. Their attitudes </p>
<p>should be decided entirely by their players.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Reaction Table: </b>If the Game Master doesn’t </p>
<p>already  have  an  initial  attitude  in  mind  for  the </p>
<p>Extra, he can roll on the chart below. </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--27</p>
<p>Character.Creation</p>
<p>Reaction.Table</p>
<p><b>  2d6 </b></p>
<p><b>Initial Reaction</b></p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>2 </b></p>
<p><b>Hostile:</b> The target is openly hostile and </p>
<p>does his best to stand in the hero’s way. </p>
<p>He won’t help without an overwhelming </p>
<p>reward or payment of some kind.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>3-4 </b></p>
<p><b>Uncooperative:</b> The  target  isn’t  willing </p>
<p>to  help  unless  there’s  a  significant </p>
<p>advantage to himself.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>5-9 </b></p>
<p><b>Neutral:</b>  The  target  has  no  particular </p>
<p>attitude  and  will  help  for  little  reward </p>
<p>if the task at hand is very easy. If the </p>
<p>task is difficult, he’ll require substantial </p>
<p>payment of some kind.</p>
<p><b> 10-11 </b></p>
<p><b>Friendly:</b> The target will go out of his way </p>
<p>for the hero. He’ll likely do easy tasks </p>
<p>for free (or very little), and is willing to </p>
<p>do more dangerous tasks for fair pay or </p>
<p>other favors.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>12 </b></p>
<p><b>Helpful:</b> The target is anxious to help the </p>
<p>hero, and will probably do so for little or </p>
<p>no pay depending on the nature of the </p>
<p>task.</p>
<p>Piloting.(Agility)</p>
<p>Piloting  allows  a  character  to  fly  airplanes, </p>
<p>helicopters, jet packs, and any other flying devices </p>
<p>common to his setting and background.</p>
<p>Repair.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Repair  is  the  ability  to  fix  gadgets,  vehicles, </p>
<p>weapons, and other machines. Characters suffer </p>
<p>a –2 penalty to their rolls if they don’t have access </p>
<p>to basic tools. A raise on a Repair roll halves the </p>
<p>time required by the specific task.</p>
<p>Riding.(Agility)</p>
<p>Riding allows a hero to mount, control, and ride </p>
<p>any beast common to his setting. Players should </p>
<p>note that mounted characters use the lowest of </p>
<p>their Fighting or Riding skills when fighting from </p>
<p>horseback. Additional  rules  for  fighting  while </p>
<p>mounted can be found on page 83.</p>
<p>Shooting.(Agility)</p>
<p>Shooting covers all attempts to hit a target with </p>
<p>a ranged weapon such as a bow, pistol, or rocket </p>
<p>launcher. The basic Target Number to hit is 4 as </p>
<p>usual, though  there  are a  number of important </p>
<p>modifiers such as range that frequently come into </p>
<p>play. See Chapter Three for more details.</p>
<p>Stealth.(Agility)</p>
<p>Stealth  is  the  ability  to  both  hide  and  move </p>
<p>quietly, as well as palm objects and pick pockets. </p>
<p>In many <i>Savage Worlds</i> games, knowing exactly </p>
<p>when your hero has been spotted and when he </p>
<p>hasn’t can be critical.</p>
<p>For  a  character  to  sneak  up  on  foes  and </p>
<p>infiltrate enemy lines, start by figuring out if the </p>
<p>“guards”  the  heroes  are  sneaking  up  on  are </p>
<p>“active” or “inactive.” <i>Inactive </i>guards aren’t paying </p>
<p>particularly close attention to their surroundings. </p>
<p>The group need only score a standard success on </p>
<p>their individual Stealth rolls to avoid being seen. </p>
<p>Failing a Stealth roll in the presence of inactive </p>
<p>guards makes them active.</p>
<p><i>Active</i>  guards  make  opposed  Notice  rolls </p>
<p>against  the  sneaking  characters’  Stealth  skills. </p>
<p>Failing  a  roll  against  active  guards  means  the </p>
<p>character is spotted.</p>
<p>Apply the following modifiers to all Stealth rolls:</p>
<p>Stealth.Modifiers</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>Situation </b></p>
<p><b>Modifier</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>Crawling </p>
<p>+2</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Running </p>
<p>–2</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Dim light </p>
<p>+1</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Darkness </p>
<p>+2</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Pitch darkness </p>
<p>+4</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Light cover </p>
<p>+1</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Medium cover </p>
<p>+2</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Heavy cover </p>
<p>+4</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>The  Last  Step: </b> Sneaking  to  within  6”  of  a </p>
<p>foe  (usually  to  get  close  enough  for  a  melee </p>
<p>attack) requires an opposed Stealth roll versus </p>
<p>the target’s Notice, whether the guard is active </p>
<p>or inactive.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Movement Rate:</b> Out of combat, each Stealth </p>
<p>roll covers moving up to five times the character’s </p>
<p>Pace. In combat, the Stealth roll covers only a </p>
<p>single round of movement.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Stealth for Groups:</b> Out of combat, make only </p>
<p>one Stealth roll for each like group of characters </p>
<p>(see Group Rolls on page 71). Use the lowest </p>
<p>movement rate to determine how much ground </p>
<p>is covered. The observers also make a group roll </p>
<p>to Notice their foes. Once a combat breaks down </p>
<p>into rounds, Stealth and Notice rolls are made on </p>
<p>an individual basis.</p>
<p>Streetwise.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Streetwise  characters  are  able  to  gather </p>
<p>information  from  the  street,  saloons,  or  other </p>
<p>contacts  through  bribes,  threats,  or  carousing. </p>
<p>Finding written information in libraries and the like </p>
<p>is covered by the Investigation skill. Streetwise </p>
<p>is  always  modified  by  a  character’s  Charisma </p>
<p>modifier.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--28</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Survival.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Survival allows a character to find food, water, </p>
<p>or  shelter  in  hostile  environments. A  character </p>
<p>may only make one roll per day. A successful roll </p>
<p>finds sustenance for one person, a raise on the </p>
<p>roll finds food and water for five adults. Horses and </p>
<p>other large beasts count as two adults. Children, </p>
<p>camels or others with small appetites count as </p>
<p>half. Those who benefit from the roll do not have </p>
<p>to make Fatigue rolls for the day for food, water, </p>
<p>or shelter.</p>
<p>Swimming.(Agility)</p>
<p>Swimming determines if a character floats or </p>
<p>sinks in water, as well as how fast he can move </p>
<p>within it. A character’s Pace is half his Swimming </p>
<p>skill in inches per turn in normal water (round up). </p>
<p>Choppy water counts as rough terrain and halves </p>
<p>this rate. Characters may not “run” while swimming </p>
<p>for extra movement.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Holding Your Breath:</b> Characters can hold </p>
<p>their breath for a number of rounds equal to 2 plus </p>
<p>their Vigor die, or half that if they weren’t prepared </p>
<p>for being submerged and didn’t have time to get </p>
<p>a good breath.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Drowning:</b> The rules for drowning are found </p>
<p>on page 100.</p>
<p>Taunt.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Taunt is a Test of Wills attack against a person’s </p>
<p>pride  through  ridicule,  cruel  jokes,  or  one-</p>
<p>upmanship. This is an opposed roll against the </p>
<p>target’s Smarts. See Tests of Will on page 86 for </p>
<p>the effects of a successful Taunt.</p>
<p>Throwing.(Agility)</p>
<p>Throwing governs all sorts of thrown weapons, </p>
<p>from hand grenades to knives, axes, and spears. </p>
<p>Throwing works just like the Shooting skill, and </p>
<p>uses all the same modifiers, including those for </p>
<p>Range. The Rate of Fire of a thrown attack is 1 per </p>
<p>hand, so a human character could throw two items </p>
<p>at once (one with each hand), suffering the usual </p>
<p>multi-action and off-hand penalties (see page 85).</p>
<p>Tracking.(Smarts)</p>
<p>Tracking allows a character to follow the tracks </p>
<p>of one or more individuals in any type of terrain. </p>
<p>Each roll generally covers following the tracks for </p>
<p>one mile, but the GM may adjust this dramatically </p>
<p>for more specific or small scale searches.</p>
<p>Tracking.Modifiers</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b> Modifier </b></p>
<p><b>Situation</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>Tracking more than 5 individuals</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>+4 </p>
<p>Recent snow</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>Mud</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>+1 </p>
<p>Dusty area</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>–4 </p>
<p>Raining</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Tracking in poor light</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Tracks are more than one day old</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Target attempted to hide tracks</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--29</p>
<p>Hindrances</p>
<p>Hindrances  are  character  flaws  and  physical </p>
<p>handicaps  that  occasionally  make  life  a  little </p>
<p>tougher  for  your  hero.  Some  Hindrances  are </p>
<p>more or less subjective (such as Overconfident). </p>
<p>They’re there to help you roleplay your character, </p>
<p>and might even net you more Bennies since the </p>
<p>Game Master awards them for properly playing </p>
<p>your character.</p>
<p>A  character  may  take  one  Major  Hindrance </p>
<p>and up to two Minor Hindrances. You’re free to </p>
<p>take  more  if  you  think  they  fit  your  character </p>
<p>description,  but  you  don’t  get  additional  points </p>
<p>for them.</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.Roleplaying.Hindrances</p>
<p><i>Some Hindrances impose game penalties </i></p>
<p><i>and  some  only  really  matter  if  the  player </i></p>
<p><i>roleplays  them.  That’s  intentional  and  not </i></p>
<p><i>something you should worry about too much. </i></p>
<p><i>The game is designed with the assumption </i></p>
<p><i>that  all  heroes  take  their  full  complement </i></p>
<p><i>of  Hindrances  and  therefore  have  two </i></p>
<p><i>additional attribute points, Edges, skills, or a </i></p>
<p><i>combination thereof.</i></p>
<p><i>It’s certainly true that a Hindrance like Big </i></p>
<p><i>Mouth  won’t  be  worth  much  if  the  player </i></p>
<p><i>doesn’t  occasionally  roleplay  it  by  blurting </i></p>
<p><i>things  out  at  inappropriate  times.  But  the </i></p>
<p><i>Game Master can pay a little attention here, </i></p>
<p><i>too.  For  example,  in  a  fantasy  campaign, </i></p>
<p><i>players  don’t  roleplay  every  minute  their </i></p>
<p><i>characters are in a tavern. But it’s easy to </i></p>
<p><i>assume  that  while  they’re  there—whether </i></p>
<p><i>it  was  acted  out  or  not—the  Big  Mouthed </i></p>
<p><i>hero let spill their plans to raid the ancient </i></p>
<p><i>tomb on the hill. Maybe the group will find </i></p>
<p><i>another team of adventurers are there before </i></p>
<p><i>them—or  worse,  waiting  to  see  what  they </i></p>
<p><i>found when they come out.</i></p>
<p><i>Finally, Hindrances are more about helping </i></p>
<p><i>a player figure out who his character is than </i></p>
<p><i>inflicting a gameplay penalty on him. Being </i></p>
<p><i>Loyal may never really be a problem—most </i></p>
<p><i>characters are just naturally loyal to others </i></p>
<p><i>in their party. But having it on the character </i></p>
<p><i>sheet reminds the player that he’s a “good </i></p>
<p><i>guy,” at least to his friends, and will help him </i></p>
<p><i>indirectly roleplay his character and make </i></p>
<p><i>decisions within that context he might have </i></p>
<p><i>made differently if he was Mean instead.</i></p>
<p>All.Thumbs.(Minor)</p>
<p>Some  people  just  aren’t  good  with  modern </p>
<p>devices. Characters with this drawback suffer a –2 </p>
<p>penalty to the Repair skill at all times. In addition, </p>
<p>when  a  hero  uses  a  mechanical  or  electronic </p>
<p>device, a roll of 1 on his skill die (regardless of </p>
<p>his Wild Die) means the device is broken. The </p>
<p>damage usually requires a Repair roll at –2 and </p>
<p>1d6 hours to fix.</p>
<p>Anemic.(Minor)</p>
<p>An anemic character is particularly susceptible </p>
<p>to sickness, disease, environmental effects, and </p>
<p>fatigue. He subtracts 2 from all Fatigue checks </p>
<p>such as those made to resist poison and disease. </p>
<p>(See page 99 for more information on Fatigue and </p>
<p>the various hazards that lead to it.)</p>
<p>Arrogant.(Major)</p>
<p>Your hero doesn’t think he’s the best—he knows </p>
<p>he is. Whatever it is—swordsmanship, kung fu, </p>
<p>running—few compare to his skills and he flaunts it </p>
<p>every chance he gets.</p>
<p>Winning just isn’t enough for your hero. He must </p>
<p>completely dominate his opponent. Anytime there </p>
<p>is even a shadow of a doubt as to who is better, </p>
<p>he  must  humiliate  his  opponent  and  prove  he </p>
<p>can snatch victory any time he wishes. He is the </p>
<p>kind of man who disarms an opponent in a duel </p>
<p>just so he can pick the sword up and hand it back </p>
<p>with a smirk.</p>
<p>Arrogant heroes always look for the “boss” in </p>
<p>battle, attacking lesser minions only if they get </p>
<p>in the way.</p>
<p>Bad.Eyes.(Minor.or.Major)</p>
<p>Your  hero’s  eyes  just  aren’t  what  they  used </p>
<p>to be. With glasses, there’s no penalty and the </p>
<p>Hindrance  is  only  Minor.  Should  he  lose  his </p>
<p>glasses  (generally  a  50%  chance  when  he’s </p>
<p>wounded,  or  no  chance  with  a  “nerd-strap”), </p>
<p>he  suffers  a  –2  penalty  to  any Trait  roll  made </p>
<p>to shoot or Notice something more than 5” (10 </p>
<p>yards) distant.</p>
<p>In low-tech settings where the hero cannot wear </p>
<p>glasses, Bad Eyes is a Major Hindrance. He must </p>
<p>subtract 2 from Trait rolls made to attack or notice </p>
<p>things 5” or more away.</p>
<p>Bad.Luck.(Major)</p>
<p>Your hero is a little less lucky than most. He gets </p>
<p>one less Benny per game session than normal. </p>
<p>A character cannot have both Bad Luck and the </p>
<p>Luck Edge.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--30</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Big.Mouth.(Minor)</p>
<p>Loose  lips  sink  ships,  the  saying  goes. Your </p>
<p>hero’s mouth could drown an armada.</p>
<p>Your character can’t keep a secret very well. </p>
<p>He reveals plans and gives away things best kept </p>
<p>among friends, usually at the worst possible times.</p>
<p>Blind.(Major)</p>
<p>The individual is completely without sight. He </p>
<p>suffers a –6 to all physical tasks that require vision </p>
<p>(which is most everything) and –2 to most social </p>
<p>tasks as he can’t “read” those he’s interacting with </p>
<p>as well as others.</p>
<p>On  the  plus  side,  Blind  characters  gain  their </p>
<p>choice  of  a  free  Edge  to  compensate  for  this </p>
<p>particularly difficult Hindrance.</p>
<p>Bloodthirsty.(Major)</p>
<p>Your hero never takes prisoners unless under </p>
<p>the direct supervision of a superior. This can cause </p>
<p>major problems in a military campaign unless his </p>
<p>superiors condone that sort of thing. Your killer </p>
<p>suffers –4 to his Charisma, but only if his cruel </p>
<p>habits are known.</p>
<p>Cautious.(Minor)</p>
<p>Some folks gather too much intelligence. This </p>
<p>character  personifies  over-cautiousness.  He </p>
<p>never  makes  rash  decisions  and  likes  to  plot </p>
<p>things out in detail long before any action is taken.</p>
<p>Clueless.(Major)</p>
<p>Your hero isn’t as aware of his world as most </p>
<p>others. He suffers –2 to Common Knowledge rolls.</p>
<p>Code.of.Honor.(Major)</p>
<p>Honor is very important to your character. He </p>
<p>keeps his word, won’t abuse or kill prisoners, and </p>
<p>generally tries to operate within his world’s particular </p>
<p>notion of proper gentlemanly or ladylike behavior.</p>
<p>Curious.(Major)</p>
<p>It killed the cat, and it might kill your hero as </p>
<p>well. Curious characters are easily dragged into </p>
<p>any adventure. They have to check out everything </p>
<p>and always want to know what’s behind a potential </p>
<p>mystery.</p>
<p>Death.Wish.(Minor)</p>
<p>Having  a  death  wish  doesn’t  mean  your </p>
<p>adventurer is suicidal—but he does want to die </p>
<p><i>after</i>  completing  some  important  goal.  Maybe </p>
<p>he wants revenge  for the murder  of  his family, </p>
<p>or maybe he’s dying from disease and wants to </p>
<p>go out in a blaze of glory. He won’t throw his life </p>
<p>away for no reason, but when there’s a chance </p>
<p>to complete his goal, he’ll do anything—and take </p>
<p>any risk—to achieve it.</p>
<p>This Hindrance is usually Minor unless the goal </p>
<p>is relatively easily fulfilled (very rare).</p>
<p>Delusional.(Minor.or.Major)</p>
<p>Your hero believes something that is considered </p>
<p>quite strange by everyone else. Minor Delusions </p>
<p>are harmless or the character generally keeps it </p>
<p>to himself (the government puts sedatives in soft </p>
<p>drinks, dogs can talk, we’re all just characters in </p>
<p>some bizarre game, etc.).</p>
<p>With a Major Delusion, he expresses his view </p>
<p>on the situation frequently and it can occasionally </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--31</p>
<p>Character.Creation</p>
<p>lead to danger (the government is run by aliens, </p>
<p>hospitals are deadly, I’m allergic to armor, zombies </p>
<p>are my friends).</p>
<p>Doubting.Thomas.(Minor)</p>
<p>Some people don’t believe in the supernatural </p>
<p>until they’re halfway down some creature’s gullet. </p>
<p>Doubting Thomases are skeptics who try their best </p>
<p>to rationalize supernatural events. Even once a </p>
<p>Doubting Thomas realizes the supernatural exists, </p>
<p>he still tries to rationalize weird events, following </p>
<p>red herrings or ignoring evidence.</p>
<p>Doubting  Thomases  suffer  –2  to  their  Fear </p>
<p>checks  when  confronted  with  undeniable </p>
<p>supernatural horror.</p>
<p>Elderly.(Major)</p>
<p>Your adventurer is getting on in years, but he’s </p>
<p>not quite ready for the nursing home. His Pace </p>
<p>is reduced by 1, and his Strength and Vigor drop </p>
<p>a die type to a minimum of d4, and cannot be </p>
<p>raised thereafter.</p>
<p>On the plus side, the wisdom of his years grants </p>
<p>the hero 5 extra skill points that may be used for </p>
<p>any skills linked to Smarts.</p>
<p>Enemy.(Minor.or.Major)</p>
<p>Someone  out  there  hates  the  character  and </p>
<p>wants  him  dead.  The  value  of  the  Hindrance </p>
<p>depends on how powerful the enemy is and how </p>
<p>often he might show up. A Minor Enemy might </p>
<p>be a lone gunslinger out for vengeance. A Major </p>
