<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--53</p>
<p>Character.Creation</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--54</p>
<p>Chapter.Two:.</p>
<p>Gear</p>
<p>In the following section is a sampling of gear </p>
<p>from  the  ancient  era  to  the  near  future.  Below </p>
<p>are  some  notes  you’ll  need  to  understand  the </p>
<p>equipment lists.</p>
<p><b>3RB: </b> The  weapon  has  a  three-round  burst </p>
<p>selector (see page 81).</p>
<p><b>AP  (Armor  Piercing): </b> The  weapon  or  round </p>
<p>ignores this many points of Armor. A weapon with </p>
<p>an AP value of 4, for instance, ignores 4 points of </p>
<p>Armor. Excess AP is simply lost.</p>
<p><b>Auto:</b>  This  full-auto  weapon  may  fire  in </p>
<p>automatic (single shot) mode.</p>
<p><b>Armor:</b> This is the amount of Armor provided </p>
<p>by the equipment, which is added to the wearer’s </p>
<p>Toughness  when  the  covered  location  is  hit  in </p>
<p>combat. A character who wears multiple layers </p>
<p>of armor only gains the highest bonus—they do </p>
<p>not  stack.  Note  that  unless  an  attacker  states </p>
<p>otherwise, hits are always directed at the victim’s </p>
<p>torso.</p>
<p><b>Caliber:</b> The number listed in parentheses after </p>
<p>firearms is the caliber of bullet it fires. Use this </p>
<p>when figuring ammunition costs or trying to figure </p>
<p>out if the ammo from one weapon fits in another.</p>
<p><b>Cost:</b>  Equipment  prices  are  relative  both  to </p>
<p>the starting funds of $500 <i>and</i> to their tech level, </p>
<p>so a Springfield musket doesn’t really cost $250 </p>
<p>in  1862. That’s  just  the  “worth”  of  the  weapon </p>
<p>relative to the tech level and the typical setting </p>
<p>it’s intended for. Remember that when comparing </p>
<p>the $150 musket to the $150 AK47—the weapons </p>
<p>are both “standard” for the typical environment </p>
<p>they’re  found  in,  even  though  the AK  is  vastly </p>
<p>superior  to  the  musket.  Characters  in  military </p>
<p>campaigns  shouldn’t  buy  equipment  at  all—</p>
<p>they’re simply assigned their gear. Some items </p>
<p>simply list “Military” as their cost, meaning they </p>
<p>are typically bought by large national militaries and </p>
<p>then supplied to the troops under their command. </p>
<p>Such weapons are not normally available on the </p>
<p>open market.</p>
<p>Economies  are  critical  to  balancing  game </p>
<p>worlds,  so  the  Game  Master  is  encouraged  to </p>
<p>re-price goods for his particular campaign.</p>
<p><b>Damage: </b> Damage  is  listed  in  terms  of  dice. </p>
<p>Projectile weapons have fixed damage (such as </p>
<p>2d6). Melee weapons have damage based on the </p>
<p>wielder’s Strength die plus another die, as listed </p>
<p>under  individual  weapon  entries. A  dagger,  for </p>
<p>instance, inflicts Str+1d4 damage.</p>
<p><b>Double Tap:</b> The weapon can rapidly fire two </p>
<p>rounds. Rather than rolling twice, add +1 to the </p>
<p>Shooting and damage rolls.</p>
<p><b>HW (Heavy Weapon):</b> The weapon can affect </p>
<p>vehicles or other devices with Heavy Armor.</p>
<p><b>HE (High Explosive):</b> High explosive rounds </p>
<p>use a burst template, the size of which is noted </p>
<p>in  the  weapon  or  ammunition’s  notes.  See  the </p>
<p>rules for Area of Effect attacks in Chapter Three.</p>
<p><b>Minimum  Strength: </b> A  character  whose </p>
<p>Strength is lower than the weapon die can use the </p>
<p>weapon, but there are penalties. First, the weapon </p>
<p>die can’t be higher than his Strength die. So if a </p>
<p>scrawny kid (d4 Str) picks up a long sword (d8), </p>
<p>he rolls 2d4 damage, not d4+d8. A brawny hero </p>
<p>with Str d10 rolls d10+d8 when using the same </p>
<p>long sword. </p>
<p>Second, if the Strength die isn’t at least equal </p>
<p>to the weapon die, the attacker doesn’t get any of </p>
<p>the weapon’s inherent bonuses, such as +1 Parry </p>
<p>or Reach. He still retains any penalties, however </p>
<p>(like –1 Parry).</p>
<p>If a weapon has a damage listed as Str+d8+2, </p>
<p>for  instance,  then  the  minimum  Strength  the </p>
<p>wielder must have is a d8.</p>
<p>Some ranged weapons list a minimum Strength </p>
<p>to use as well. A character with a lower Strength </p>
<p>can use the weapon, but suffers a –1 penalty to his </p>
<p>attack roll for every step of difference between his </p>
<p>Strength and the minimum Strength required. The </p>
<p>penalty is ignored if the weapon can be braced on </p>
<p>a bipod or other support. </p>
<p><b>Parry +X:</b> The weapon adds the bonus to the </p>
<p>character’s Parry score when used.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--55</p>
<p>Gear</p>
<p><b>Range:</b> This lists the weapon’s Short, Medium, </p>
<p>and Long range. Ranges are listed in inches so </p>
<p>that  you  can  use  a  ruler  to  move,  shoot,  and </p>
<p>fight on the table-top with miniatures. Each inch </p>
<p>is equal to 2 yards in the real world, so that 5” is </p>
<p>really 10 yards, or 30 feet.</p>
<p>Weapon ranges are “effective” ranges for the </p>
<p>table-top. If you need to know the real world range </p>
<p>of a weapon (for battles that don’t take place on </p>
<p>the table-top, for instance), multiply each range </p>
<p>bracket by 2.5. A tank round with a Long range of </p>
<p>300, for example, has a “real world” Long range </p>
<p>of 750”, or 1500 yards.</p>
<p><b>Rate of Fire:</b> This is the maximum number of </p>
<p>shots that may be taken by this weapon per action. </p>
<p>Unless a weapon says otherwise, the user can fire </p>
<p>up to the weapon’s Rate of Fire (rather than its </p>
<p>full Rate of Fire). If a single shot is taken, it uses </p>
<p>a single round of ammunition and does not incur </p>
<p>any recoil penalties. Two or more shots with such </p>
<p>weapons always incurs the –2 autofire penalty.</p>
<p><b>Reach:</b> Weapons with “reach” allow their user to </p>
<p>make Fighting attacks at the listed range. A reach </p>
<p>of 1”, for example, allows a character to strike a </p>
<p>target 1” distant. Weapons without a reach value </p>
<p>can only strike targets at arm’s length (adjacent).</p>
<p>Reach  can  be  very  important  when  fighting </p>
<p>from horseback and <i>against</i> mounted foes (see </p>
<p>page 83). </p>
<p><b>Reloading:</b> Antique weapons, such as muskets </p>
<p>and  crossbows,  are  very  slow  to  reload.  Each </p>
<p>weapon tells you how many actions it requires </p>
<p>to reload, such as 1/3, which means the weapon </p>
<p>can fire every third round if the firer does nothing </p>
<p>but load every action. Characters can load and </p>
<p>walk at no penalty. If the character tries to run and </p>
<p>load, he must make an Agility roll (at the usual </p>
<p>–2 penalty for running). Failure simply means no </p>
<p>progress toward reloading was made that action.</p>
<p><b>Semi-Auto: </b> The  weapon  fires  on  semi-auto </p>
<p>and can take advantage of the double-tap rules </p>
<p>(page 81).</p>
<p><b>Snapfire Penalty:</b> Certain weapons, such as </p>
<p>sniper rifles, are very inaccurate if fired “from the </p>
<p>hip”  rather  than  using  their  excellent  sights  or </p>
<p>scopes. If the character moves in the action he </p>
<p>fires, he suffers a –2 penalty.</p>
<p><b>Three Round Burst:</b> The  weapon can fire 3 </p>
<p>rounds with one pull of the trigger. This adds +2 </p>
<p>to the Shooting and damage rolls at the cost of </p>
<p>the extra ammunition.</p>
<p>Encumbrance</p>
<p>In  general,  you  shouldn’t  worry  about </p>
<p>Encumbrance.  Characters  will  usually  carry  no </p>
<p>more  than  their  characters  think  they  actually </p>
<p>need. But occasionally it may become dramatically </p>
<p>important—such  as  during  a  chase  or  when </p>
<p>attempting  to  lug  a  heavy  treasure  away  from </p>
<p>a  roaring  dragon!  When  that  occurs,  use  the </p>
<p>guidelines below.</p>
<p>A character can carry five times his Strength die </p>
<p>type in pounds without incurring any penalties. </p>
<p>This is called his “Load Limit.” A character with </p>
<p>a Strength of d8, for example, can comfortably </p>
<p>carry  40  pounds.  (Ignore  normal  clothes  when </p>
<p>figuring weight.)</p>
<p>Carrying too much weight inflicts a –1 penalty for </p>
<p>every additional multiple of your Load Limit. The </p>
<p>penalty applies to all Agility and Strength totals, as </p>
<p>well as skills linked to either of those two attributes.</p>
<p>A  hero  with  a  d8  Strength,  for  example,  has </p>
<p>a Load Limit of 40 pounds. He can carry 41-80 </p>
<p>pounds  at a –1  penalty  to  his  Strength, Agility, </p>
<p>and related skill rolls. He could also carry 81-120 </p>
<p>pounds at –2, or 121-160 pounds at –3.</p>
<p>Characters cannot regularly carry weight that </p>
<p>inflicts a penalty of more than –3. They may be </p>
<p>able to lift greater weights (up to a –4 penalty) </p>
<p>for a few short steps at the discretion of the GM, </p>
<p>however.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--56</p>
<p>Selected.Gear.Notes</p>
<p>Below  are  notes  for  some  of  the  weapons, </p>
<p>armor,  and  other  devices  you’ll  find  on  the </p>
<p>Equipment Lists.</p>
<p>Armor</p>
<p>Note that the weights listed for most large suits </p>
<p>of  armor  such  as  hauberks,  plate,  and  power </p>
<p>armor, are figured for their “distributed” weight. </p>
<p>This assumes the armor is properly fitted, which </p>
<p>takes  a  Knowledge  (Armorsmithing)  roll,  some </p>
<p>basic tools, and 1d6 hours. If armor is not properly </p>
<p>fitted (such as when wearing armor taken from a </p>
<p>foe), the weight is typically doubled.</p>
<p>Kevlar</p>
<p>Kevlar offers 2 points of protection against most </p>
<p>attacks. Kevlar weave “binds” spinning bullets and </p>
<p>so negates up to 4 points of AP from bullets, and </p>
<p>provides +4 protection from them as well.</p>
<p>Power.Armor</p>
<p>Power armor suits carry much of their own mass, </p>
<p>hence the low weight values. Scout suits weigh </p>
<p>100  pounds  when  powered  down,  Battle  suits </p>
<p>weigh 150, and Heavy Suits weigh 220 pounds. </p>
<p>All power armor contains comm-units with a 5-mile </p>
<p>range. Powered armor typically lasts for one week </p>
<p>without recharge. It requires a special recharging </p>
<p>facility and 10 hours to return to full power. The </p>
<p>GM  may  decide  suits  lose  power  faster  under </p>
<p>excessive use.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Scout Suit: </b>These suits are made for </p>
<p>reconnaissance.  In  addition  to  the </p>
<p>standard comm-unit, they are coated </p>
<p>in stealth paint that adds +4 to Stealth </p>
<p>rolls  vs.  radar  and  other  automated </p>
<p>detection systems (but not people).</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Battle Suit: </b>This is the standard power </p>
<p>armor worn by most heavy troopers in </p>
<p>futuristic settings. It increases Strength </p>
<p>by one die type, adds +2 to Pace, </p>
<p>and  allows  users  to  jump  2d6” </p>
<p>horizontally  or  1d6”  vertically. </p>
<p>A Heads Up Display provides </p>
<p>targeting information for linked </p>
<p>weapons,  adding  +1  to  the </p>
<p>wearer’s Shooting rolls.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Heavy  Suit: </b> Heavy  suits  (or </p>
<p>assault suits) are designed for </p>
<p>hard fighting under the most </p>
<p>intense  combat  conditions. </p>
<p>They  subtract  2  from  Pace </p>
<p>and  boost  Strength  by  two </p>
<p>die  types. They  mount  at  least  one  heavy </p>
<p>weapon of some sort such as a flamethrower </p>
<p>or a minigun, and feature enhanced targeting </p>
<p>computers  that  add  +2  to  the  character’s </p>
<p>Shooting rolls.</p>
<p>Shields</p>
<p>If a character with a shield is hit by a ranged </p>
<p>attack  from  the  protected  side,  roll  damage </p>
<p>normally, but add the Armor bonus of the shield to </p>
<p>the character’s Toughness (it acts as an obstacle).</p>
<p>Ranged.Weapons.&amp;.Accessories</p>
<p>Bipods</p>
<p>Most full machine guns are also equipped with </p>
<p>either  an  integral  or  detachable  bipod.  Once </p>
<p>deployed, these provide a more stable shooting </p>
<p>position and help control recoil. It takes one action </p>
<p>to deploy a bipod and set the weapon up. Once </p>
<p>in position the autofire penalty is reduced to –1. </p>
<p>If the hero moves, this benefit is negated and he </p>
<p>will have to spend another action to redeploy the </p>
<p>bipod.</p>
<p>Scopes</p>
<p>Optical scopes can be attached to all manner </p>
<p>of firearms from rifles to hand guns, and magnify </p>
<p>targets to make distance shooting easier. A scope </p>
<p>provides a +2 Shooting bonus to shots at Medium </p>
<p>Range  or  higher  as  long  as  the  firer  does  not </p>
<p>move this round.</p>
<p>Shotguns</p>
<p>Shotguns  fire  a  spread  of  metal </p>
<p>balls (also called “shot”), and so do </p>
<p>more damage at close range where </p>
<p>the spread is less and more of the </p>
<p>shot  hits  the  target.  Farther  away, </p>
<p>the  shot  spreads  more  and  causes </p>
<p>less damage. Because of the increased </p>
<p>chance  of  hitting  someone  due  to </p>
<p>the  spread,  shotguns  add  +2  to  their </p>
<p>user’s  Shooting  rolls. They  cause </p>
<p>1d6 damage at Long range, 2d6 </p>
<p>at  Medium,  and  3d6  at  Short </p>
<p>range.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Double  Barrels:</b> </p>
<p>Shotguns  with  two  attached </p>
<p>barrels  are  called  “double </p>
<p>barrels.” If the attacker wants </p>
<p>to fire both barrels at once, he </p>
<p>rolls  an  additional  Shooting </p>
<p>die  just  like  when  firing  Full </p>
<p>Auto (plus Wild Die), including </p>
<p>recoil (which cancels out the </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--57</p>
<p>Gear</p>
<p>usual  +2  for  shotguns).  Roll  damage  for  each </p>
<p>successful shot separately.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Slugs:</b>  Shotguns  can  also  fire  slugs.  The </p>
<p>attacker doesn’t get the +2 shotgun bonus to his </p>
<p>Shooting roll, but the damage is 2d10 regardless </p>
<p>of the range increment.</p>
<p>Special.Weapons</p>
<p>Bouncing.Betties</p>
<p>These deadly mines are designed to pop up into </p>
<p>the air and rain shrapnel down from about head-</p>
<p>height. Only full overhead cover offers an Armor </p>
<p>bonus against such devices. Simply being prone </p>
<p>offers no protection from these deadly explosives.</p>
<p>Cannons</p>
<p>Cannons can fire three different types of shells: </p>
<p>solid shot, shrapnel, and canister. The crew can </p>
<p>pick the type of ammunition to be loaded each </p>
<p>time it reloads.</p>
<p>Solid Shot is just that—big round balls made </p>
<p>to batter walls or plow through packed ranks of </p>
<p>troops. To fire, the leader of the crew makes a </p>
<p>Shooting roll as usual. A target directly behind and </p>
<p>adjacent to the first is also hit on a d6 roll of 1-3, </p>
<p>and takes full damage. This continues until there </p>
<p>are no more adjacent foes.</p>
<p>Shrapnel is an explosive shell filled with metal </p>
<p>balls, nails, and other scraps. The debris is hurled </p>
<p>outward when the shell explodes, shredding lightly </p>
<p>armored targets in a shower of steel. Shrapnel is </p>
<p>an area effect attack, and uses the Medium Burst </p>
<p>Template.</p>
<p>Canister is a shell made to detonate inside the </p>
<p>barrel  of  the  cannon. The  jagged  metal  inside </p>
<p>the “canister” then sprays out of the cannon to </p>
<p>shred anything within its deadly cone like a giant </p>
<p>shotgun. To  determine  the  effects  of  canister, </p>
<p>place a ruler in front of the cannon in the direction </p>
<p>you want it to fire and make a Shooting roll with no </p>
<p>range modifiers. If the shot is missed, move the </p>
<p>far end of the ruler 1” left or right (roll randomly).</p>
<p>Now place a Medium Burst Template at the near </p>
<p>end of the ruler and move it directly forward along </p>
<p>that path for 24”. Every target under the template </p>
<p>is hit for 2d6 damage. Cover acts as Armor just </p>
<p>as with any area effect weapon, meaning prone </p>
<p>characters add +2 to their Toughness.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Reloading: </b> Cannons  require  one  action  to </p>
<p>reload  with  a  crew  of  four,  or  two  actions  with </p>
<p>less crewmen.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Line of Sight:</b> Cannon crews must be able to </p>
<p>see their targets to hit them. Howitzers, mortars, </p>
<p>and  bombards  may  fire  at  targets  they  cannot </p>
<p>see  (assuming  they  know  roughly  where  the </p>
<p>target is) at a –4 penalty, and double deviation </p>
<p>(see <b>Combat</b>).</p>
<p>Flamethrowers</p>
<p>Flamethrowers include any device that squirts </p>
<p>an incendiary liquid or even pure flame. To use </p>
<p>it, the attacker places the small end of the Cone </p>
<p>Template at the tip of his character’s weapon, and </p>
<p>the large end on as many targets as he’s able. </p>
<p>The attacker then makes a Shooting roll at +2. </p>
<p>Defenders who make an Agility roll equal to or </p>
<p>greater than the attacker’s Shooting total move </p>
<p>out of the way and are unaffected. The rest suffer </p>
<p>the weapon’s damage (typically 2d10) and roll to </p>
<p>see if they catch fire (see <b>Fire</b>).</p>
<p>Like other area effect weapons, flamethrowers </p>
<p>typically  bypass  most  body  armor  (see<b> Area </b></p>
<p><b>Effect Attacks</b>).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Vehicular  Flamethrowers: </b> Military </p>
<p>organizations  often  have  flamethrowers  with </p>
<p>much longer ranges. The flame fired from a British </p>
<p>Crocodile, for example, has a range of about 70 </p>
<p>yards (35”). The flame must be arced to reach </p>
<p>these  distances  however,  so  a  character  may </p>
<p>choose to use a Medium Burst Template instead </p>
<p>of the Cone Template when using such weapons. </p>
<p>The center of the template may be placed up to the </p>
<p>maximum range of the particular flamethrower, as </p>
<p>listed in its notes. This is treated just like any other </p>
<p>area effect attack, though targets still get a chance </p>
<p>to dodge out of the cone as detailed above.</p>
<p>Grenades</p>
<p>A  character  within  the  burst  radius  has  two </p>
<p>additional  options.  To  pick  up  and  throw  the </p>
<p>grenade before it goes off, he must make an Agility </p>
<p>roll at –4 (or –2 if he was on Hold). Failure means it </p>
<p>goes off and he takes an additional die of damage.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Covering  Grenades:</b> A character  may also </p>
<p>throw  himself  on  a  grenade.  He  takes  double </p>
<p>the  normal  dice  of  damage  for  his </p>
<p>heroic act, but his total Toughness </p>
<p>is  subtracted  from  the  damage </p>
<p>inflicted  on  other  characters </p>
<p>in  the  blast  radius. </p>
<p>Allies won’t normally </p>
<p>perform </p>
<p>such </p>
<p>a </p>
<p>suicidal act, though </p>
<p>t h e  </p>
<p>G M  </p>
<p>m i g h t </p>
<p>rule  otherwise  in </p>
<p>specific  situations, </p>
<p>such  as  when  an </p>
<p>ally  has  a  “loyal” </p>
<p>personality.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--58</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Missiles</p>
<p>Air-to-air  (or  space-to-space)  weapons  are </p>
<p>designed  to  destroy  enemy  fighters  and  other </p>
<p>small targets with a focused warhead. To activate, </p>
<p>the pilot must first “capture” the target’s signature </p>
<p>on his own control panel. This is accomplished by </p>
<p>various  means  including  heat-signature,  radar, </p>
<p>emissions,  or  even  profile,  depending  on  tech </p>
<p>level.</p>
<p>To get a lock, the pilot picks his target and must </p>
<p>then  succeed  at  an  opposed  Piloting  roll. The </p>
<p>attacker must subtract range modifiers from the </p>
<p>Piloting roll just as if he were Shooting.</p>
<p>Once  locked,  the  pilot  decides  how  many </p>
<p>missiles to release (usually up to his full payload </p>
<p>depending on his craft). At Short Range, the target </p>
<p>has one round to evade. He has two rounds (and </p>
<p>chances) at Medium Range, and three at Long </p>
<p>Range.</p>
<p>Evading a missile requires a Piloting roll at –4. </p>
<p>Note that many craft contain additional evasion </p>
<p>systems,  such  as  chafe  or  flares,  that  add  +4 </p>
<p>to this roll if they’re of the right type (flares for </p>
<p>heat-seeking missiles and chafe for radar-guided </p>
<p>missiles).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Air  to  Ground  Targets:</b>  Use  the  same </p>
<p>procedure as above but the target makes a Driving </p>
<p>or Boating roll against the attacker’s Piloting skill.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Surface to Air Missiles:</b> As above, but the </p>
<p>attacker makes a Shooting roll and the defender </p>
<p>makes a Piloting roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Anti-Missile  Systems:</b>  Larger  ships  often </p>
<p>have anti-missile systems designed to shoot down </p>
<p>missiles with targeted lasers, walls of matter, or </p>
<p>hails of lead. All systems require a modicum of skill </p>
<p>and a lot of luck. First the crewman in charge of </p>
<p>the particular AMS battery makes a Shooting roll </p>
<p>minus the range. (Don’t subtract for the size or </p>
<p>speed of the missile—the AMS already accounts </p>
<p>for that.) Each successful hit has a 1 in 6 chance </p>
<p>of shooting down the torpedo. A Phalanx system </p>
<p>with a RoF of 5, for example, rolls 5 dice, and each </p>
<p>die that comes up a “1” shoots a missile down. </p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Obstacles:</b> Evading prey can add +2 to the </p>
<p>Piloting  roll  with  substantial  cover—such  as </p>
<p>asteroids, canyon walls, or the hull of a capital </p>
<p>ship.</p>

<p>Vehicle.Mounted.&amp;.AT.Guns</p>
<p>Note that most tank guns can fire Armor Piercing (AP) or High Explosive (HE) shells. A 37mm tank gun, </p>
<p>for example, can fire an Armor Piercing round at 4d8 damage with an AP value of 3, or a High Explosive </p>
<p>shell that causes 4d6 damage, has an AP of 3, and impacts with a Medium Burst Template.</p>

<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--66</p>
<p>Vehicles</p>
<p>On the following pages are a number of sample </p>
<p>vehicles for land, air, and water, including some </p>
<p>military  vehicles  such  as  tanks  and  armored </p>
<p>personnel carriers.</p>
<p><b>Acc/Top  Speed </b> is  the  vehicle’s Acceleration </p>
<p>and Top  Speed  in  inches  per  round. The Top </p>
<p>Speed of vehicles is set for battlefields (not the </p>
<p>open highway, where they can usually double their </p>
<p>speed). They’re adapted to work on the table-top, </p>
<p>which means they’re not entirely realistic but work </p>
<p>well for the game. Top Speed is typically half the </p>
<p>vehicle’s operational speed for military vehicles, </p>
<p>or one-quarter speed for civilian cars. Acceleration </p>
<p>varies far more widely—use the examples from </p>
<p>the Vehicle Table as a guide.</p>
<p><b>Climb </b>is a relative value used to determine if </p>
<p>an aircraft has a better ability to gain altitude than </p>
<p>another, as you’ll see in the Chase Rules on page </p>
<p>82. If converting vehicles of your own, use the </p>
<p>following guidelines:</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>Climb </b></p>
<p><b>Example Aircraft</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Balloons, steampunk helicopters</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–1 </p>
<p>Craft which ascend relatively slowly, </p>
<p>such as civilian helicopters</p>
<p> </p>
<p>0 </p>
<p>WWII  bombers,  “heavy”  aircraft, </p>
<p>modern helicopters</p>
<p> </p>
<p>1 </p>
<p>Light civilian aircraft, medium fighters</p>
<p> </p>
<p>2 </p>
<p>Early light fighters</p>
<p> </p>
<p>3 </p>
<p>Jets, very agile fighters, aircraft which </p>
<p>ascend very quickly</p>
<p>Previous editions of <i>Savage Worlds</i> used Climb </p>
<p>as an actual measurement of inches an aircraft </p>
<p>could ascend in a round. In setting books where </p>
<p>Climb  uses  the  previous  Climb  values,  simply </p>
<p>estimate based on the table above.</p>
<p><b>Toughness</b>  is  the  vehicle’s  base  durability </p>
<p>including Armor (in parentheses); vehicles  with </p>
<p>three  numbers  listed  have  different  values  for </p>
<p>Front/Sides/Rear. Figuring Toughness for vehicles </p>
<p>is very difficult due to different types of materials </p>
<p>or armor, weak points vs. strong points, and so </p>
<p>on, but in general you can use this chart to set </p>
<p>the base Toughness:</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>Weight in Tons </b></p>
<p><b>Base Toughness</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>&lt;1 </p>
<p>6–8</p>
<p> </p>
<p>2–4 </p>
<p>9–11</p>
<p> </p>
<p>5–25 </p>
<p>11–12</p>
<p> </p>
<p>26–60 </p>
<p>13–14</p>
<p> </p>
<p>61–100 </p>
<p>15–18</p>
<p> </p>
<p>100–200 </p>
<p>19–22</p>
<p> Every 100 tons thereafter </p>
<p>+3–5 Toughness</p>
<p>For Armor,  figure  the  average  thickness  in </p>
<p>inches of any metal on the vehicle and multiply </p>
<p>by 2.5. This is a good baseline and will need more </p>
<p>tinkering if it’s really important based on the type </p>
<p>of metal, how much of the vehicle it covers, and </p>
<p>so on.</p>
<p><b>Passengers </b>lists the number of crew plus any </p>
<p>additional passengers it can transport. A notation </p>
<p>of “2+8,” for example, means it has a crew of 2 </p>
<p>and can carry 8 additional passengers.</p>
<p><b>Cost</b> is the average price of the vehicle. If the </p>
<p>cost is “Military,” the cost is beyond the means of </p>
<p>most characters and is typically purchased only </p>
<p>by state militaries.</p>
<p>Special.Notes</p>
<p><b>Air Bags:</b> Passengers with air bags roll half the </p>
<p>normal damage dice in a collision (round down), </p>
<p>minus one.</p>
<p><b>Amphibious:</b>  The  vehicle  can  enter  water </p>
<p>without flooding or capsizing. See the individual </p>
<p>descriptions  for  their  movement  rates  while  in </p>
<p>water.</p>
<p><b>AMCM (Anti-Missile Counter Measures):</b> Some </p>
<p>jets or spacecraft are equipped with anti-missile </p>
<p>counter measures, such as chaff, flares, or decoy </p>
<p>pods. The  number  of AMCM  is  a  reflection  of </p>
<p>“bursts,” not actual flares or decoys. When used, </p>
<p>an AMCM adds +2 to the user’s Piloting roll <i>that </i></p>
<p><i>round only</i> for purposes of evading missiles.</p>
<p><b>Fixed Gun:</b> The vehicle’s weapon cannot rotate.</p>
<p><b>Four Wheel Drive (4WD):</b> These vehicles treat </p>
<p>each inch of difficult terrain as 1.5 (instead of 2).</p>
<p><b>Heavy Armor:</b> Only weapons marked as Heavy </p>
<p>Weapons can hurt this vehicle, regardless of the </p>
<p>damage roll. This keeps a really lucky pistol shot </p>
<p>from destroying a tank. Vehicles with Heavy Armor </p>
<p>halve damage they take from colliding with other </p>
<p>obstacles  (including  vehicles)  that  don’t  have </p>
<p>Heavy Armor.</p>
<p><b>Heavy Weapon:</b> This weapon can harm vehicles </p>
<p>equipped with Heavy Armor.</p>
<p><b>Hover:</b> The vehicle is a hovercraft and can ignore </p>
<p>most low terrain obstacles and water.</p>
<p><b>Infrared Night Vision:</b> Thermal imaging devices </p>
<p>halve darkness penalties (round down) for heat-</p>
<p>producing targets.</p>
<p><b>Night Vision:</b> “Starlight” and other night vision </p>
<p>equipment  eliminate  Dim  and  Dark  lighting </p>
<p>penalties.</p>

<p>  <b>Notes: </b>Heavy Armor, Sloped Armor, Tracked</p>
<p>  <b>Weapons:</b> DT 7.62 turret</p>
<p><b>Sloped Armor:</b>  In  the  best  armored  vehicles, </p>
<p>armor is sloped so as to increase the chance that </p>
<p>a hit will be deflected off the tank’s armor. Ballistic </p>
<p>attacks against this target suffer a –2 penalty.</p>
<p><b>Spacecraft:</b> The vehicle is designed for use in </p>
<p>outer space. Those followed with /Atmospheric </p>
<p>can enter and exit planetary orbits as well.</p>
<p><b>Stabilizer:</b> A  stabilizer  reduces  the  Unstable </p>
<p>Platform penalty for whatever weapon it’s attached </p>
<p>to (usually its main gun unless stated otherwise) </p>
<p>to  –1  (rather  than  the  usual  –2). An  Improved </p>
<p>Stabilizer negates the penalty entirely.</p>
<p><b>Stealth Paint: </b>This is black paint that imposes a </p>
<p>–4 to rolls made to spot the vehicle with sensors.</p>
<p><b>Tracked:</b> Unless otherwise noted, the vehicle is </p>
<p>assumed to have wheels. Tracked vehicles can </p>
<p>climb over most low obstacles such as logs, and </p>
<p>treat each inch of difficult terrain as 1.5 (instead </p>
<p>of 2).</p>
<p><b>Weapons:</b> Weapons are statted in the vehicle’s </p>
<p>text. Note that the Rate of Fire for missiles and </p>
<p>rockets indicates how many may be fired in one </p>
<p>action.</p>

<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--70</p>
<p>Chapter.Three:.</p>
<p>Game.Rules</p>
<p>The <i> Savage  Worlds </i> rules  provide  a  simple </p>
<p>framework for your tales of adventure and glory. </p>
<p>In this chapter we’ll show you how to make basic </p>
<p>skill and attribute checks and make your combats </p>
<p>Fast, Furious, and Fun!</p>
<p>Wild.Cards.&amp;.Extras</p>
<p>Your hero (a player character), and unique allies, </p>
<p>villains, and monsters are collectively called “Wild </p>
<p>Cards.” These beings have a little better chance at </p>
<p>doing things, are a little tougher to put down, and </p>
<p>are generally more detailed than common guards, </p>
<p>minions, or lackeys—collectively called “Extras.”</p>
<p>Wild Cards are noted with a design of some sort </p>
<p>before their name, like this:</p>
<p>X</p>
<p>.Serious.Chapel</p>
<p>The  actual  Wild  Card  symbol  varies  and  is </p>
<p>usually  themed  for  the  particular  setting,  such </p>
<p>as a skull-and-crossbones for a pirate game like </p>
<p><i>50 Fathoms,</i> or the visage of the puritan himself </p>
<p>in <i>Solomon Kane®,</i> but a symbol in front of the </p>
<p>name always means the character is a Wild Card<i>.</i></p>
<p>Besides  your  own  characters,  it’s  up  to  the </p>
<p>Game Master to decide which characters are Wild </p>
<p>Cards. The sergeant of the City Watch probably </p>
<p>isn’t a Wild Card, but Sergeant Grimlock of the City </p>
<p>Watch, a veteran of many wars and an important </p>
<p>character in your campaign, certainly is. Skytch </p>
<p>the Dragon is also a Wild Card, though his three </p>
<p>young  wyrms  aren’t. You’ll  see  the  difference </p>
<p>between Wild Cards and Extras as you continue </p>
<p>to read, but for later reference, the differences are:</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Wild Cards can suffer multiple wounds.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Wild Cards always roll a Wild Die along with </p>
<p>their Trait die when making tests and take the </p>
<p>better of the two.</p>
<p>Trait.Tests</p>
<p>To use an attribute or skill, simply roll the die </p>
<p>assigned to it. If the result is a 4 or better (the </p>
<p>“Target Number” or TN), the action is successful. </p>
<p>For example, if a  character’s Strength is a  d6, </p>
<p>he  rolls  a  six-sided  die.  On  a  4  or  better,  he’s </p>
<p>successful.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Modifiers: </b>Circumstances modify the die roll, </p>
<p>such as shooting at something at long range or </p>
<p>finding a well-hidden clue. Some things, such as </p>
<p>ranged attacks, have standard modifiers. It’s up </p>
<p>to the GM to determine any modifiers for more </p>
<p>subjective tasks, such as spotting an ambush or </p>
<p>eavesdropping on a conversation through a door.</p>
<p>In general, an easy task, such as finding tracks </p>
<p>in the mud, is made at +2. A difficult task, such as </p>
<p>finding tracks by torchlight, is made at –2. A very </p>
<p>difficult task, such as finding tracks in a rainstorm, </p>
<p>is made at –4.</p>
<p>Aces</p>
<p>All  Trait  tests  and  damage  rolls  in <i> Savage </i></p>
<p><i>Worlds</i> are open-ended. That means that when </p>
<p>you roll the highest number possible on a die (a 6 </p>
<p>on a d6, an 8 on a d8, and so on), you get to roll </p>
<p>that die again and add it to the total. This is called </p>
<p>an “Ace.” Any modifiers to the die roll should be </p>
<p>tacked on after adding up an Aced roll.</p>
<p>Raises</p>
<p>Sometimes  it’s  important  to  know  just  how </p>
<p>successful a Trait test was. Every 4 points over </p>
<p>what you need for success is called a “raise.” If </p>
<p>your hero needs a 4 to Shoot an opponent and </p>
<p>rolls an 11, he hits with one raise (and would have </p>
<p>two raises with a roll of 12). Figure raises after </p>
<p>adjusting for any modifiers.</p>
<p>Unskilled.Attempts</p>
<p>If a character doesn’t have a skill for an action </p>
<p>he’s attempting, he rolls a d4 and subtracts 2 from </p>
<p>the total. Wild Card characters still get their Wild </p>
<p>Die for these rolls (which are also subject to the –2 </p>
<p>penalty). The GM may decide that a character has </p>
<p>no chance at a particular skill if he has no training </p>
<p>in it—such as performing surgery or flying a plane.</p>
<p>Opposed.Rolls</p>
<p>Sometimes rolls are “opposed” by an opponent. </p>
<p>If two characters are wrestling for control of an </p>
<p>ancient  artifact,  for  example,  they  both  make </p>
<p>Strength rolls and compare results.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--71</p>
<p>Game.Rules</p>
<p>When this happens, the acting character gets </p>
<p>his Trait total first. If he wants to spend Bennies </p>
<p>(see the next section), he does so now. When </p>
<p>he’s satisfied with his total, his opponent gets to </p>
<p>roll. The highest total wins. In a tie, the two foes </p>
<p>continue to struggle with no clear victor.</p>
<p>The  winner  of  an  opposed  roll  considers </p>
<p>his  opponent’s  total  as  his TN  for  purposes  of </p>
<p>determining any raises.</p>
<p>Cooperative.Rolls</p>
<p>Sometimes characters may want to cooperate </p>
<p>and help a friend complete some kind of urgent </p>
<p>task. If two or more characters want to perform a </p>
<p>task together (and the GM decides it’s possible </p>
<p>for them to do so), the lead character makes his </p>
<p>roll and adds +1 for every success and raise his </p>
<p>companions achieved on their own rolls. This has </p>
<p>a normal maximum of +4 for all tasks except those </p>
<p>of Strength, which have no maximum.</p>
<p>Characters may <i>not </i>make cooperative rolls if </p>
<p>they don’t actually have the skill in question. (You </p>
<p>can’t make default rolls to aid with Boating, for </p>
<p>example, if your character doesn’t actually have </p>
<p>the Boating skill.)</p>
<p>Group.Rolls</p>
<p>When you want to make a noncombat Trait roll </p>
<p>for a group of Extras, roll one Trait die as usual </p>
<p>along with a Wild Die. Take the better of the two </p>
<p>as always and treat this as the group’s total. This </p>
<p>way  you  get  a  nice  average  without  having  to </p>
<p>make  individual  Fear  rolls  for  every  Extra  who </p>
<p>sees a dragon, or watch one clumsy soldier ruin </p>
<p>a stealthy approach for his 49 companions.</p>
<p>The.Wild.Die</p>
<p>Extras roll a single die as described above. But </p>
<p>Wild Cards roll an extra d6 and take the highest </p>
<p>of their normal die or the “Wild Die” when making </p>
<p>skill or attribute rolls. Wild Dice are rolled just like </p>
<p>the Trait die, and can Ace as well (see above).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Critical Failure: </b>The downside is that snake-</p>
<p>eyes (double 1s) on one of these rolls is a critical </p>
<p>failure. The GM gets to make up something rotten </p>
<p>to happen to your character. That’s the price Fate </p>
<p>charges for making someone a hero.</p>
<p><i><b>Example: </b> Buck  Savage,  international </i></p>
<p><i>adventurer, faces wild-eyed cultists. He has </i></p>
<p><i>a d10 Shooting and rolls his d6 Wild Die. The </i></p>
<p><i>Wild Die comes up 4, but he Aces (a 10) on </i></p>
<p><i>the d10. He sets the Wild Die aside and rolls </i></p>
<p><i>the d10 again. He gets another 10, then rolls </i></p>
<p><i>again and gets a 3. His total is (10+10+3=) 23!</i></p>
<p>Bennies</p>
<p>Every now and then the dice may not work for </p>
<p>you. That’s why <i>Savage Worlds</i> gives you, the </p>
<p>player, a little control over your hero’s fate.</p>
<p>Every  player  starts  each  game  session  with </p>
<p>three “Bennies” (American slang for “benefits”), </p>
<p>represented by gaming stones or other tokens that </p>
<p>signify a little bit of good luck or fate. The Game </p>
<p>Master may also give you more Bennies for great </p>
<p>roleplaying, overcoming major obstacles, or even </p>
<p>entertaining everyone with an outlandish action, </p>
<p>side-splitting comment, or other memorable act. </p>
<p>(Tips for awarding Bennies can be found in the </p>
<p>Game Master’s section on page 145.)</p>
<p>You  can  use  Bennies  to  reroll  any Trait  test. </p>
<p>Make the entire roll from scratch. If you’re firing </p>
<p>three shots on full-auto and don’t like the results, </p>
<p>pick up all three dice and your Wild Die and roll </p>
<p>again.  You  can  keep  spending  Bennies  and </p>
<p>rerolling as long as you like, and take the best of </p>
<p>your attempts. If you roll a 5, for example, and a </p>
<p>Benny gets you a 4, keep the original 5 instead.</p>
<p>Bennies cannot be spent on tables, damage rolls </p>
<p>(unless a character has the No Mercy Edge), or </p>
<p>any other roll that isn’t a Trait roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Soak Rolls:</b> Bennies can also be used to save </p>
<p>your bacon from deadly attacks. Choose carefully </p>
<p>where you spend them! See <b>Damage</b> for complete </p>
<p>information on how to make Soak rolls.</p>
<p>Game.Master.Bennies</p>
<p>Game Masters get Bennies too. At the start of </p>
<p>each session, the GM gets one Benny for each </p>
<p>player character. He may use these for any of his </p>
<p>villains throughout the course of the game.</p>
<p>Each  of  the  GM’s  Wild  Cards  also  gets  two </p>
<p>Bennies per game session. They can use these </p>
<p>or any of the Bennies in the common pool to save </p>
<p>their  evil  skins,  but  they  can’t  share  their  own </p>
<p>Bennies with other nonplayer characters. </p>
<p>As with heroes, Bennies are not saved between </p>
<p>sessions.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--72</p>
<p>Combat</p>
<p>Whether  they  are  the  blood-soaked  plains  of </p>
<p>Mars or the corpse-strewn battlefields of the far </p>
<p>future—these are <i>Savage Worlds </i>and there will </p>
<p>be violence. We recommend using miniatures or </p>
<p>markers of some sort so that players understand </p>
<p>their surroundings and can use the terrain to their </p>
<p>advantage. If miniatures aren’t your thing, see the </p>
<p>sidebar on page 73.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Distance:</b> Because the game assumes you </p>
<p>are  using  terrain  or  a  battle-mat  and  standard </p>
<p>28mm miniatures, movement and weapon ranges </p>
<p>are listed in inches. If you need to translate that </p>
<p>to regular distance, each inch is equal to 2 yards.</p>
<p>If the GM needs a different scale to accommodate </p>
<p>a  larger  battle,  such  as  a  long-range  firefight </p>
<p>between tank platoons, simply divide weapon and </p>
<p>movement ranges as needed.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Time:</b>  When  a  fight  breaks  out,  game  time </p>
<p>breaks down into rounds of six seconds each. Ten </p>
<p>rounds, then, is one minute.</p>
<p>Using.Allies</p>
<p>Allied Extras are divided up among all the players </p>
<p>to control. This is a very important part of <i>Savage </i></p>
<p><i>Worlds<b></i> </b>because our settings often feature allied </p>
<p>bands of skilled hirelings, fellow grunts, or loyal </p>
<p>retainers, and the game is designed to handle them </p>
<p>quickly and easily. It’s also designed for the <i>players</i> </p>
<p>to control them—not the Game Master.</p>
<p>It doesn’t matter whether or not the <i>characters </i></p>
<p>control the allies, only that the <i>players</i> do. This </p>
<p>keeps everyone involved in the action even if his </p>
<p>hero is out of the fight, and makes running large </p>
<p>combats much easier and fun for everyone. Of </p>
<p>course the GM can always take charge of Extras </p>
<p>when the need arises, but with good roleplayers, </p>
<p>this  should  rarely  be  necessary.  Some  tips  for </p>
<p>the Game Master on using Allies can be found </p>
<p>on page 146.</p>
<p>Initiative</p>
<p>The action in <i>Savage Worlds </i>is fast and furious. </p>
<p>To help the Game Master keep track of who goes </p>
<p>in what order and add a little randomness to the </p>
<p>game,  we  use  a  single  deck  of  playing  cards </p>
<p>with both Jokers left in to determine everyone’s </p>
<p>initiative.</p>
<p>Deal in characters as follows:</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Every Wild Card is dealt a single card. Any </p>
<p>allies under that player’s control act on his </p>
<p>initiative card as well.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Each  group  of  Game  Master  characters, </p>
<p>such as all zombies, all wolves, and so on, </p>
<p>share a card.</p>
<p>Exactly which nonplayer character groups get </p>
<p>their own cards is up to the GM. If he wants to </p>
<p>break his 30 zombies into 5 groups of 6, that’s fine. </p>
<p>Your goal is to do whatever makes running the </p>
<p>battle as quick and easy as possible. Generally, </p>
<p>Wild Cards and other unique characters get their </p>
<p>own card.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Shuffle:</b> Shuffle the deck after any round in </p>
<p>which a Joker was dealt.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Large Groups:</b> In very large groups, or time-</p>
<p>sensitive games, the Game Master might want </p>
<p>to try dealing a single card per side (heroes and </p>
<p>villains). On the heroes’ turn, simply start at one </p>
<p>end of the group and work quickly around. This </p>
<p>will speed  things up  dramatically if that’s more </p>
<p>important than varying initiative order. If one or </p>
<p>more characters have Level Headed or Quick, let </p>
<p>that apply to the draw (but only once).</p>
<p>The.Countdown</p>
<p>Once  the  cards  are  dealt,  the  Game  Master </p>
<p>starts the round by counting down from the Ace to </p>
<p>the Deuce, with each group resolving its actions </p>
<p>when its card comes up. </p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Ties: </b>Ties are resolved by suit order: Spades </p>
<p>are  first,  then  Hearts,  Diamonds,  and  Clubs </p>
<p>(reverse alphabetical order).</p>
<p>Jokers</p>
<p>When  a  player  draws  a  Joker,  his  character </p>
<p>can  go  whenever  he  wants  in  the  round,  even </p>
<p>interrupting another character’s action if he wants. </p>
<p>In addition, add +2 to all Trait tests this round, and </p>
<p>+2 to damage totals as well!</p>
<p>Hold</p>
<p>A  hero  may  choose  to  wait  and  see  what </p>
<p>happens by taking a Hold action. He may then </p>
<p>go later in the round if he chooses. A Held action </p>
<p>lasts until it’s used. If a character has a Held card </p>
<p>when a new round starts, he’s not dealt in.</p>
<p>If  a  character  is  Shaken  while  on  Hold,  he </p>
<p>immediately  loses  his  “Hold”  status  and  any </p>
<p>remaining  actions  for  the  round.  If  the  victim </p>
<p>soaks the attack and avoids the Shaken result, </p>
<p>he remains on Hold.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Interrupting Actions: </b>If a character on Hold </p>
<p>wants to interrupt an action (including a rival who </p>
<p>was also on Hold), he and the opponent make </p>
<p>opposed Agility rolls. Whoever rolls highest goes </p>
<p>first.  In  the  rare  case  of  a  tie,  the  actions  are </p>
<p>simultaneous.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--73</p>
<p>Game.Rules</p>
<p>Surprise</p>
<p>Combat often starts before everyone involved </p>
<p>is prepared. An ambush, a sudden double-cross, </p>
<p>or a trap might all give one side in a fight an edge </p>
<p>over the other.</p>
<p>When this happens, the side that started the </p>
<p>fight  is  not  dealt  cards  but  begins  the  fight  on </p>
<p>Hold. Victims of the surprise attack must make </p>
<p>Notice rolls. Those who make it are dealt in as </p>
<p>usual. Those who fail get no card in the first round </p>
<p>of combat.</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.Action.Cards</p>
<p><i>Some  new  players  have  been  hesitant </i></p>
<p><i>to use cards for initiative. We’ve presented </i></p>
<p><i>optional  die-rolling  systems  in  previous </i></p>
<p><i>editions,  but  the  truth  is,  the  card-based </i></p>
<p><i>initiative system works and is a lot of fun. If </i></p>
<p><i>you’re new to it, try it. </i></p>
<p><i>We’ve found the excitement of drawing a </i></p>
<p><i>Joker—or getting a Queen when the monster </i></p>
<p><i>that’s about to kill your hero is on a Jack—is </i></p>
<p><i>a major part of the game and those who give </i></p>
<p><i>it a chance quickly realize how well it works </i></p>
<p><i>for  Savage  Worlds—particularly  when  it </i></p>
<p><i>comes to keeping track of large groups of </i></p>
<p><i>heroes and villains.</i></p>
<p>Games.Without.Miniatures</p>
<p>Weapon  ranges,  character  and  vehicle </p>
<p>movement rates, and even the templates are all </p>
<p>based on using figures on a table-top. That’s why </p>
<p>they’re  measured  in  inches  rather  than  yards, </p>
<p>miles-per-hour, and so on.</p>
<p>But many times you won’t want to use miniatures. </p>
<p>Here are some quick and dirty ways to handle </p>
<p>those  situations  that  are  usually  dependent  on </p>
<p>table-top measurements.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Range:</b> The  GM  should  simply  decide  how </p>
<p>far away targets are. Mentally, it’s much easier </p>
<p>for most people to think in yards. Remember that </p>
<p>every two yards is one inch on the table-top, so </p>
<p>if a target is about 20 yards away, it’s 10” away.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Templates:</b>  Without  exact  placement  of </p>
<p>miniatures  it’s  tricky  to  know  how  many  foes </p>
<p>might be caught by an area-effect attack (like a </p>
<p>grenade).  For  these  occasions,  figure  a  Small </p>
<p>Burst Template affects 1d3 foes, a Medium Burst  </p>
<p>or Cone Template affects 2d4 foes, and a Large </p>
<p>Burst Template catches 2d6 opponents.</p>
<p>Allies  adjacent  or  in  combat  with  these  foes </p>
<p>suffer damage as well unless the attack hits with </p>
<p>a raise.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--74</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Movement</p>
<p>Characters may move their full Pace (usually 6” </p>
<p>for humans) in a round. This is considered a free </p>
<p>action and doesn’t inflict a penalty to any other </p>
<p>actions, such as firing a weapon or taunting a foe.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Crawling:</b> A character may crawl 2” per turn. </p>
<p>This counts as being prone when being fired upon.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Crouching:</b> A  character  may  move  while </p>
<p>crouching  at  half  Pace.  He  may  run  while </p>
<p>crouched  (halve  his  total  Pace  after  rolling  for </p>
<p>running).  Ranged  attacks  against  him  suffer  a </p>
<p>–1 penalty.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Going Prone: </b>A character may fall prone at </p>
<p>any time during his action. Getting up costs 2” of </p>
<p>movement. See Prone on page 85 for the benefits </p>
<p>of being prone.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Difficult  Ground:</b>  Difficult  ground  such  as </p>
<p>mud, steep hills, or snow, slows characters down. </p>
<p>Count each inch of difficult ground as two inches </p>
<p>for purposes of movement.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Jumping:</b> A character can jump 1” horizontally </p>
<p>from a dead stop, or up to 2” with a “run and go.” </p>
<p>A successful Strength roll grants one extra inch </p>
<p>of distance.</p>
<p>Running</p>
<p>A  character  may  run  an </p>
<p>a d d i t i o n a l </p>
<p>1d6”  during  his  turn  if  he </p>
<p>wishes. Characters suffer a –2 </p>
<p>penalty (the standard multi-</p>
<p>action  penalty)  to  all </p>
<p>other actions made </p>
<p>while running.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Group  Running  Rolls: </b> When  rolling  for </p>
<p>a  group  of  nonplayer  characters,  villains,  or </p>
<p>monsters,  the  GM  or  controlling  player  makes </p>
<p>a  single  running  roll. The  whole  group  doesn’t </p>
<p>actually <i>have to </i>run—it’s just a convenient way </p>
<p>to save a little time in the heat of battle.</p>
<p>Actions</p>
<p>Characters  perform “actions” when their  card </p>
<p>comes  up  each  round. A  character  can  move </p>
<p>(see  Movement)  and  perform  one  regular </p>
<p>action—attacking, running, casting a spell, and </p>
<p>so on—without penalty.</p>
<p>Characters can perform a multitude of actions </p>
<p>when their card comes up in combat. The most </p>
<p>common actions are making Tests of Will, using a </p>
<p>power, or attacking with the Fighting or Shooting </p>
<p>skill. These are all covered on the following pages.</p>
<p>Simpler  actions  such  as  readying  an  item, </p>
<p>drawing a sword, or other quick tasks usually take </p>
<p>one action. More complex actions, such as lighting </p>
<p>a torch, digging through a backpack to find a small </p>
<p>item, and so on, might require a random amount </p>
<p>of time (such as 1d6 rounds). The Game Master </p>
<p>has the final say.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Readying  Weapons:</b>  Drawing  a  weapon </p>
<p>usually takes an entire round, but a character can </p>
<p>do it faster if she wants. This is an action, however, </p>
<p>and so inflicts the standard multi-action penalty of </p>
<p>–2 to the character’s attack roll.</p>
<p>Drawing  two  weapons  at  once,  drawing  a </p>
<p>weapon from a difficult location (such as an ankle </p>
<p>holster or inside a coat), or drawing a large or </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--75</p>
<p>Game.Rules</p>
<p>unwieldy  weapon  (a  rifle,  a  shotgun,  etc.),  still </p>
<p>inflicts a single –2 penalty, but requires an Agility </p>
<p>roll. If the roll is failed, the weapons are drawn but </p>
<p>the character may not attack that round.</p>
<p>Multiple.Actions</p>
<p>Characters may perform multiple actions such </p>
<p>as Intimidating someone while blasting away with </p>
<p>a shotgun, running and Fighting, attacking with a </p>
<p>weapon in each hand, and so on. Two important </p>
<p>rules apply:</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>A character can’t fire more than his weapon’s </p>
<p>Rate of Fire in one round</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>A character may not make more than one </p>
<p>Fighting  attack  with  the  same  weapon  in </p>
<p>one round</p>
<p>In essence, a hero may not perform the same </p>
<p>action  twice  in  a  round—he  can’t  make  two </p>
<p>simultaneous Intimidation rolls or cast two different </p>
<p>spells. He could make a Fighting and a Shooting </p>
<p>attack if he had a gun in one hand and a knife in </p>
<p>the other, however, and could even issue a Taunt </p>
<p>at the same time. He could only make two Fighting </p>
<p>attacks if he had a knife in each hand, however </p>
<p>(or had the Frenzy Edge).</p>
<p>Each  additional  action  attempted  in  a  round </p>
<p>subtracts  2  from  all  the  hero’s  rolls.  If  an </p>
<p>adventurer  wants  to  fire  a  gun  with  one  hand </p>
<p>and slice at an adjacent foe with a sword in the </p>
<p>other, for instance, he subtracts 2 from both rolls. </p>
<p>If he also wanted to make a Test of Wills against </p>
<p>someone at the same time, he subtracts 4 from </p>
<p>all his rolls.</p>
<p>Wild Cards get their Wild Die on each action </p>
<p>as usual.</p>
<p>A moving character may perform these actions </p>
<p>at different points in his movement, but still suffers </p>
<p>the multi-action penalty. This might seem a little </p>
<p>odd in practice—a character might shoot a pistol, </p>
<p>move a few inches, then issue a Taunt—suffering </p>
<p>a –2 penalty to both even though the Taunt roll </p>
<p>has yet to be resolved—so make sure the player is </p>
<p>committed to both actions before resolving either.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Free Actions:</b> Some minor actions are “free” </p>
<p>and don’t inflict multi-action penalties. Speaking </p>
<p>a  short  sentence  or  two,  moving  up  to  the </p>
<p>character’s Pace, falling prone, resisting opposed </p>
<p>rolls, or dropping an item are all examples of free </p>
<p>actions.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>One Wild Die Per Action:</b> When Wild Cards </p>
<p>roll multiple dice for a single action, such as when </p>
<p>firing a machine gun, they roll only one Wild Die. </p>
<p>A warrior with the Frenzy Edge, for example, rolls </p>
<p>two Fighting dice and one Wild Die. He can use </p>
<p>the Wild Die’s total to replace either of his Fighting </p>
<p>dice  if  he  chooses. The  Wild  Die  must  either </p>
<p>replace one of the regular dice or be ignored—it </p>
<p>never adds another action or attack to the roll.</p>
<p>Attacks</p>
<p>The heart of <i>Savage Worlds</i> is its fast, furious </p>
<p>combat. Here’s everything you need to know to </p>
<p>decimate your foes and keep your hero alive.</p>
<p>Melee.Attacks</p>
<p>The Target Number to hit an opponent is equal to </p>
<p>the opponent’s Parry score (2 plus half his Fighting </p>
<p>ability; that’s a 2 if he has no Fighting skill).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Bonus  Damage: </b> If  your  attack  hits  with  a </p>
<p>raise,  add  +1d6  to  your  damage  total  as  well! </p>
<p>The d6 may Ace just like any other damage roll.</p>
<p>Ranged.Attacks</p>
<p>The Shooting skill covers everything from pistols </p>
<p>to rocket launchers. The base TN to hit something </p>
<p>at Short range is 4 as usual. Shots at Medium </p>
<p>range subtract 2 from the Shooting roll, and shots </p>
<p>at Long range subtract 4 from the roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Bonus Damage: </b>If you hit your target with a </p>
<p>raise, add +1d6 to the damage total. This roll may </p>
<p>Ace just like any other damage roll.</p>
<p>Range.Modifiers</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>Range </b></p>
<p><b>Modifier</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>Short </p>
<p>—</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Medium </p>
<p>–2</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Long </p>
<p>–4</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Rate of Fire:</b> The Rate of Fire is how many </p>
<p>Shooting dice the character rolls when firing the </p>
<p>weapon. These additional shots must be taken at </p>
<p>the same time, and each point of RoF grants the </p>
<p>hero another Shooting die. A pistol with a RoF of </p>
<p>2, for example, gives a character two Shooting </p>
<p>dice, which may be aimed at two different targets.</p>
<p>A submachine gun with a RoF of 3, for example, </p>
<p>allows the player to roll up to three Shooting dice </p>
<p>at  once  at  up  to  three  different  targets. These </p>
<p>shots can be split among all possible targets as </p>
<p>the player desires, but must all be taken at the </p>
<p>same time. A shooter with an Uzi can’t fire one </p>
<p>shot, then move and fire two more, for instance.</p>
<p>Wild Cards roll one Wild Die with the Shooting </p>
<p>roll, and can use it in place of one of the Shooting </p>
<p>dice if they choose.</p>
<p><i><b>Example: </b> A  special  agent  fires  a </i></p>
<p><i>submachine  gun  (RoF  3)  at  two  terrorists. </i></p>
<p><i>The  agent  is  a  Wild  Card  with  a  Shooting </i></p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--76</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p><i>of d8, so he rolls 3d8 plus a Wild Die (d6). </i></p>
<p><i>The player decides to put two shots into the </i></p>
<p><i>closest terrorist and one at the further target. </i></p>
<p><i>He rolls the two at the closest terrorist first, </i></p>
<p><i>then rolls a third d8 for the second. He also </i></p>
<p><i>rolls his Wild Die.</i></p>
<p><i>He scores a hit on each with his Shooting </i></p>
<p><i>dice and misses with the third, but his Wild </i></p>
<p><i>Die  hit  so  he  replaces  one  of  the  failed </i></p>
<p><i>Shooting dice with it and gets his maximum </i></p>
<p><i>three hits (since that was his RoF).</i></p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Firing  Blind:</b>  Sometimes  a  character  may </p>
<p>want  to  fire  at  targets  they  have  no  fix  on </p>
<p>whatsoever.  In  these  cases,  allow  the  attacker </p>
<p>to make a Shooting roll at –4 as above. Should </p>
<p>he hit, the target may make a simple Stealth roll </p>
<p>to ignore the attack (it misses).The Stealth roll is </p>
<p>made at –2 if the attack was made with a RoF of </p>
<p>3 or more.</p>
<p>Attack.Modifiers</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Cover:</b> Attackers  suffer  a  penalty  when </p>
<p>attempting to hit a target behind cover:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Light  Cover: </b> Characters  subtract  1  from </p>
<p>their attack rolls if half or less of their target </p>
<p>is obscured.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Medium Cover: </b>The penalty is increased to </p>
<p>–2 if more than half of the target is hidden from </p>
<p>view. This is the usual penalty for attacking a </p>
<p>prone character (see <b>Prone</b>).</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Heavy Cover: </b>The penalty is –4 if only a small </p>
<p>part of the target is visible (prone beside a tree, </p>
<p>behind a high wall, peeking around the corner </p>
<p>of a building, etc.).</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Near Total Cover:</b> Attacking through a very </p>
<p>tight opening that provides near total cover, </p>
<p>such as an arrow slit, subtracts 6 from enemy </p>
<p>attack rolls.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Illumination:</b> Attacking  a  poorly-illuminated </p>
<p>target is more difficult than a similar attack with </p>
<p>good lighting, and incurs the following penalties:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Dim:  Twilight,  light  fog,  night  with  a </b></p>
<p><b>full  moon,  and  so  on  subtract  1  from </b></p>
<p><b>combatants’ attack rolls.</b></p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Dark:</b> Normal darkness with some ambient light </p>
<p>(starlight, partial moon) inflicts a –2 penalty, and </p>
<p>targets aren’t visible outside of 10”.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Pitch Darkness: </b>Targets aren’t visible at all </p>
<p>in pitch blackness, but if a character knows </p>
<p>roughly where a victim is (he can hear him, </p>
<p>target is in a confined space, a glint of light </p>
<p>shines off his blade, etc.), he may be attacked </p>
<p>at –4.</p>
<p>Damage</p>
<p>After a successful close combat or ranged hit, </p>
<p>the attacker rolls damage. Ranged weapons do </p>
<p>fixed damage as listed in the Gear section. Most </p>
<p>pistols, for example, cause 2d6 damage.</p>
<p>Hand  weapons  cause  damage  equal  to  the </p>
<p>attacker’s Strength die plus a second die, which </p>
<p>depends on the weapon. A barbarian with a d12 </p>
<p>Strength  and  a  long  sword  (d8  damage)  rolls </p>
<p>d12+d8 damage. Even though Strength is used </p>
<p>to determine melee damage, this isn’t a Trait roll. </p>
<p>Wild Cards don’t add a Wild Die to the roll and </p>
<p>they can’t spend Bennies if they’re unhappy with </p>
<p>the results.</p>
<p>All damage rolls can Ace.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Unarmed  Combat  Damage:</b> An  unarmed </p>
<p>combatant rolls only his Strength die.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Bonus  Damage:</b>  Well-placed  attacks  are </p>
<p>more  likely  to  hit  vital  areas,  and  so  do  more </p>
<p>damage. If your hero gets a raise on his attack roll </p>
<p>(regardless of how <i>many</i> raises),<i> </i>he adds +1d6 to </p>
<p>the final total. This roll may also Ace!</p>
<p>Applying.Damage</p>
<p>The damage of an attack is compared to the </p>
<p>victim’s Toughness just like a Trait roll (though it </p>
<p>isn’t one so you can’t spend a Benny on it). With </p>
<p>a success, the victim is Shaken. For each raise </p>
<p>over his Toughness he suffers a wound as well, </p>
<p>as shown below:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Success:</b>  The  character  is  Shaken.  If  he </p>
<p>was already Shaken, he suffers a wound and </p>
<p>remains Shaken. To cause a wound, the latter </p>
<p>Shaken  result  must  come  from  a  physical </p>
<p>attack  of  some  kind—not  a Test  of  Wills  or </p>
<p>other maneuver.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Raise:</b>  The  character  suffers  a  wound  for </p>
<p>every raise on the damage roll, and is Shaken. </p>
<p>(When wounds are caused, it doesn’t matter if </p>
<p>the victim was already Shaken beforehand.)</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b> A barbarian hits an ogre with a </i></p>
<p><i>Toughness of 11. The barbarian’s friend, a </i></p>
<p><i>rogue, has already taunted the beast (a Test </i></p>
<p><i>of Wills, see page 86) and Shaken it.</i></p>
<p><i>If the barbarian’s damage is 11-14, that’s a </i></p>
<p><i>success and would normally Shake the ogre. </i></p>
<p><i>Since it’s already Shaken, he suffers a wound </i></p>
<p><i>and remains Shaken.</i></p>
<p><i>If the barbarian’s damage is 15+—a raise or </i></p>
<p><i>more—he’d simply cause wounds. The ogre </i></p>
<p><i>is already Shaken, and since a wound was </i></p>
<p><i>caused, there’s no further effect.</i></p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--77</p>
<p>Game.Rules</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.Up,.Down,.or.Off.the.Table</p>
<p><i>One  of  the  core  principles  of  Savage </i></p>
<p><i>Worlds is that figures are up, down, or off </i></p>
<p><i>the table. This assumes you’re using figures, </i></p>
<p><i>but the concept applies even if you’re not. </i></p>
<p><i>Up, down, or off the table means the Game </i></p>
<p><i>Master  can  have  dozens  of  actors  (allies, </i></p>
<p><i>enemies, etc.) and easily keep track of them </i></p>
<p><i>all because those that are up are fine and </i></p>
<p><i>operating normally, those that are down are </i></p>
<p><i>Shaken,  and  those  who  are  Incapacitated </i></p>
<p><i>are removed. Understanding that may help </i></p>
<p><i>you better understand why the Shaken rules </i></p>
<p><i>work the way they do. </i></p>
<p><i>Of course the Game Master shouldn’t fill </i></p>
<p><i>the table with miniatures simply because he </i></p>
<p><i>can. Instead, the ability to handle such large </i></p>
<p><i>fights is a tool to let the player characters </i></p>
<p><i>adopt  different  strategies  than  they  would </i></p>
<p><i>in most games where this just isn’t practical. </i></p>
<p><i>Want to recruit a warband to help you take </i></p>
<p><i>down that orc horde? No problem! Want to </i></p>
<p><i>add several platoons of tanks to help storm </i></p>
<p><i>that enemy trench? Can do! One of our most </i></p>
<p><i>infamous convention scenarios is a dozen </i></p>
<p><i>tanks and 50 soldiers per side supporting a </i></p>
<p><i>raid by the player characters—all handled </i></p>
<p><i>in about two hours with roleplaying, puzzle-</i></p>
<p><i>solving, and a massive combat. The power </i></p>
<p><i>and flexibility to do that, while still maintaining </i></p>
<p><i>a high level of character customization and </i></p>
<p><i>detail, is one of the features we think makes </i></p>
<p><i>Savage Worlds so special.</i></p>
<p>Damage.Effects</p>
<p>Damage  can  result  in  three  effects:  Shaken, </p>
<p>wounds, and Incapacitation.</p>
<p>Shaken</p>
<p>If the damage of an attack is a simple success </p>
<p>(0-3 points over Toughness), the target is Shaken. </p>
<p>Shaken  characters  are  rattled,  distracted,  or </p>
<p>momentarily  shocked. They  aren’t  stunned  but </p>
<p>are temporarily suppressed enough that they must </p>
<p>make a Spirit roll to be effective.</p>
<p>On  their  action,  a  Shaken  character  must </p>
<p>attempt to recover from being Shaken by making </p>
<p>a Spirit roll:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Failure:</b> The character remains Shaken. He </p>
<p>can only perform free actions (see page 75).</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Success:</b> The character is no longer Shaken, </p>
<p>but can still only perform free actions.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Raise:</b> The character is no longer Shaken and </p>
<p>may act normally.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Spending  Bennies:</b> A  player  may  spend  a </p>
<p>Benny at any time to remove his Shaken status. If </p>
<p>it’s currently his action, he may act as if he gained </p>
<p>a raise on the Spirit roll.</p>
<p>Wounds</p>
<p>Every raise on the damage roll inflicts a wound.</p>
<p>Wounded Extras are removed from play. They’re </p>
<p>dead, injured, or otherwise out of the fight.</p>
<p>Wild  Cards  can  take  three  wounds  and  still </p>
<p>function. If another wound would be caused after </p>
<p>that, they’re Incapacitated (see below). Wild Cards </p>
<p>never have more than three wounds—anything </p>
<p>beyond that is just considered three wounds and </p>
<p>Incapacitated (see below).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Wound Penalties: </b>Each wound a Wild Card </p>
<p>suffers causes a –1 cumulative penalty to his Pace </p>
<p>(minimum of 1) and to all further Trait tests—up to </p>
<p>the maximum of a hero’s 3 wounds. A hero with 2 </p>
<p>wounds, for example, suffers a –2 penalty to his </p>
<p>Pace and any Trait tests.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Timing:</b> Characters sometimes take multiple </p>
<p>hits  on  the  same Action  Card.  Resolve  each </p>
<p>damage  roll  separately  and  completely  before </p>
<p>moving on to the next (including any Soak rolls).</p>
<p>Incapacitation</p>
<p>Incapacitated  characters  aren’t  necessarily </p>
<p>dead,  but  are  generally  too  beaten,  battered, </p>
<p>or bruised to do anything useful. They may not </p>
<p>perform actions and are not dealt Action Cards </p>
<p>in  combat.  Incapacitated  Extras  are  removed </p>
<p>from play. </p>
<p>Wild Cards are Incapacitated if they suffer more </p>
<p>than three wounds (cumulatively or all at once). </p>
<p>When a Wild Card becomes Incapacitated, make </p>
<p>an immediate Vigor roll:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Total of 1 or Less:</b> The character dies.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Failure:</b> Roll on the Injury Table. The Injury </p>
<p>is permanent and the victim is Bleeding Out </p>
<p>(see below).</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Success:</b> Roll on the Injury Table. The Injury </p>
<p>goes away when all wounds are healed.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Raise:</b>  Roll  on  the  Injury Table. The  Injury </p>
<p>goes away in 24 hours, or when all wounds </p>
<p>are healed.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--78</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Injury.Table</p>
<p> <b> 2d6 </b></p>
<p><b>Wound</b></p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>2 </b></p>
<p><b>Unmentionables: </b>If the injury is </p>
<p>permanent, reproduction is out of the </p>
<p>question without miracle surgery or </p>
<p>magic. There is no other effect from </p>
<p>this result.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>3-4 </b></p>
<p><b>Arm:</b> Roll left or right arm randomly; it’s </p>
<p>unusable like the One Arm Hindrance </p>
<p>(though if the primary arm is affected, </p>
<p>off-hand penalties still apply to the </p>
<p>other).</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>5-9 </b></p>
<p><b>Guts:</b> Your hero catches one </p>
<p>somewhere between the crotch and </p>
<p>the chin. Roll 1d6: </p>
<p><i> </i></p>
<p><i> </i></p>
<p><i> 1-2 </i></p>
<p><i>Broken:</i> Agility reduced a die </p>
<p>type (minimum d4).</p>
<p><i> </i></p>
<p><i> </i></p>
<p><i> 3-4 </i></p>
<p><i>Battered:</i> Vigor reduced a die </p>
<p>type (minimum d4).</p>
<p><i> </i></p>
<p><i> </i></p>
<p><i> 5-6 </i></p>
<p><i>Busted:</i> Strength reduced a die </p>
<p>type (minimum d4).</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>10 </b></p>
<p><b>Leg:</b> Gain the Lame Hindrance (or the </p>
<p>One Leg Hindrance if already Lame).</p>
<p><b> 11-12 </b></p>
<p><b>Head:</b> A grievous injury to the head. </p>
<p>Roll 1d6: </p>
<p><i> </i></p>
<p><i> </i></p>
<p><i>  1-2 </i></p>
<p><i>Hideous Scar: </i>Your hero now </p>
<p>has the Ugly Hindrance.</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>  <i>3-4 </i></p>
<p><i>Blinded:</i> An eye is damaged. </p>
<p>Gain the One Eye Hindrance (or the </p>
<p>Blind Hindrance if he only had one </p>
<p>good eye).</p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p> <i> 5-6 </i></p>
<p><i>Brain Damage:</i> Massive trauma </p>
<p>to the head. Smarts reduced one die </p>
<p>type (min d4).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Bleeding  Out:</b> The  injured  character  must </p>
<p>make a Vigor roll at the start of each round after </p>
<p>the one in which he was injured and before Action </p>
<p>Cards are dealt:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Success:</b>  The  victim  must  roll  again  next </p>
<p>round,  or  every  minute  thereafter  if  not  in </p>
<p>combat.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Raise:</b> The  victim  stabilizes  and  no  further </p>
<p>rolls are required.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Failure:</b> The character dies from blood loss. </p>
<p>Other characters may stop a victim’s bleeding </p>
<p>by making a Healing roll. If successful, the victim </p>
<p>stabilizes immediately and no further rolls are </p>
<p>required. This use of the Healing skill just stops </p>
<p>the bleeding. See page 87 for the use of the </p>
<p>Healing skill to recover actual wounds.</p>
<p>Soak.Rolls</p>
<p>A  character  may  spend  a  Benny  to  make  a </p>
<p>“Soak”  roll,  which  is  a  Vigor  check. A  success </p>
<p>and each raise reduces the number of wounds </p>
<p>suffered from that attack by one.</p>
<p>If the character Soaks <i>all</i> of the wounds from </p>
<p>an  attack,  he  removes  his  Shaken  condition </p>
<p>too (even from a previous source). Don’t count </p>
<p>the wound  modifiers  he’s <i>about</i> to suffer when </p>
<p>making this roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Timing: </b>A character may only make one Soak </p>
<p>roll  per  attack.  If  a  Soak  roll  eliminates  2  of  3 </p>
<p>wounds, for instance, a hero can’t make another </p>
<p>Soak roll to eliminate the third wound. (The hero </p>
<p><i>could</i> spend a second Benny to reroll the Vigor </p>
<p>roll as usual, however.)</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Shaken:</b> A character can also spend a Benny </p>
<p>to immediately eliminate a Shaken condition (and </p>
<p>act  if  it’s  his  action). This  can  be  done  at  any </p>
<p>time—even just after a failed Spirit roll to recover.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--79</p>
<p>Situational.Combat.Rules</p>
<p>Below  are  a  number  of  rules  for  special </p>
<p>maneuvers  characters  might  perform  during </p>
<p>furious combat.</p>
<p>Aim</p>
<p>A character who spends a full round aiming (no </p>
<p>movement allowed) may add +2 to his Shooting </p>
<p>or Throwing  roll  in  the  following  round  versus </p>
<p>whatever he aimed at (a person, vehicle, etc.). </p>
<p>Aiming for multiple rounds has no extra effect.</p>
<p>Area.Effect.Attacks</p>
<p>Grenades, spell effects, and other attacks that </p>
<p>cover a large area are “area effect attacks.” The </p>
<p>three most common size attacks have been made </p>
<p>into Small, Medium, and Large Burst Templates, </p>
<p>found on page 180 and on our website.</p>
<p>To  attack  with  an  area  effect  weapon,  the </p>
<p>character  places  the  template  on  the  table  (or </p>
<p>picks where he wants the center of the blast to </p>
<p>be) and makes a Shooting or Throwing roll. If the </p>
<p>attack is successful, the blast is centered where </p>
<p>desired. Everything under (or partially under) the </p>
<p>template is affected, rolling damage separately </p>
<p>for each Wild Card or group of Extras.</p>
<p>Failure means the blast deviates. Just how far </p>
<p>depends on whether it was thrown or launched, </p>
<p>and what range bracket the target was in (Short, </p>
<p>Medium, or Long). Roll 1d6” for thrown weapons </p>
<p>(such as grenades) and 1d10” for fired projectiles. </p>
<p>Multiply by 1 for Short range, 2 for Medium, and </p>
<p>3 for Long.</p>
<p>Next roll a d12 and read it like a clock facing </p>
<p>to determine the direction the missile deviates. </p>
<p>A weapon can never deviate more than half the </p>
<p>distance to the original target. That keeps things </p>
<p>from going behind the thrower.</p>
<p>Targets  use  their  lowest  armor  value  against </p>
<p>area effect damage—armor must cover the entire </p>
<p>body without gaps to provide any protection at all.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Cover: </b> Targets  who  are  prone  or  behind </p>
<p>cover still get some protection from area effect </p>
<p>attacks. In these cases, the modifier they would </p>
<p>normally receive against ranged attacks acts as </p>
<p>that many points of Armor instead. A character in </p>
<p>major cover, like a foxhole, negates four points of </p>
<p>damage from a blast if he’s caught within it.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Diving  for  Cover:</b> Thrown  weapons  with  a </p>
<p>blast effect (such as grenades) and artillery allow </p>
<p>potential  targets  a  chance  to  move  out  of  the </p>
<p>area of effect. Give targets who saw the danger </p>
<p>coming an Agility roll at –2 to jump out of the way </p>
<p>and avoid the damage. If successful, move the </p>
<p>character  just  outside  the  template  (his  choice </p>
<p>exactly where). Grenades can be thrown back as </p>
<p>well (see Grenades on page 57).</p>
<p>Automatic.Fire</p>
<p>Automatic  weapons  (those  with  an  RoF  of </p>
<p>2  or  higher)  fire  much  faster—and  inherently </p>
<p>differently—than regular firearms.</p>
<p>To attack with a full-auto weapon, roll a number </p>
<p>of Shooting dice equal to the weapon’s Rate of </p>
<p>Fire. Compare each die separately to the Target </p>
<p>Number to see if it hit. If the weapon has a RoF of </p>
<p>3, for example, a Wild Card rolls three Shooting </p>
<p>dice  plus  a  Wild  Die  and  uses  the  best  three </p>
<p>results.</p>
<p>Note that Wild Cards roll their Shooting dice plus </p>
<p>a Wild Die. They still can’t hit with more shots than </p>
<p>the weapon’s Rate of Fire, however.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Recoil:</b>  Fully  automatic  fire  is  typically </p>
<p>inaccurate because of the recoil between each </p>
<p>shot. Subtract 2 from the Shooting roll when firing </p>
<p>full-auto (ignore the penalty when firing a single </p>
<p>shot with such a weapon).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Full-Auto  and  Ammo: </b> This  system  is </p>
<p>somewhat abstract so that we don’t have to roll </p>
<p>dice for every single bullet (though we treat them </p>
<p>as  such  for  game  purposes). This  means  that </p>
<p>each die rolled on full-auto represents a number </p>
<p>of bullets equal to the weapon’s Rate of Fire when </p>
<p>counting  ammo,  even  though  only  one  “bullet” </p>
<p>can hit and cause damage from that die. Firing </p>
<p>all three dice with a Thompson gun (Rate of Fire </p>
<p>3), for example, consumes 9 bullets.</p>
<p>Most automatic weapons can be set to fire full-</p>
<p>auto or single shot. If a weapon has selectable </p>
<p>automatic use (Auto), you can fire a single shot </p>
<p>(and also ignore the full-auto penalty of –2).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Suppressive  Fire:</b>  Instead  of  attacking </p>
<p>specific targets, characters with fully  automatic </p>
<p>weapons can “spray” an area with lead in hopes </p>
<p>of  killing  or  suppressing  a  larger  number  of </p>
<p>victims. To suppress an area, the attacker places </p>
<p>the  Medium  Burst Template  on  the  battlefield </p>
<p>and makes a single Shooting roll (regardless of </p>
<p>the weapon’s Rate of Fire). Include the standard </p>
<p>modifiers  for  range,  the  full-auto  penalty,  and </p>
<p>any other miscellaneous factors, but ignore the </p>
<p>target’s modifiers if any (such as being prone or </p>
<p>in cover—these come into play in another way as </p>
<p>you’ll see below). If the attack misses, the spray </p>
<p>is off-target and has no effect.</p>
<p>If the attack is successful, all possible targets </p>
<p>within  the  area  make  Spirit  rolls,  adding  any </p>
<p>cover modifiers they would normally have against </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--80</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>ranged  attacks  to  this  roll. Those  who  fail  are </p>
<p>Shaken. Those  who  roll  a  1  on  their  Spirit  die </p>
<p>(regardless of any Wild Dice) are actually hit by </p>
<p>the attack and suffer damage normally.</p>
<p>Suppressive fire uses five times the weapon’s </p>
<p>Rate  of  Fire  in  bullets. A  weapon  with  a  Rate </p>
<p>of  Fire  of  3,  for  example,  uses  15  bullets  for </p>
<p>suppressive fire.</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b>  Buck  and  Virginia,  two  pulp-</i></p>
<p><i>era adventurers, are escaping from ancient </i></p>
<p><i>ruins in a stolen biplane. Suddenly, they’re </i></p>
<p><i>swarmed  by  an  evil  alligator  shaman  and </i></p>
<p><i>his brainwashed minions. Virginia spins the </i></p>
<p><i>plane’s  Maxim  gun  around  and  fires.  She </i></p>
<p><i>uses suppressive fire to slow them down. </i></p>
<p><i>She places a Medium Burst Template 16” </i></p>
<p><i>away—that’s Medium Range for the Maxim—</i></p>
<p><i>and rolls her Shooting. She gets a 13, –2 for </i></p>
<p><i>full-auto,  –2  for  an  unstable  platform  (the </i></p>
<p><i>plane), and –2 for Medium range, for a total </i></p>
<p><i>of 7. Success! The tribesmen in the template </i></p>
<p><i>must roll their Spirit or be Shaken. Those who </i></p>
<p><i>make it charge on through, but those who </i></p>
<p><i>roll a 1 are hit!</i></p>
<p>Breaking.Things</p>
<p>Occasionally  a  character  may  want  to  break </p>
<p>something, such as a weapon, a lock, or a door. </p>
<p>Use the Toughness values below for these kinds </p>
<p>of objects. Use these rules for solid objects. Larger </p>
<p>objects with many components (such as vehicles) </p>
<p>take multiple hits as per the vehicle rules.</p>
<p>Most  anything  can  be  broken  given  enough </p>
<p>time  and  effort,  so  use  this  system  only  when </p>
<p>attempting  to  break  things  in  a  hurry  (such  as </p>
<p>during combat rounds).</p>
<p>The  Parry  of  an  inanimate  object  is  2.  The </p>
<p>catch  is  that  damage  rolls  against  them  don’t </p>
<p>count  bonuses  from  raises  on  the  attack  roll, </p>
<p>nor Aces.  Unlike  a  person  or  even  a  vehicle, </p>
<p>an attack cannot hit a “vital” area on a lock or </p>
<p>a door and thus do more damage. If an attack </p>
<p>can’t do enough damage to destroy an object, it </p>
<p>can’t destroy it (at least not quickly). This keeps </p>
<p>characters from shattering swords with a feather </p>
<p>and a lucky Strength roll.</p>
<p>If  the  damage  roll  equals  or  exceeds  the </p>
<p>object’s Toughness, it’s broken, bent, shattered, </p>
<p>or otherwise ruined. The GM decides the exact </p>
<p>effects—such as whether a good strike opens a </p>
<p>hole in a door or knocks it off its hinges.</p>
<p>See <b>Obstacles</b> to attack <i>through</i> objects.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--81</p>
<p>Game.Rules</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Damage Types: </b>After the type of Object and </p>
<p>its Toughness is the type of damage that can affect </p>
<p>it. Swords do cutting or piercing damage, spears </p>
<p>are  piercing  weapons,  and  so  on.  Bullets  are </p>
<p>considered piercing weapons, though shotguns </p>
<p>do blunt damage at close range for the purpose </p>
<p>of this table.</p>
<p>The  type  of  damage  is  important  for  objects </p>
<p>because shooting a single bullet through a door, </p>
<p>for instance, may penetrate it, but won’t destroy </p>
<p>it. Only a blunt or cutting attack is likely to destroy </p>
<p>a door in one shot.</p>
<p>Object.Toughness</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>Object </b></p>
<p><b>Toughness </b></p>
<p><b>Damage Type</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>Light Door </p>
<p>8 </p>
<p>Blunt, Cutting</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Heavy Door </p>
<p>10 </p>
<p>Blunt, Cutting</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Lock </p>
<p>8 </p>
<p>Blunt, Piercing</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Handcuffs </p>
<p>12 </p>
