<p>Blunt, Piercing, </p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>  Cutting</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Knife, Sword </p>
<p>10 </p>
<p>Blunt, Cutting</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Rope </p>
<p>4 </p>
<p>Cutting, Piercing</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Small Shield </p>
<p>8 </p>
<p>Blunt, Cutting</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Medium Shield </p>
<p>10 </p>
<p>Blunt, Cutting</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Large Shield </p>
<p>12 </p>
<p>Blunt, Cutting</p>
<p>Called.Shots</p>
<p>Use the following modifiers and effects when </p>
<p>characters wish to target specific locations:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Limb  (–2): </b> An  attack  to  a  limb  causes  no </p>
<p>additional  damage  but  may  ignore  armor </p>
<p>or  have  some  other  special  effect  (see  the </p>
<p><b>Disarm </b>maneuver).</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Head or Vitals (–4):</b> The attacker gains +4 </p>
<p>damage  from  a  successful  attack  to  these </p>
<p>critical areas. The target must actually have </p>
<p>vital areas, and the attacker must know where </p>
<p>they are to gain this advantage.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Small  Target  (–4):</b> Attacks  against  small </p>
<p>targets such as the heart of a vampire or a </p>
<p>missing scale on a large dragon’s chest are </p>
<p>made at –4. The effect of success depends on </p>
<p>the situation—the vampire might die instantly, </p>
<p>the missing scale may mean the dragon gets </p>
<p>no armor, etc. If the GM has no particular effect </p>
<p>in mind, it adds +4 damage just like a shot to </p>
<p>the head or vitals.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Tiny Target (–6):</b> Particularly small or narrow </p>
<p>targets,  such  as  the  eye-slit  of  a  knight’s </p>
<p>helmet, carry a –6 modifier. The effects of a </p>
<p>hit depend on the target. In the case of the </p>
<p>knight, the blow ignores armor and inflicts +4 </p>
<p>damage because it’s a head shot (as above).</p>
<p>Defend</p>
<p>If a character’s only regular action is to defend, </p>
<p>his  Parry  is  increased  by  +2  until  his  next </p>
<p>action. The defender may move normally while </p>
<p>performing this maneuver, but no running or other </p>
<p>actions are allowed.</p>
<p>Disarm</p>
<p>A character can try to make an opponent drop </p>
<p>a  weapon  (or  other  object)  with  either  a  close </p>
<p>combat or a ranged attack. To cause a disarm </p>
<p>check, the attacker must first hit the opponent’s </p>
<p>arm (–2, see <b>Called Shots</b>). The defender must </p>
<p>then make a Strength roll. If the roll is less than </p>
<p>the damage, he drops his weapon. The attacker </p>
<p>may choose to make this a nonlethal attack with a </p>
<p>melee weapon. Ranged attacks can be nonlethal </p>
<p>if the attacker targets the weapon instead of the </p>
<p>limb (generally –4 instead of –2).</p>
<p>Double.Taps.&amp;.Three.Round.Bursts</p>
<p>A character with a semi-automatic weapon (such </p>
<p>as a Colt .45, an M1 Carbine, or even an M16) can </p>
<p>fire two shots in one action by “double-tapping.” </p>
<p>Double tapping is a single Shooting roll that gives </p>
<p>the user +1 to hit and damage but expends two </p>
<p>rounds of ammunition.</p>
<p>Many modern automatic weapons, such as the </p>
<p>M16A2,  have  a  selector  switch  that  allows  the </p>
<p>user to go from single shot, to burst fire, to fully-</p>
<p>automatic as a free action. Burst fire, or a Three </p>
<p>Round burst, gives the user +2 to hit and damage, </p>
<p>and uses exactly three rounds of ammunition.</p>
<p>The.Drop</p>
<p>Sometimes an attacker is able to catch a foe </p>
<p>off-guard and gets “the drop” on him. This usually </p>
<p>happens  at  a  distance  of  only  a  few  feet,  but </p>
<p>other situations may occur (a sniper on a nearby </p>
<p>rooftop).</p>
<p>Only the GM can determine when one character </p>
<p>has obtained this kind of advantage over another. </p>
<p>Usually  it’s  when  the  victim  is  in  the  classic </p>
<p>hostage  pose,  is  completely  unaware  of  the </p>
<p>danger, or has been caught unarmed by an armed </p>
<p>foe. The attacker is considered on Hold and adds </p>
<p>+4 to his attack and damage rolls should he decide </p>
<p>to strike.</p>
<p>Finishing.Move</p>
<p>A  completely  helpless  victim  (bound, </p>
<p>unconscious,  etc.)  may  be  dispatched  with  a </p>
<p>lethal weapon of some sort as an action. This is </p>
<p>automatic unless the GM decides there’s a special </p>
<p>situation, such as a particularly tough or naturally </p>
<p>armored victim, a chance for escape, and so on.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--82</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>The killer must usually dispatch his foe up close </p>
<p>and personal, but the GM may occasionally let </p>
<p>finishing moves be performed at range.</p>
<p>Firing.Into.Melee</p>
<p>Occasionally heroes have to fire into the middle </p>
<p>of hand-to-hand fights. The trouble is that even </p>
<p>though we might see figures standing perfectly </p>
<p>still on the table-top, in “reality,” they’re circling </p>
<p>each other, wrestling back and forth, and moving </p>
<p>erratically. For that reason, firing into a tangle of </p>
<p>people, such as a melee, is quite dangerous. Use </p>
<p>the <b>Innocent Bystander</b> rules when this occurs </p>
<p>(see below).</p>
<p>Full.Defense</p>
<p>In  addition  to  the  usual  Defend  option,  a </p>
<p>character can go for a full defensive action. He </p>
<p>makes a Fighting roll at +2 and uses the result </p>
<p>as his Parry until his next action. This is a trait </p>
<p>test, so he gets to roll his Wild Die as well. And, </p>
<p>of course, the dice can Ace, and you can choose </p>
<p>to use Bennies on the roll if you want to.</p>
<p>Note that the character’s Parry never gets worse </p>
<p>as a result of the roll. If the roll is lower than the </p>
<p>hero’s  Parry  score,  he  keeps  that  instead  (but </p>
<p>gains no bonus from the full defense).</p>
<p>A hero using the full defense maneuver cannot </p>
<p>move at all, however. He’s doing everything he </p>
<p>can to fend off whatever is attacking him. If he </p>
<p>wants  to  move  away  as  well,  use  the  Defend </p>
<p>maneuver instead.</p>
<p>Ganging.Up</p>
<p>Ganging up on a foe allows attackers to flank, </p>
<p>exploit  openings,  and  generally  harass  their </p>
<p>outnumbered opponent. Each additional adjacent </p>
<p>foe adds +1 to all the attackers’ Fighting rolls, up to </p>
<p>a maximum of +4. If three warriors attack a single </p>
<p>hero, for example, each of the three warriors gets </p>
<p>a +2 bonus to their Fighting rolls.</p>
<p>Grappling</p>
<p>Sometimes  it’s  best  to  restrain  an  opponent </p>
<p>rather than beat him to a bloody pulp. That’s where </p>
<p>grappling comes in.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--83</p>
<p>Game.Rules</p>
<p>Grappling is an opposed Fighting roll that causes </p>
<p>no damage. If the attacker wins, he’s entangled his </p>
<p>foe. With a raise, his foe is also Shaken.</p>
<p>Once entangled, the defender may attempt to </p>
<p>break free on his next action. Both the defender </p>
<p>and attacker pick either their Strength or Agility </p>
<p>and then an opposed roll is made. If successful, </p>
<p>the defender is free but the attempt consumes </p>
<p>his action. If he does so with a raise, he’s free </p>
<p>and may act normally. Failure means he is still </p>
<p>entangled. Instead of breaking free the defender </p>
<p>may attempt a different action but at a –4 penalty.</p>
<p>After  grappling,  the  attacker  may  attempt  to </p>
<p>damage  his  victim  on  subsequent  rounds  by </p>
<p>making an opposed roll as above. On a success </p>
<p>he does his Strength in damage (gaining the extra </p>
<p>d6 for a raise as normal).</p>
<p>Improvised.Weapons</p>
<p>Heroes  often  find  themselves  fighting  with </p>
<p>objects that aren’t intended for use as weapons. </p>
<p>Torches, vases, chairs, tankards, bottles, tools, </p>
<p>and other mundane items are frequently pressed </p>
<p>into service in combat. And sometimes characters </p>
<p>find  themselves  using  existing  weapons  in </p>
<p>improvised ways, defending with a ranged weapon </p>
<p>in melee or trying to throw a hand weapon not </p>
<p>designed to be thrown.</p>
<p>Such improvised weapons cause the wielder to </p>
<p>suffer a –1 Fighting or Throwing penalty, as well as </p>
<p>–1 to Parry. The Game Master is the final judge of </p>
<p>an improvised weapon’s effectiveness.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Small Weapons:</b> Range 3/6/12, Damage </p>
<p>Str+d4, RoF 1, Min Str d4, –1 attack and </p>
<p>Parry</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Medium Weapons:</b> Range 2/4/8, Damage </p>
<p>Str+d6, RoF 1, Min Str d6, –1 attack and </p>
<p>Parry</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Large Weapons:</b> Range 1/2/4, Damage </p>
<p>Str+d8, RoF 1, Min Str d8, –1 attack and </p>
<p>Parry</p>
<p>Innocent.Bystanders</p>
<p>When  an  attacker  misses  a  Shooting  or </p>
<p>Throwing  roll,  it  may  sometimes  be  important </p>
<p>to see if any other targets in the line of fire were </p>
<p>hit. The GM should only use this rule when it’s </p>
<p>dramatically  appropriate—not  for  every  missed </p>
<p>shot in a hail of gunfire.</p>
<p>Each miss that comes up a 1 on the Shooting </p>
<p>die indicates a random adjacent character was </p>
<p>hit. If the attacker was firing on full-auto or with a </p>
<p>shotgun, a roll of 1 or 2 hits the bystander. Roll </p>
<p>damage normally.</p>
<p>Horses and other animals are possible targets </p>
<p>when firing on mounted characters as well.</p>
<p>It’s sometimes easier to hit an adjacent victim </p>
<p>than the original target using this quick system. </p>
<p>That may not be entirely realistic, but it’s fast and </p>
<p>simple, it makes large groups of people vulnerable </p>
<p>to missile fire, and best of all, increases the drama </p>
<p>of firing at opponents locked in melee with the </p>
<p>attacker’s allies.</p>
<p>Mounted.Combat</p>
<p>Characters  fighting  from  horseback  (or  other </p>
<p>strange  beasts)  have  certain  advantages  and </p>
<p>disadvantages in combat, as described below.</p>
<p>Mounts aren’t dealt Action Cards—they act with </p>
<p>their riders. Animals specifically noted as being </p>
<p>trained to fight (such as warhorses) may attack </p>
<p>any threat to their front during their riders’ action. </p>
<p>Untrained horses do not fight unless riderless, and </p>
<p>even then usually only if cornered.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Horsemanship:</b>  Characters  who  wish  to </p>
<p>fight from horseback must use the lowest of their </p>
<p>Fighting or Riding skills. This makes it important </p>
<p>for cavalrymen to actually be able to ride well!</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Collisions:</b> If a mounted character runs into </p>
<p>something solid—such as a wall—both he and </p>
<p>the mount suffer Collision Damage as explained </p>
<p>in the Vehicle Rules on page 115.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Running:</b>  Riders  suffer  the  usual  running </p>
<p>penalty (–2) to attacks if the animal runs.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Falling:</b> Anytime  a  character  is  Shaken  or </p>
<p>suffers  a  wound  while  on  horseback  he  must </p>
<p>make a Riding roll to stay horsed. If he fails, he </p>
<p>falls. If the horse is moving, the rider suffers 2d6 </p>
<p>damage (he’s merely Fatigued for the rest of the </p>
<p>fight if the horse was still).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Firing on Mounted Targets:</b> Shots directed at </p>
<p>mounted characters use the Innocent Bystander </p>
<p>rules to see if the horse was hit. Of course, an </p>
<p>attacker can always aim for the horse instead.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Charging:</b> A rider on a charging horse adds </p>
<p>+4 to his damage roll with a successful Fighting </p>
<p>attack. To be considered charging, the rider must </p>
<p>have  moved  at  least  6”  or  more  in  a  relatively </p>
<p>straight line towards his foe.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Setting Weapons:</b> A weapon with a Reach of </p>
<p>1 or greater can be “set” against a cavalry attack. </p>
<p>To do so, the attacker must be on Hold when he is </p>
<p>attacked by a charging mount (see above).</p>
<p>If  so,  he  rolls  to  interrupt  as  usual,  but  each </p>
<p>combatant adds +2 to his Agility roll for each point </p>
<p>of his weapon’s reach. The winner attacks first, </p>
<p>and adds the +4 charge bonus to his damage; the </p>
<p>loser gains no bonus.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--84</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Wounded  Mounts:</b>  When  an  animal  is </p>
<p>Shaken  or  wounded,  it  rears  or  bucks. A  rider </p>
<p>must make a Riding roll to stay mounted, or suffer </p>
<p>the consequences of falling (see above). Mounts </p>
<p>which aren’t trained in fighting flee in a random </p>
<p>direction  when  Shaken,  taking  their  riders  with </p>
<p>them.</p>
<p>Nonlethal.Damage</p>
<p>A  character  who  wants  to  beat  someone  up </p>
<p>without killing them can choose to do nonlethal </p>
<p>damage.  This  requires  the  attacker  use  only </p>
<p>his fists or a blunt weapon of some sort. Edged </p>
<p>weapons may be used if they have a flat side, but </p>
<p>this subtracts –1 from the attacker’s Fighting rolls. </p>
<p>Nonlethal  damage  causes  wounds  as  usual, </p>
<p>but if a character is rendered Incapacitated he’s </p>
<p>knocked out for 1d6 hours instead.</p>
<p>Nonlethal wounds are otherwise treated exactly </p>
<p>as lethal wounds. This means it’s much easier to </p>
<p>render an Extra unconscious than a Wild Card. </p>
<p>This is intentional, and should work well for most </p>
<p>genres where heroes can take multiple punches </p>
<p>before going down for the count, but most “mooks” </p>
<p>go out with one or two good punches. </p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b> Virginia is whacked on the head </i></p>
<p><i>by a cultist. The villain gets lucky and does </i></p>
<p><i>4 wounds to our heroine. Virginia rolls her </i></p>
<p><i>Vigor and gets a failure. Because he was </i></p>
<p><i>doing nonlethal damage, Virginia is simply </i></p>
<p><i>knocked out for 1d6 hours.</i></p>
<p>Obstacles</p>
<p>Sometimes  characters  have  sufficient  power </p>
<p>to attack their foes <i>through</i> obstacles. (See the </p>
<p><b>Breaking  Things</b>  section  to  actually  destroy </p>
<p>intervening obstacles.) To do so, first decide if the </p>
<p>attack hits. If it misses, there’s no additional effect </p>
<p>other than a small hole in the intervening obstacle.</p>
<p>If the attack would have hit without the cover </p>
<p>modifier, the round is on target but the obstacle </p>
<p>acts as armor for the target behind it. In the table </p>
<p>below are the Armor bonuses for some obstacles </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--85</p>
<p>Game.Rules</p>
<p>commonly used as cover. This is added directly to </p>
<p>the target’s Toughness, including any actual armor </p>
<p>he’s  wearing  in  the  affected  location.  Subtract </p>
<p>the weapon’s Armor Piercing value from the total </p>
<p>protection offered—not from <i>both </i>the obstacle and </p>
<p>armor actually worn by the target.</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b>  A  soldier  blasts  an  Axis </i></p>
<p><i>abomination  hiding  behind  a  stone  wall </i></p>
<p><i>(Armor +10) with a bazooka. The bazooka </i></p>
<p><i>ignores 9 points of Armor, so the wall only </i></p>
<p><i>provides 1 point of protection.</i></p>
<p>Obstacle.Toughness</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>Armor </b></p>
<p><b>Obstacle</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>+1 </p>
<p>Glass, leather</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>Plate glass window, shield</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+3 </p>
<p>Modern interior wall, sheet </p>
<p> </p>
<p> </p>
<p>  metal, car door</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+4 </p>
<p>Oak door, thick sheet metal</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+6 </p>
<p>Cinder block wall</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+8 </p>
<p>Brick wall</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+10 </p>
<p>Stone wall, bulletproof glass</p>
<p>Off-Hand.Attacks</p>
<p>Characters  are  assumed  to  be  right-handed </p>
<p>unless the player decides otherwise. Actions that </p>
<p>require precise eye-hand coordination, such as </p>
<p>Fighting or Shooting, suffer a –2 penalty when </p>
<p>done with the off-hand.</p>
<p>Prone</p>
<p>Smart heroes lie down when lead starts flying. </p>
<p>They  move,  shoot,  and  then  get  prone  behind </p>
<p>cover before their action is over, forcing attackers </p>
<p>to go on Hold to attack them. Prone gives them </p>
<p>Medium  Cover  against  most  attacks.  Ranged </p>
<p>attacks  within  3”  ignore  the  modifier  since  the </p>
<p>target is just as exposed as if he were standing </p>
<p>next to these characters. </p>
<p>If a prone defender is caught in melee, his Parry </p>
<p>is reduced by 2 and he must subtract 2 from his </p>
<p>Fighting rolls. Getting up from prone costs 2” of </p>
<p>movement.</p>
<p>Push</p>
<p>Sometimes characters may want to push a foe </p>
<p>in hopes of knocking him out of position, prone, or </p>
<p>even into a deadly hazard. This is called a Push.</p>
<p>To push a foe, the attacker and the target make </p>
<p>opposed Strength rolls. If successful, the attacker </p>
<p>has three choices. He can:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Bash:</b> Push the target 1” for a success </p>
<p>and 2” on a raise on the Strength roll. If the </p>
<p>defender hits an obstacle, he suffers 1d6 </p>
<p>damage for every 1” he would have moved.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Shield Bash:</b> If the attacker has a shield, </p>
<p>he can push the attacker as above, but also </p>
<p>causes Str damage. Add +1 to the damage </p>
<p>for a small shield, +2 for a medium shield, </p>
<p>and +3 for a large shield.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Knock Prone:</b> The defender is knocked </p>
<p>prone.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Running:</b> If the attacker ran at least 3” before </p>
<p>the push, he adds +2 to his roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Hazards:</b> The Game Master must determine </p>
<p>results from any other obstacles or hazards, such </p>
<p>as a cliff edge, wall of fire, etc.</p>
<p>Ranged.Weapons.in.Close.Combat</p>
<p>No  ranged  weapon  larger  than  a  pistol  may </p>
<p>be  fired  at  adjacent  foes  engaged  in  melee. </p>
<p>Larger weapons may be used as clubs, however. </p>
<p>Pistols can be fired in close combat, but since the </p>
<p>defender is actively fighting back, the TN for the </p>
<p>Shooting roll is his Parry rather than the standard </p>
<p>TN of 4.</p>
<p>That means it’s harder to hit someone who’s </p>
<p>wrestling  with  your  character  in  melee  than </p>
<p>someone a few feet further away who isn’t actively </p>
<p>wrestling with your hero.</p>
<p>Rapid.Attack</p>
<p>Sometimes  an  outnumbered  fighter  needs  to </p>
<p>sacrifice skill for blind luck. A rapid attack is a wild </p>
<p>swing of the blade or a hasty spray of shots that </p>
<p>favors fortune over expertise. Both the melee and </p>
<p>ranged version inflict a –2 penalty to Parry.</p>
<p>In  melee,  the  warrior  can  make  up  to  three </p>
<p>attacks as a single action. Roll a Fighting die for </p>
<p>each, and subtract –4 for the totals (Wild Cards </p>
<p>get a Wild Die as usual).</p>
<p>Ranged  attackers  with  a  semi-automatic </p>
<p>weapon or revolver may fire up to six shots at a </p>
<p>–4 penalty to each. Roll a Shooting die for each </p>
<p>shot (along with the Wild Die if the attacker is a </p>
<p>Wild Card). Single action revolvers refer to this </p>
<p>maneuver as “fanning the hammer,” and it requires </p>
<p>two hands to perform.</p>
<p>Rapid attack cannot be combined with any other </p>
<p>effect that allows attacking multiple foes in one </p>
<p>action (Sweep, Frenzy, Autofire, Two Weapons, </p>
<p>etc.), nor may it be used with a Double Tap or </p>
<p>Three Round Burst.</p>
<p>The rapid attack must be taken all at once but </p>
<p>the warrior can assign his skill dice to multiple </p>
<p>targets if he chooses.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--86</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Tests.of.Will</p>
<p>Intimidation and Taunt allow a character to make </p>
<p>a “Test of Wills” attack against an opponent. In </p>
<p>combat situations or during competitive miniature </p>
<p>battles,  Tests  of  Will  have  objective  effects, </p>
<p>as  seen  below.  More  subjective  effects  are </p>
<p>determined by the Game Master in roleplaying </p>
<p>situations.</p>
<p>To make a Test of Wills, the character makes </p>
<p>an opposed roll against his chosen target. The </p>
<p>defender uses Smarts to resist Taunt, and Spirit </p>
<p>to resist Intimidation.</p>
<p>The Game Master should modify both character’s </p>
<p>rolls depending on the situation. Waving a gun in </p>
<p>someone’s face isn’t polite, but it’s definitely worth </p>
<p>a +2 bonus to Intimidation, for example (unless </p>
<p>the target has an even bigger gun!).</p>
<p>A success means the attacker gets a +2 bonus </p>
<p>to his next action against the defender during this </p>
<p>combat. A raise on the roll gives the attacker the </p>
<p>bonus and makes the defender Shaken as well. </p>
<p>This can be a great setup for an attack, a trick, or </p>
<p>even a second Test of Wills if the first one didn’t </p>
<p>get a Shaken result.</p>
<p>Test.of.Wills.Table</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>“Attack” Skill </b></p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>Resisted By…</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>Taunt </p>
<p>vs. </p>
<p>Smarts</p>
<p> </p>
<p>Intimidation </p>
<p>vs. </p>
<p>Spirit</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b>  Buck  Savage  tries  to  Taunt  a </i></p>
<p><i>crocodile cultist by flipping his machete and </i></p>
<p><i>grinning like a hyena. He rolls his Taunt and </i></p>
<p><i>beats the warrior’s Smarts with a raise. The </i></p>
<p><i>cultist is Shaken and Buck adds +2 to his next </i></p>
<p><i>action against the spearman.</i></p>
<p>Touch.Attack</p>
<p>A character who simply wants to touch a foe </p>
<p>(usually to deliver a magical effect of some kind) </p>
<p>may add +2 to his Fighting roll.</p>
<p>Tricks</p>
<p>Heroes  often  attempt  fancy  maneuvers  or </p>
<p>clever tricks to distract their foes and set them up </p>
<p>for deadly follow-up attacks. This might include </p>
<p>throwing  sand  in  an  opponent’s  eyes,  ducking </p>
<p>between a tall foe’s legs to stab him in the back, </p>
<p>and so on. Tricks do not include weapon feints—</p>
<p>those  are  already  “assumed”  in  a  character’s </p>
<p>Fighting and Parry scores.</p>
<p>To  perform  the  trick,  the  player  must  first </p>
<p>describe exactly what his character is doing. Next </p>
<p>he makes an opposed Agility or Smarts roll against </p>
<p>his foe. The GM must determine which is more </p>
<p>appropriate based on the player’s description of </p>
<p>the maneuver.</p>
<p>If the character is successful, his opponent is </p>
<p>distracted  and  suffers  –2  to  his  Parry  until  his </p>
<p>next action. With a raise, the foe is distracted and </p>
<p>Shaken as well.</p>
<p>These  penalties  do  not  stack. Tricking  a  foe </p>
<p>twice has no additional effect.</p>
<p><i><b>Example: </b>Buck is backed into a corner by </i></p>
<p><i>a very large and dangerous thug. Our hero </i></p>
<p><i>pulls the oldest trick in the book. He says “Hi </i></p>
<p><i>Virginia!” and pretends to smile at someone </i></p>
<p><i>behind his less-than-brilliant foe. He and the </i></p>
<p><i>thug both make Smarts rolls, and Buck wins </i></p>
<p><i>with a raise. The thug swirls about, expecting </i></p>
<p><i>an attack from behind, and is momentarily </i></p>
<p><i>Shaken. The unfortunate goon also suffers </i></p>
<p><i>–2 to his Parry until his next action, giving </i></p>
<p><i>Buck time for a quick rabbit punch that just </i></p>
<p><i>might put the big fellow down.</i></p>
<p>Two.Weapons</p>
<p>A character may attack with a weapon in each </p>
<p>hand if he desires. This works just like any other </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--87</p>
<p>multi-action,  and  inflicts  a  –2  penalty  to  each </p>
<p>attack. (Note that the Two-Fisted Edge negates </p>
<p>the multi-action penalty when attacking with two </p>
<p>weapons.)</p>
<p>Unless your hero has the Ambidextrous Edge, </p>
<p>subtract  another  2  points  from  the  off-handed </p>
<p>attack  (see  Off-Hand Attacks). A  hero  with  the </p>
<p>Frenzy Edge adds the additional die with either </p>
<p>melee attack of his choice.</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b> A warrior is backed into a corner </i></p>
<p><i>by a pack of ravenous dire wolves. He has </i></p>
<p><i>two  short  swords  but  isn’t Ambidextrous. </i></p>
<p><i>The first roll suffers a –2 penalty (for using </i></p>
<p><i>two  weapons),  and  the  second  suffers  a </i></p>
<p><i>–4 penalty (the multi-action penalty for two </i></p>
<p><i>weapons  plus  the  off-hand  penalty).  He </i></p>
<p><i>makes his Fighting roll twice, and gets his </i></p>
<p><i>Wild Die with each roll.</i></p>
<p>Unarmed.Defender</p>
<p>If one character has a melee weapon and his </p>
<p>foe doesn’t, the opponent is considered unarmed </p>
<p>and is very likely in a world of hurt. Since he can </p>
<p>only  dodge  and  evade  rather  than  parry,  any </p>
<p>armed attacker trying to hit him may add +2 to his </p>
<p>Fighting roll. Nearly all animals and monsters are </p>
<p>considered armed due to natural weapons such </p>
<p>as claws and teeth.</p>
<p>Unstable.Platform</p>
<p>A character attempting to fire a ranged attack </p>
<p>from the back of a horse or other mount, a moving </p>
<p>vehicle, or other “unstable platform” suffers –2 to </p>
<p>his Shooting roll.</p>
<p>Wild.Attack</p>
<p>Sometimes  a  desperate  character  may  want </p>
<p>to  throw  caution  to  the  wind  and  attack  with </p>
<p>everything he’s got. This is called a “wild attack,” </p>
<p>and can be devastating if used correctly. If used </p>
<p>recklessly,  it  can  quickly  get  even  a  veteran </p>
<p>character slaughtered.</p>
<p>Performing  a  wild  attack  adds  +2  to  the </p>
<p>character’s Fighting attack and resulting damage </p>
<p>roll, but his Parry is reduced by 2 until his next </p>
<p>action.</p>
<p>Wild attacks can be used with multiple attacks, </p>
<p>such as from the Frenzy or Sweep Edges, or with </p>
<p>two weapons.</p>
<p>Withdrawing.From.Close.Combat</p>
<p>Inevitably,  your  hero  will  decide  discretion  is </p>
<p>the  better  part  of  valor.  Whenever  a  character </p>
<p>retreats  from  melee,  all  adjacent  non-Shaken </p>
<p>opponents get an immediate free attack (but only </p>
<p>one—no Frenzy or other Edges apply unless they </p>
<p>specifically say otherwise).</p>
<p>A  character  may  take  the  Defend  option  (+2 </p>
<p>Parry) while retreating from combat, but won’t be </p>
<p>able to perform other actions that round besides </p>
<p>movement and still suffers the free attack.</p>
<p><i><b>Example: </b> Inspector  Mars  is  attacked  by </i></p>
<p><i>three  cultists  in  melee.  He  decides  to  run </i></p>
<p><i>for it, giving each cultist a free Fighting roll </i></p>
<p><i>against  him.  Mars  wisely  uses  the  Defend </i></p>
<p><i>maneuver as well to increase his Parry by </i></p>
<p><i>+2 until he can get away.</i></p>
<p>Healing</p>
<p>The Healing skill can be used to treat any wound </p>
<p>suffered within the last hour. Each attempt takes </p>
<p>10 minutes.</p>
<p>A  character  may  only  attempt  to  heal  fresh </p>
<p>wounds on a given patient once within the hour </p>
<p>they were  sustained. A  different  character may </p>
<p>attempt a Healing roll, but once attempted, that </p>
<p>healer  has  done  all  he  can  for  that  particular </p>
<p>patient.</p>
<p>A success on a Healing roll removes one wound, </p>
<p>and a raise removes two. Further raises have no </p>
<p>effect.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Modifiers:</b>  The  healer  must  subtract  the </p>
<p>patient’s  wound  levels  from  his  skill  roll. A </p>
<p>wounded character trying to heal his own injuries </p>
<p>suffers  from  both  effects  (his  wounds  plus  the </p>
<p>wound penalty to the Healing roll).</p>
<p>Healing requires some basic supplies such as </p>
<p>bandages and reasonably clean water. If these </p>
<p>aren’t available, the healer suffers a –2 penalty </p>
<p>to his roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>The Golden Hour: </b>After one hour, only natural </p>
<p>healing or the <i>greater healing</i> power can help.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Incapacitated  Patients: </b> If  a  victim  is </p>
<p>Incapacitated the healer must first make a Healing </p>
<p>roll to remove that state, He may then attempt </p>
<p>further Healing rolls to remove actual wounds.</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.The.Golden.Hour</p>
<p><i>In the real world, paramedics have a term </i></p>
<p><i>called  “the  Golden  Hour.”  Patients  who </i></p>
<p><i>survive  their  initial  trauma  have  about  an </i></p>
<p><i>hour to survive most life-threatening injuries. </i></p>
<p><i>If they receive medical attention during that </i></p>
<p><i>time, they can generally be saved. The longer </i></p>
<p><i>the wait, however, the more likely the wounds </i></p>
<p><i>are to be fatal.</i></p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--88</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p><i>In Savage Worlds, we extend this concept </i></p>
<p><i>to healing in general. A medic who can treat </i></p>
<p><i>a wound within the first hour can typically </i></p>
<p><i>negate  some  of  its  ill  effects.  Besides </i></p>
<p><i>reflecting  the  real-world  concept,  it  also </i></p>
<p><i>provides  good  game  balance  and  drama. </i></p>
<p><i>If the party could simply retry Healing rolls </i></p>
<p><i>over and over at any time, their group would </i></p>
<p><i>never experience the drama of being beaten </i></p>
<p><i>up by the bad guys, then deciding whether </i></p>
<p><i>or not to seek shelter and rest up, or striving </i></p>
<p><i>on despite their gruesome wounds.</i></p>
<p>Natural.Healing</p>
<p>Every  five  days,  wounded  or  Incapacitated </p>
<p>characters  may  make  Vigor  rolls.  Wild  Cards </p>
<p>remove one wound level (or their Incapacitated </p>
<p>status) with a success, or improve two steps with </p>
<p>a raise. A critical failure on a natural healing roll </p>
<p>increases a Wild Card’s wound level by one. If </p>
<p>the hero already has three wounds he becomes </p>
<p>Incapacitated.  Extras  lose  their  Incapacitated </p>
<p>status with a success and expire if they roll a 1 </p>
<p>on their Vigor die.</p>
<p>Subtract wound penalties from  these  rolls as </p>
<p>usual,  as  well  as  any  of  the  modifiers  below. </p>
<p>These  are  cumulative,  so  rough  traveling  in </p>
<p>intense cold with one wound is a total penalty of </p>
<p>–5, for example.</p>
<p>Medical  attention  means  that  someone  with </p>
<p>the Healing skill is actively checking the patient’s </p>
<p>wounds,  changing  dressings,  giving  what </p>
<p>medicines  are  available,  and  generally  looking </p>
<p>after the patient’s well-being.</p>
<p>Natural.Healing.Modifiers</p>
<p><b>  Modifier </b></p>
<p><b>Condition</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Rough traveling</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>No medical attention</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Poor environmental conditions, </p>
<p>such as intense cold, heat, or </p>
<p>rain</p>
<p> </p>
<p>— </p>
<p>Medical attention (1940 or </p>
<p>earlier)</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+1 </p>
<p>Medical attention (1941 or better)</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>Medical attention (2010 and </p>
<p>beyond)</p>
<p>Aftermath</p>
<p>It’s  often  important to know  what  happens to </p>
<p>Extras  who  were  Incapacitated  during  a  fight. </p>
<p>This creates interesting choices for the players </p>
<p>after battle as they must decide what to do with </p>
<p>their wounded companions and living captives. Do </p>
<p>they leave their men behind? Do they slaughter </p>
<p>their foes? These situations should present your </p>
<p>group with chances to roleplay their characters, </p>
<p>and challenges to their overall plans as they have </p>
<p>to deal with prisoners or walking wounded.</p>
<p>After a fight, the players make Vigor rolls for </p>
<p>all of their wounded allies and the GM rolls for </p>
<p>wounded foes. With a success, the Extra is alive </p>
<p>but Incapacitated (failure indicates death). With </p>
<p>a raise, the wounds were only superficial and the </p>
<p>character may function normally.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Walking Wounded: </b>If it becomes important </p>
<p>to know which Incapacitated characters can walk </p>
<p>and which cannot, make a second Vigor roll for </p>
<p>each. Those who make it are “walking wounded”—</p>
<p>they may shamble slowly but still cannot fight or </p>
<p>perform other useful actions.</p>
<p>Those who don’t make the roll can be moved </p>
<p>but  risk  aggravating  their  injuries.  They  must </p>
<p>make another Vigor roll for each and every hour </p>
<p>of movement. Should they fail, they begin to die. </p>
<p>They may be stabilized with a Healing roll at –2, </p>
<p>but any further movement will no doubt be fatal.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--89</p>
<p><b>Aim:</b> +2 Shooting/Throwing if character does </p>
<p>not move</p>
<p><b>Area Effect Attacks:</b> Targets under template </p>
<p>suffer damage, treat cover  as  armor;  missed </p>
<p>attack  rolls  cause  1d6”  deviation  for  thrown </p>
<p>weapons, 1d10” for launched weapons; x1 for </p>
<p>Short, x2 for Medium, x3 for Long</p>
<p><b>Automatic Fire:</b> See rules</p>
<p><b>Breaking  Things:</b>  See  Obstacle Toughness </p>
<p>Table; Parry 2; No bonus damage or Aces</p>
<p><b>Called Shots:</b> Limb –2; Head –4, +4 damage; </p>
<p>Small target –4; Tiny target –6</p>
<p><b>Cover:</b> Light –1; Medium –2; Heavy –4</p>
<p><b>Darkness:</b>  Dim  –1;  Dark  –2,  targets  are  not </p>
<p>visible beyond 10”; For Pitch Darkness, targets </p>
<p>must be detected to be attacked at –4</p>
<p><b>Defend:</b> +2 Parry; character may take no other </p>
<p>actions</p>
<p><b>Disarm:</b> –2 attack; defender makes Str roll vs. </p>
<p>damage or drops weapon</p>
<p><b>Double  Tap/3  Rd  Burst:</b>  +1  attack  and </p>
<p>damage/+2 attack and damage</p>
<p><b>The Drop:</b> +4 attack and damage</p>
<p><b>Finishing Move:</b> Instant kill to helpless foe with </p>
<p>lethal weapon</p>
<p><b>Firing Into Melee:</b> See Innocent Bystanders</p>
<p><b>Full Defense:</b> Fighting roll at+2 replaces Parry </p>
<p>if higher</p>
<p><b>Ganging  Up:</b>  +1  Fighting  per  additional </p>
<p>attacker; maximum of +4</p>
<p><b>Grappling:</b> Opposed Fighting roll to grapple. </p>
<p>Raise=opponent  Shaken;  Defender  makes </p>
<p>opposed Strength or Agility to break free (any </p>
<p>other action made at –4); Attacker can make </p>
<p>opposed Str or Agility to cause damage</p>
<p><b>Improvised Weapons:</b> <i>Small Weapons:</i> Range </p>
<p>3/6/12, Damage Str+d4, RoF 1, Min Str d4, –1 </p>
<p>attack  and  Parry; <i> Medium  Weapons:</i>  Range </p>
<p>2/4/8, Damage Str+d6, RoF 1, Min Str d6, –1 </p>
<p>Attack and Parry; <i>Large Weapons:</i> Range 1/2/4, </p>
<p>Damage Str+d8, RoF 1, Min Str d8, –1 attack </p>
<p>and Parry</p>
<p><b>Innocent  Bystanders:</b>  Missed  Shooting  or </p>
<p>Throwing  roll  of  1  (1  or  2  with  shotguns  or </p>
<p>autofire) hits random adjacent target</p>
<p><b>Nonlethal Damage:</b> Characters are knocked out </p>
<p>instead of potentially killed when Incapacitated</p>
<p><b>Obstacles:</b> If attack hits by the concealment </p>
<p>penalty, the obstacle acts as Armor</p>
<p><b>Off-Hand Attack:</b> –2 to Fighting/Shooting with </p>
<p>off-hand</p>
<p><b>Prone:</b> As Medium cover; prone defenders are </p>
<p>–2 Fighting, –2 Parry</p>
<p><b>Push:</b> <i> Bash:</i>  Push  the  target  1”  for  every </p>
<p>success and raise on the Strength roll; <i>Shield </i></p>
<p><i>Bash:</i> As above but causes Strength damage, </p>
<p>+1 for a small shield, +2 for a medium shield, </p>
<p>and +3 for  a  large  shield; <i>Knock  Prone:</i> The </p>
<p>defender is knocked prone</p>
<p><b>Ranged Weapons in Close Combat:</b> Pistols </p>
<p>only; Target Number is defender’s Parry</p>
<p><b>Rapid Attack:</b> Make up to 3 Fighting attacks at </p>
<p>–4; or fire up to 6 shots from a semi-automatic </p>
<p>weapon or </p>
<p>revolver at –4 penalty to each die</p>
<p><b>Suppressive  Fire:</b>  With  successful  Shooting </p>
<p>roll, targets in Med Burst Template make a Spirit </p>
<p>roll or are Shaken; roll of 1 are hit for normal </p>
<p>damage</p>
<p><b>Touch Attack:</b> +2 Fighting</p>
<p><b>Tricks:</b>  Describe  action;  make  opposed </p>
<p>Agility </p>
<p>or </p>
<p>Smarts </p>
<p>roll; </p>
<p>opponent </p>
<p>is </p>
<p>–2 Parry until next action; with a raise, foe is –2 </p>
<p>Parry and Shaken</p>
<p><b>Two Weapons:</b> –2 attack; additional –2 for off-</p>
<p>hand if not Ambidextrous</p>
<p><b>Unarmed Defender:</b> Armed attackers gain +2 </p>
<p>Fighting</p>
<p><b>Unstable Platform:</b> –2 Shooting from a moving </p>
<p>vehicle or animal</p>
<p><b>Wild Attack:</b> +2 Fighting; +2 damage; –2 Parry </p>
<p>until next action</p>
<p><b>Withdrawing from Close Combat:</b> Adjacent </p>
<p>foes get one free attack at retreating character</p>
<p>Attack.Options.Summary</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--90</p>
<p>Extended.Combat.Example</p>
<p>The heroes are two soldiers in a Weird Wars: Vietnam campaign—Lt. Griffith and Private Daniels. </p>
<p>While on patrol, they’re attacked by a pack of ravenous walking dead. The Game Master goes to </p>
<p>combat rounds and deals out Action Cards. He draws a Ten of Clubs for the zombies. Lt. Griffith draws </p>
<p>a Jack of Spades. Private Daniels has the Quick Edge and draws a Two of Clubs. That’s too low so </p>
<p>he draws again and gets a Joker!</p>
<p>Private.Dan’–.Joker</p>
<p>Dan whips out a grenade and places the Medium Burst Template directly over three of the zombies. </p>
<p>They’re in Short Range so there are no penalties to the roll. The Joker gives him a +2 and his Throwing </p>
<p>is d6. He rolls a 6, which is an Ace. He rolls again and gets a 4. That’s 6+4+2 for the Joker, or 12. He </p>
<p>hits with a raise over the standard TN of 4. Grenades cause 3d6 damage, and 4d6 with the raise. After </p>
<p>Acing and adding all the dice together, he gets a 23. That’s far more than a raise over the zombies’ </p>
<p>Toughness 7, so that’s a wound to each. They’re Extras so that’s all they can take and all three are </p>
<p>wiped out.</p>
<p>Lt..Griffith.–.Jack.of.Spades</p>
<p>Lt. Griffith now fires full-auto and decides to split his shots between zombies G, F, and E. His Shooting </p>
<p>is d6 and he has an M-16. He doesn’t have the Rock n’ Roll Edge so his shots are at –2. His TN at </p>
<p>Short Range is 4, so with the –2 he needs to roll 6 or better on each of his dice.</p>
<p>The first two dice are misses. The third die is an Ace (6) so he rolls again and gets another Ace </p>
<p>(6). He rolls once more and gets a 4 for a total of 16, minus two for full-auto is 14. A hit with a raise </p>
<p>on Zombie E. The Lt.’s Wild Die also Aces and winds up with a grand total of 6. He chooses to use </p>
<p>that on Zombie F.</p>
<p>Lt. Griffith rolls the hit on Zombie F first. Damage on an M-16 is 2d8. He rolls the dice together and </p>
<p>gets a total of 9. That exceeds the zombie’s Toughness of 7 but isn’t a raise, so it’s Shaken.</p>
<p>Now Lt. Griffith rolls the hit with the raise on Zombie E. The damage is 2d8 plus a d6 for the raise. </p>
<p>Griffith rolls a 4, an 8, and a 6. He rolls both the 8 and the 6 again since those are both Aces and </p>
<p>gets a 2 on both dice. That’s (4+8+6+2+2=) 20! That’s a raise over the zombie’s Toughness so it’s </p>
<p>blasted to pieces.</p>
<p>The.Zombies.—.Ten.of.Clubs</p>
<p>Now the zombies go. Zombie F is Shaken and makes a Spirit roll at +2 for being undead. It gets </p>
<p>a total of 7, so it’s unShaken but cannot act. It can move, however, so it shambles into contact with </p>
<p>Private Dan.</p>
<p>Zombies D and G also move into contact with Private Dan. Since Zombie F is in contact, this gives </p>
<p>them +2 on their Fighting rolls due to the Gang Up Bonus (page 73). Zombie D misses but G gets a </p>
<p>total of 6 and Dan’s Parry is 5, so that’s a hit. It does Str+d4 with its claws. It’s Strength is a d6, so the </p>
<p>GM rolls a d6+d4 and gets a 5. Dan’s Toughness is 5, so he’s Shaken.</p>
<p>Round.Two</p>
<p>The GM shuffles the deck because Dan got a Joker, then deals new Action Cards. Lt. Griffith gets a </p>
<p>lousy Two of Clubs. The zombies get an Ace of Diamonds, and Dan gets an Ace of Clubs. Diamonds </p>
<p>go before Clubs, so the zombies act first.</p>
<p>Zombies.—.Ace.of.Diamonds</p>
<p>All the zombies can now attack, and give each other +2 to their Fighting rolls since they’re ganging </p>
<p>up on the private. Zombie D misses with a 1. Zombie G gets a 5, +2 for ganging up for a total of 7. </p>
<p>That’s regular damage of Str+d4, and it gets a total of 6. Since Dan is already Shaken, that causes </p>
<p>a wound and he remains Shaken.</p>
<p>Zombie F now gets really lucky. It hits with a raise and rolls15 damage. Every raise over Dan’s </p>
<p>Toughness is a wound, so that’s two wounds. He already has one from Zombie G, so that brings him </p>
<p>to three wounds total. He’s already Shaken so that doesn’t change.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--91</p>
<p>Private.Dan.—.Ace.of.Clubs</p>
<p>Dan fails his Spirit roll and can’t act. He doesn’t move out of combat either or the zombies will get </p>
<p>free attacks on him (page 76).</p>
<p>Lt..Griffith.—.Two.of.Clubs</p>
<p>It’s all up to Lt. Griffith. He fires on full-auto again and puts a die into each zombie. He misses </p>
<p>Zombie D but hits with a raise on Zombie G—it goes down with a damage total of 13 (a raise over </p>
<p>its Toughness).</p>
<p>Unfortunately, the shot at Zombie F comes up a 2. The Innocent Bystander rules kick in (page </p>
<p>73) and Private Dan is hit for 2d8 damage! The Lt. grimaces, rolls, and causes 12 damage. Dan is </p>
<p>already at 3 wounds and this causes more, so he’s Incapacitated. He makes a Vigor roll at –3 for </p>
<p>his wounds and gets a total of 2. He rolls on the Injury Table and is Bleeding Out (page 69). He’ll </p>
<p>have to make Vigor rolls each round to keep from dying unless Lt. Griffith can finish the zombies </p>
<p>and heal him.</p>
<p>But the GM asks Lt. Griffith to make a Fear check as he sees his trooper fall to the gruesome </p>
<p>monsters. He fails.</p>
<p>The last thing Private Dan sees is his commanding officer run screaming into the night.</p>
<p>War is Hell.</p>
<p>A</p>
<p>B</p>
<p>C</p>
<p>D</p>
<p>E</p>
<p>F</p>
<p>G</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--92</p>
<p>The following chapter contains rules you need </p>
<p>only  in  particular  situations—such  as  handling </p>
<p>large  groups  of  allies,  chases,  or  dealing  with </p>
<p>hazards such as fire, drowning, or radiation.</p>
<p>If this is your first time through the book, skim </p>
<p>over the various sections so you know what’s in </p>
<p>here, then come back and check them out in detail </p>
<p>when you need them.</p>
<p><b>Allies:</b>  Whether  they’re  sturdy  men-at-arms,  a </p>
<p>hardy pirate crew, or soldiers under the heroes’ </p>
<p>command, Allies are often a big part of <i>Savage </i></p>
<p><i>Worlds. </i> Here  you’ll  find  statistics  for  common </p>
<p>henchmen, how to use them, and how to increase </p>
<p>the experience of those who survive your warriors’ </p>
<p>adventures.</p>
<p><b>Chases:</b> Fast-paced chases between hunter and </p>
<p>prey are the subject of this section. Run anything </p>
<p>from  cat-and-mouse  car  chases  through  busy </p>
<p>cities to massive dogfights with this simple system. </p>
<p>For those who played previous versions of <i>Savage </i></p>
<p><i>Worlds,</i> this is all new and greatly simplified.</p>
<p><b>Dramatic Tasks:</b> Defusing a bomb, performing </p>
<p>an ancient ritual, or hacking a computer are all </p>
<p>Dramatic Tasks. These  rules  turn  what  would </p>
<p>otherwise be simple skill rolls into a tense and </p>
<p>exciting encounter.</p>
<p><b>Fear:</b> This is the section to turn to when your hero </p>
<p>faces Things Man Was Not Meant to Know. Fear </p>
<p>tests and the grim consequences of failing them </p>
<p>are found within.</p>
<p><b>Hazards:</b> Hazards include the rules for Fatigue, </p>
<p>plus Bumps and Bruises, Cold, Disease, Drowning, </p>
<p>Falling,  Fire,  Heat,  Hunger,  Poison,  Radiation, </p>
<p>Sleep, and Thirst.</p>
<p><b>Interludes:</b> Books, movies, and real life provide </p>
<p>heroes  with  many  opportunities  to  share </p>
<p>something of their personality or their past with </p>
<p>their companions. This often gets overlooked in </p>
<p>roleplaying games where the narrative is focused </p>
<p>on action and mysteries. Interludes address this </p>
<p>by giving some form and function to small talk, and </p>
<p>rewarding those players who get into character </p>
<p>and roleplay a dramatic tale of their adventurer’s </p>
<p>backstory.</p>
<p><b>Mass  Battles:</b>  Many  Savage  Settings  feature </p>
<p>massive  wars,  bloody  battles,  and  terrible </p>
<p>conflicts. This system gives the Game Master and </p>
<p>the player characters a way to game out any size </p>
<p>battle quickly and dramatically. It also provides </p>
<p>a way for the heroes to get in on the action and </p>
<p>influence the final results.</p>
<p><b>Setting  Rules:</b>  The  standard  rules  are  a </p>
<p>foundation for your campaign. Setting Rules help </p>
<p>one world stand out from another. Here you’ll find </p>
<p>rules like Blood &amp; Guts—which allows players to </p>
<p>spend  Bennies on  damage  rolls!  Or  No  Power </p>
<p>Points, which trades the Power Point system for </p>
<p>a faster but riskier method. These Setting Rules </p>
<p>are referenced in the One Sheet adventures at the </p>
<p>end of this book at the start of each tale.</p>
<p><b>Social  Conflicts:</b>  How  do  you  handle  a  court </p>
<p>case? What do you do if your heroes petition the </p>
<p>king for aid—while a rival actively works against </p>
<p>them? This section presents a simple and dramatic </p>
<p>system for social conflicts based on Persuasion </p>
<p>and  how  successful—or  unsuccessful—the </p>
<p>petitioner is.</p>
<p><b>Travel:</b> Here you’ll find guidelines for travel times </p>
<p>and how to stage encounters along the way.</p>
<p><b>Vehicles:</b> Vehicular battles are a staple of post-</p>
<p>apocalyptic fiction, World War II adventures, and </p>
<p>other technological settings. This section covers </p>
<p>how to  move vehicles  on the  table-top,  handle </p>
<p>attacks between them, and what happens when </p>
<p>they go out of control.</p>
<p>Chapter.Four:.</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--93</p>
<p>Allies</p>
<p>Allies play a big part in many <i>Savage Worlds</i> </p>
<p>games. They serve as troops under your hero’s </p>
<p>command in <i>Weird War</i> games, loyal retainers </p>
<p>in fantasy settings, or fellow fighters in glorious </p>
<p>rebellions against oppression.</p>
<p>Keeping  up  with  allies  in <i> Savage  Worlds</i>  is </p>
<p>simple.  Just  download  the Ally  Sheet  from  our </p>
<p>website and fill in the blanks. </p>
<p>Allied.Personalities</p>
<p>You can add a little flavor to your allies by rolling </p>
<p>on the Personality Table. Jot down the keyword on </p>
<p>the Ally Sheet so that you and your Game Master </p>
<p>can have a little insight into each particular ally’s </p>
<p>character.</p>
<p>For the most part, you should consider these </p>
<p>general  impressions  with  no  particular  game </p>
<p>effect. What they can do is help both the players </p>
<p>and the GM decide just how an ally might react </p>
<p>in a given situation. In a <i>Weird War Two</i> game, </p>
<p>for  instance,  a  player  with  a  young  lieutenant </p>
<p>character could look over his list and choose the </p>
<p>“Observant”  character  to  pull  guard  duty.  If  he </p>
<p>has to go with the “Lazy” soldier for some reason, </p>
<p>there’s a good chance the GM will rule he falls </p>
<p>asleep sometime during his watch. </p>
<p>Personality.Table</p>

<p>Heroic</p>
<p>Allies.&amp;.Experience</p>
<p>Allies  who  take  part  in  battle  with  their  more </p>
<p>heroic employers gain experience as well—but not </p>
<p>as quickly as player characters. Don’t keep track </p>
<p>of their experience points—just roll randomly to </p>
<p>see if they’ve “leveled.”</p>
<p>At the end of a game session in which the allies </p>
<p>had  a  significant  role  (usually  by  participating </p>
<p>in combat), roll a d6 for each group of identical </p>
<p>troops. On a roll of 5-6, the survivors level up just </p>
<p>like player characters and get one Advance. On </p>
<p>a failure, they don’t.</p>
<p>Ammo</p>
<p>Keeping  track  of  ammo  for  all  your  firearm-</p>
<p>bearing allies can be a real pain. Here’s an easy </p>
<p>and dramatic way to handle this problem.</p>
<p>The ammo level of each group of allied Extras </p>
<p>starts at Very High, High (the usual level), Low, </p>
<p>or Out, as determined by the GM. A dot for each </p>
<p>of these ammunition states can be found on the </p>
<p>Ally Sheet.</p>
<p>After each fight, the ammo drops a level (unless </p>
<p>the GM feels the allies didn’t really use much in </p>
<p>that scene). In combat, if the allies are dealt a </p>
<p>Two,  their  ammo  level  drops  a  level <i> after</i>  that </p>
<p>round. This  makes  for  dramatic  situations  and </p>
<p>realistic  logistical  problems  while  eliminating  a </p>
<p>major bookkeeping chore.</p>
<p>Typical.Allies</p>
<p>Here are a few typical soldier archetypes you </p>
<p>might use for your own <i>Savage Worlds</i> games. </p>
<p>Fill in any additional skills or Edges as you see </p>
<p>fit. A group of rangers, for example, should have </p>
<p>the Tracking skill, while cavalrymen should have </p>
<p>the Riding skill, and so on.</p>
<p>Soldier</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility d6, Smarts </p>
<p>d4,  Spirit  d6,  Strength  d6, </p>
<p>Vigor d6</p>
<p><b>Skills:</b> Fighting d6, Notice </p>
<p>d6, Shooting d6, Stealth </p>
<p>d4</p>
<p><b>Charisma:</b>  —; <b> Pace:</b>  6; </p>
<p><b>Parry:</b> 5; <b>Toughness:</b> 5</p>
<p>Experienced.Soldier</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d6, </p>
<p>Smarts  d6,  Spirit  d6, </p>
<p>Strength d8, Vigor d8</p>
<p><b>Skills:</b>  Fighting  d8, </p>
<p>Notice  d8,  Shooting </p>
<p>d8, Stealth d6</p>
<p><b>Charisma: </b> —;<b>  Pace:</b>  6; </p>
<p><b>Parry:</b> 6;<b> Toughness:</b> 6</p>
<p><b>Edges:</b> Any  two  combat </p>
<p>Edges.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--94</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Chases</p>
<p>The  Vehicle  Rules  on  page  113  handle  fast-</p>
<p>paced action on the table-top. This is impractical </p>
<p>for chases that take place over longer distances </p>
<p>such as city streets or the open ocean, so use the </p>
<p>Chase rules instead. It’s built for situations when </p>
<p>the heroes must catch someone or something—or </p>
<p>escape from it!</p>
<p>Chase.Length</p>
<p>First determine the length of the Chase:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Standard:</b> Five rounds, used for most chases </p>
<p>and usually simulates less than a minute of </p>
<p>action per turn.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Extended:</b> 10 rounds, used for long pursuits </p>
<p>such as those that take place between ships, </p>
<p>and may be measured in minutes, hours, or </p>
<p>even days.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Dogfight:</b>  The  “chase”  is  actually  a  battle </p>
<p>with  highly  mobile  participants.  It  ends  only </p>
<p>when  one  side  or  the  other  withdraws  or </p>
<p>accomplishes its objectives.</p>
<p>At  the  end  of  the  last  round,  any  prey  who </p>
<p>haven’t  been  otherwise  stopped  or  captured </p>
<p>escape and the pursuit ends.</p>
<p>Chase.Cards</p>
<p>Rather than dealing Action Card as usual, have </p>
<p>everyone roll the appropriate “maneuvering Trait” </p>
<p>as listed below:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Agility:</b> Foot chase</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Boating:</b> Watercraft</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Driving:</b> Vehicular chase</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Piloting:</b> Air or spacecraft</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Riding:</b> Mounted animals</p>
<p>Participants draw a card for each success and </p>
<p>raise on the maneuvering Trait roll, and keep one </p>
<p>(usually the highest) as their Action Card. This </p>
<p>determines not only their initiative but how well </p>
<p>they managed to “maneuver” that particular round.</p>
<p>Characters  who  don’t  score  at  least  a  single </p>
<p>success remain in the chase but get no Action </p>
<p>Card that round.</p>
<p>Making a maneuvering Trait roll does not count </p>
<p>as an action.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Advantage: </b> A  character  with  a  higher  card </p>
<p>than a foe is said to have “advantage” over him. </p>
<p>This  is  abstract  so  it  may  mean  the  character </p>
<p>is  ahead,  behind,  or  even  parallel  to  his  foe. </p>
<p>The  important  thing  is  that  he  has  somehow </p>
<p>maneuvered in such a way as to gain a superior </p>
<p>position, and can bring his weapons to bear (if he </p>
<p>has any and is inclined to use them). See <b>Attacks</b> </p>
<p>for further information on using the Advantage.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Speed:</b> A character may add +2 to his roll if </p>
<p>he or his vehicle has a higher top speed than the </p>
<p>fastest opponent; or +4 if his top speed is twice </p>
<p>as  fast  as  his  opponent’s.  (Use  Pace  plus  the </p>
<p>character’s  maximum  possible  running  value  if </p>
<p>on foot or mounted.)</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Edges:</b>  Level  Headed  and  the  Quick  Edge </p>
<p>don’t apply to maneuvering Trait rolls in Chases.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Terrain:</b> If the Chase takes place primarily over </p>
<p>difficult terrain, the GM should inflict a –2 penalty </p>
<p>to all participants’ Trait rolls.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Climb:</b> If an aircraft has a better Climb than </p>
<p>his foe, he may also add +2 to the roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Passengers:</b> Those riding in a vehicle have </p>
<p>a choice. If they want to help the pilot or driver </p>
<p>maneuver,  and  it  makes  sense  in  the  context </p>
<p>of  the  situation,  they  may  make  a  cooperative </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--95</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p><b>Card</b></p>
<p><b>Range</b></p>
<p><b>Complication Table</b></p>
<p><b>Two</b></p>
<p>Out of Range. The enemy is </p>
<p>out of range or blocked and </p>
<p>no attack is possible this </p>
<p>round</p>
<p><b>Disaster:</b> Make a Trait roll at –4. If the roll is failed, the </p>
<p>character suffers a disaster of some sort—a car hits </p>
<p>a solid obstacle at its top speed, a runner falls off a </p>
<p>ledge, etc. Where this isn’t possible, the runner gives </p>
<p>out, the vehicle stops, etc. In any event, this participant </p>
<p>is out of the chase.</p>
<p><b>3-10</b></p>
<p>Long Range (–4)</p>
<p><b>Major Obstacle:</b> Objects of some sort get in the way. </p>
<p>Make a Trait roll at –2 to avoid them or suffer damage </p>
<p>appropriate to half top speed if in a vehicle, or a Fatigue </p>
<p>level (Bumps and Bruises, see page 86) if on foot.</p>
<p><b>Jack–</b></p>
<p><b>Queen</b></p>
<p>Medium Range (–2)</p>
<p><b>Minor  Obstacle:</b>  Objects  of  some  sort  get  in  the </p>
<p>way. Make a Trait roll to avoid them or suffer damage </p>
<p>appropriate to half top speed if in a vehicle, or a Fatigue </p>
<p>level (Bumps and Bruises, see page 86) if on foot.</p>
<p><b>King–</b></p>
<p><b>Joker</b></p>
<p>Short (no penalty), </p>
<p>and melee attacks are </p>
<p>possible</p>
<p><b>Distraction:</b>  Something  obscures  the  character’s </p>
<p>vision or path. He cannot attack this round.</p>
<p>Attack.Range.and.Complication.Table</p>
<p>maneuvering  Trait  roll.  They  draw  no  cards </p>
<p>themselves but may add to the driver’s total as </p>
<p>usual.</p>
<p>The GM must decide what makes sense here. </p>
<p>A  passenger  in  a  car  might  help  navigate  and </p>
<p>make a cooperative roll—but a second passenger </p>
<p>probably could not. On a sailing ship, the crew </p>
<p>could make a group cooperative Boating roll to </p>
<p>help the captain’s total.</p>
<p>A passenger who makes a cooperative roll and </p>
<p>wants to act would suffer the usual multi-action </p>
<p>penalty.  Passengers  act  on  the  driver’s Action </p>
<p>Card.</p>
<p>Attacks</p>
<p>Characters act on their Action Card as usual. </p>
<p>Since distance is abstract, the value of their Action </p>
<p>Card  determines  any  penalties  to  the  roll  due </p>
<p>to Range and whether or not a melee attack is </p>
<p>possible (in chases where it’s even a possibility).</p>
<p>A character must have Advantage (an equal or </p>
<p>higher Action Card) than his target to attack it. A </p>
<p>character with an Action Card of 7, for example, can </p>
<p>only attack targets with Action Cards of 7 or lower. </p>
<p>A target with a higher card has outmaneuvered the </p>
<p>attacker this round and cannot be targeted.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Groups:</b>  Extras  roll  as  distinct  groups  and </p>
<p>act  on  the  same Action  Card  as  usual.  Divide </p>
<p>each group’s attacks up proportionately among </p>
<p>the  opposition,  losing  attacks  against  heroes </p>
<p>who  have Advantage  over  them.  For  example, </p>
<p>nine  wolves  act  on  Seven  and  there  are  five </p>
<p>player characters, so each hero is assigned two </p>
<p>wolves. Any heroes who have higher cards than </p>
<p>the wolves aren’t attacked that round. (This keeps </p>
<p>characters with low cards from getting attacked </p>
<p>by every enemy in the chase.)</p>
<p>The opposite is not true. The player characters </p>
<p>may always attack any foe or group of foes they </p>
<p>have Advantage over.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Force:</b> A driver with advantage may attempt to </p>
<p>distract or even ram another vehicle in the chase </p>
<p>as a normal action. The “trappings” may vary, but </p>
<p>it is treated as an opposed maneuvering Trait roll </p>
<p>modified  by  range.  (Cooperative  rolls  may  be </p>
<p>allowed as usual.) On a success, the target suffers </p>
<p>a –2 to his next maneuvering Trait roll. On a raise, </p>
<p>the target is affected as if they hit an obstacle (see </p>
<p>Complications).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Shaken Characters: </b>If a character driving or </p>
<p>piloting a vehicle is Shaken, he must make an </p>
<p>Out of Control roll (see page 115). If the vehicle </p>
<p>suffers damage, calculate it at half the vehicle’s </p>
<p>Top Speed (see page 113).</p>
<p>Characters  who  are  Shaken  at  the  start  of  a </p>
<p>turn make their maneuvering Trait roll at –2. They </p>
<p>attempt  to  become  un-Shaken  on  their Action </p>
<p>Card as usual.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--96</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Complications</p>
<p>If a character’s Action Card is a Club, he faces </p>
<p>some sort of Compli cation on his action. Check </p>
<p>the Complications Table.</p>
<p>Players who rolled high enough to get multiple </p>
<p>cards may choose to take a lower card to avoid </p>
<p>a  Complication. After  the  Complication  is  dealt </p>
<p>with,  the  character  may  continue  his  action  as </p>
<p>usual—assuming he survived.</p>
<p>Example:.Knights.&amp;.Bandits</p>
<p>The heroes are a knight and a squire chasing </p>
<p>three bandits through the woods. The knights are </p>
<p>on horseback while the bandits are on foot.</p>
<p>The three bandits make a group Agility roll (as </p>
<p>they’re on foot) and get a 5. That’s one success </p>
<p>so they draw a single card—a lowly Three.</p>
<p>The squire’s Riding roll is 7 after adding +2 for </p>
<p>being faster than the bandits. That’s one success </p>
<p>so he draws one Action Card—a Jack of Clubs. </p>
<p>Clubs  signals  a  Complication,  and  the  Jack </p>
<p>indicates a Minor Obstacle (the GM decides he </p>
<p>must avoid low-hanging branches). The Squire </p>
<p>fails and is hit by the heavy limbs for Fatigue from </p>
<p>Bumps and Bruises.</p>
<p>The knight’s Riding total is 13. He draws three </p>
<p>cards and chooses the best—a Joker. Since he’s </p>
<p>ahead of the bandits and has a King or higher he </p>
<p>can make a melee attack. He levels his lance and </p>
<p>one less rogue plagues the King’s Road.</p>
<p>Example:.Road.Warriors</p>
<p>Abel drives a rig through the wasteland. Big Ben </p>
<p>rides shotgun beside him, while Cale mans the </p>
<p>turret on the back of the fuel tanker. Dala trails </p>
<p>behind on a motorcycle.</p>
<p>Chasing them are five gangers on motorbikes. </p>
<p>They make a group Driving roll of 7, draw a single </p>
<p>card, and get an Eight.</p>
<p>Abel makes a Driving roll and gets a 7. Big Ben </p>
<p>decides to “navigate,” pointing out the debris in </p>
<p>the road ahead, and makes a cooperative Driving </p>
<p>roll. He’s successful and adds +2 to Abel’s total, </p>
<p>making it 9. That’s a success and a raise, so Abel </p>
<p>draws two cards and takes the highest—a Ten. </p>
<p>He’s faster than the gangers, so they can’t attack </p>
<p>him this round.</p>
<p>Cale mans a crossbow turret on the back of the </p>
<p>rig. He acts on Abel’s card of Ten, meaning he’s at </p>
<p>Long Range (–4). He fires and a scores a kill—a </p>
<p>ganger bites the radioactive dust.</p>
<p>Dala  is  on  a  motorcycle  and  rolls  a  4.  She </p>
<p>gets one card—a Four of Clubs. That’s a Major </p>
<p>Obstacle  Complication  so  she  has  to  make  a </p>
<p>Driving roll at –2 or suffer a nasty wipeout. She </p>
<p>barely makes it, the GM describing her skidding </p>
<p>around an outcropping of rocks.</p>
<p>The  gangers  are  on  Eight  so  they  can  only </p>
<p>attack  Dala  (on  a  Four).  Since  there  are  five </p>
<p>of them and four targets, the GM divides them </p>
<p>up proportionately and decides only two are in </p>
<p>position  to  attack.  One  ganger  misses  but  the </p>
<p>other  scores  a  hit  and  wounds  Dala,  meaning </p>
<p>she has to make another Driving roll or go Out </p>
<p>of Control.</p>
<p>Dramatic.Tasks</p>
<p>Heroes  often  find  themselves  in  dramatic </p>
<p>situations such as defusing a bomb or hacking a </p>
<p>computer with a definite—and often deadly—time </p>
<p>limit. The system below simulates these events </p>
<p>and helps the Game Master throw some monkey </p>
<p>wrenches and drama into what would otherwise </p>
<p>be simple skill rolls.</p>
<p>To  start,  first  determine  the  skill  to  be  used, </p>
<p>such  as  Knowledge  (Demolitions)  to  defuse  a </p>
<p>bomb, Knowledge (Occult) to complete a ritual, </p>
<p>Knowledge  (Electronics)  to  hack  a  computer, </p>
<p>and so on.</p>
<p>A standard Dramatic Task takes five “actions,” </p>
<p>and requires the same number of successes (see </p>
<p>below). Actions here may mean combat rounds </p>
<p>or they may mean “attempts.” That’s entirely up </p>
<p>to the Game Master and the situation.</p>
<p>Performing.a.Dramatic.Task</p>
<p>Each  action,  the  hero  draws  an Action  Card </p>
<p>(even  if  not  in  combat)  and  acts  on  that  card. </p>
<p>Edges such as Level Headed or Quick work as </p>
<p>usual.</p>
<p>If  the  character  nets  five  successes  with </p>
<p>the  required  Trait  before  time  runs  out,  he’s </p>
<p>accomplished his goal. It’s best to keep track of </p>
<p>these successes with tokens of some sort so you </p>
<p>don’t forget from round to round—and to give the </p>
<p>player a visible measure of his success.</p>
<p>If time runs out and the hero has not acquired </p>
<p>five  successes,  the  bomb  detonates,  the  ritual </p>
<p>fails, the computer shuts down, an alarm sounds, </p>
<p>or the action otherwise fails. It’s up to the Game </p>
<p>Master whether it may be attempted again, but </p>
<p>since this was a Dramatic Task, it should normally </p>
<p>have resulted in something very bad, or at least </p>
<p>require an entirely new approach to attempt again.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Difficulty:</b> Most Dramatic Tasks should have </p>
<p>at least a –2 modifier. This reflects any safeguards </p>
<p>on a bomb, the complexity of a ritual, security on </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--97</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p>a computer, etc. These are <i>dramatic </i>tasks, and by </p>
<p>their very nature should be fairly difficult.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Cooperative  Rolls:</b>  Other  characters  may </p>
<p>assist  the  acting  hero  as  usual,  using  the </p>
<p>Cooperative Rolls rule on page 71.</p>
<p>Complications</p>
<p>If the acting character’s Action Card is a Club, </p>
<p>something  has  gone  horribly  wrong.  He  must </p>
<p>make his roll this turn at –2 (in addition to any </p>
<p>other  modifiers).  If  failed,  the  worst  possible </p>
<p>resolution to the task occurs—the target escapes, </p>
<p>the argument ends violently, the bomb explodes, </p>
<p>or the battle is lost.</p>
<p><i><b>Example: </b>Ian “Haxxor” McTavish is trying </i></p>
<p><i>to  hack  into  a  high-security  mainframe  at </i></p>
<p><i>the corporate headquarters of Genesis, Inc. </i></p>
<p><i>He draws a Three of Clubs and must make </i></p>
<p><i>his  Knowledge  (Computers)  roll this round </i></p>
<p><i>at  –2.  If  he  fails,  Genesis  security  system </i></p>
<p><i>detects the intrusion. In this high-tech world </i></p>
<p><i>of netrunners, the Game Master decides Ian </i></p>
<p><i>doesn’t just fail—a powerful jolt of electricity </i></p>
<p><i>is sent through the cables for 2d6 damage!</i></p>
<p>Example:.Defusing.a.Bomb</p>
<p>The  heroes  are  counter-terrorist  operatives </p>
<p>holding off waves of suicidal attackers while their </p>
<p>demolitions  expert,  John,  attempts  to  defuse  a </p>
<p>nuclear weapon. The Game Master decides the </p>
<p>skill required is Knowledge (Demolitions), and all </p>
<p>rolls suffer a –2 penalty due to the bomb’s inherent </p>
<p>complexity.</p>
<p>On  the  first  round,  the  rest  of  the  heroes </p>
<p>attack  the  terrorists  while  John  tries  to  defuse </p>
<p>the bomb. His Action Card is a Six of Clubs—a </p>
<p>Complication! The Game Master tells John the </p>
<p>bomb was booby-trapped. John puts everything </p>
<p>he has into the roll this turn and asks for help from </p>
<p>one of his teammates who also has Knowledge </p>
<p>(Demolitions).</p>
<p>The companion makes a cooperative roll and </p>
<p>adds  +2  to  John’s  total.  John  starts  by  rolling </p>
<p>a pathetic 3. He spends a Benny and rolls a 7. </p>
<p>The –2 for the task makes it 5 and the –2 for the </p>
<p>Complication  makes  it  a  3.  The  companion’s </p>
<p>cooperative roll brings it back up to 5—and saves </p>
<p>the day!</p>
<p>John  now  needs  four  more  successes  to </p>
<p>defuse the bomb. This round he draws a Joker—</p>
<p>breakthrough! “I’ve figured out the bomb-maker’s </p>
<p>pattern,” he says confidently and gets a raise on </p>
<p>his  Knowledge  (Demolitions)  roll.  Now  he  has </p>
<p>three total successes.</p>
<p>In the third round, John gets a Nine but fails his </p>
<p>roll. He starts to sweat.</p>
<p>Round four. John manages one success for a </p>
<p>running total of four. “I don’t know if I’m going to </p>
<p>make it!” he yells to the team. Soon after, John </p>
<p>takes a wound from one of the terrorists’ rifles, </p>
<p>leaving him Shaken and wounded!</p>
<p>It’s  the  fifth  round. The  terrorists  are  closing </p>
<p>in. John gets an Ace for his Action Card but fails </p>
<p>his Spirit roll to recover from being Shaken! He </p>
<p>spends his last Benny to eliminate the Shaken </p>
<p>result,  calls  for  help  from  his  friend  again,  and </p>
<p>makes  his  Knowledge  (Demolitions)  roll.  If  he </p>
<p>makes it, the nuke is defused. If he doesn’t—it’s </p>
<p>time to start a new campaign!</p>
<p>Example:.The.Ritual</p>
<p>The heroes are five investigators trying to stop </p>
<p>an ancient and terrifying creature from entering </p>
<p>our  world.  Four  of  the  team  battle  the  cultists </p>
<p>who summoned it in their underground lair. The </p>
<p>fifth, Professor Carter, begins to read a magical </p>
<p>incantation  he  believes  will  close  the  gate  and </p>
<p>send the thing back to whatever hell it came from.</p>
<p>In the first two rounds, the confident Dr. Carter </p>
<p>gets four total successes. In the third, things go </p>
<p>awry. He draws the unfortunate Seven of Clubs. </p>
<p>The  Game  Master  describes  a  sudden  surge </p>
<p>of  tentacles  and  amorphous  yet  angular  ooze </p>
<p>seeping into our world.</p>
<p>Professor Carter rolls at –2 and fails. “All hail </p>
<p>our new masters,” says the doctor just before the </p>
<p>Thing from another world devours him and begins </p>
<p>a reign of terror that lasts a thousand years.</p>
<p>Not every story has a happy ending. These are </p>
<p><i>savage</i> worlds, after all…</p>
<p>Fear</p>
<p>Certain  creatures  and  horrific  scenes  may </p>
<p>challenge a character’s resolve and cause them </p>
<p>to make a Fear check (a Spirit roll) when they </p>
<p>see them. A success on the Spirit roll means the </p>
<p>character manages to overcome the situation and </p>
<p>carry on. A failed Fear roll depends on whether </p>
<p>the cause of the roll was grotesque or terrifying </p>
<p>in nature.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Fear/Nausea:</b> If the scene was grotesque or </p>
<p>horrific, such as a grisly discovery or learning </p>
<p>a secret “Man Was Not Meant to Know,” the </p>
<p>character is Shaken and must make a Vigor </p>
<p>roll or suffer severe nausea/mental shock that </p>
<p>causes a level of Fatigue for the remainder </p>
<p>of the encounter. A natural 1 on the Spirit die </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--98</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>(regardless of the Wild Die) causes the victim </p>
<p>to roll on the Fright Table as well.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Terror:</b> A terrifying trigger, such as a monstrous </p>
<p>creature  or  unknowable  evil,  is  much  more </p>
<p>intense, and can rattle even the most stout-</p>
<p>hearted hero. Extras are typically Panicked. </p>
<p>Wild Cards must roll on the Fright Table should </p>
<p>they fail their Fear check. Roll 1d20 and add </p>
<p>the monster’s Fear penalty, if any, to the roll (a </p>
<p>–2 adds +2 to the roll, for example).</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Becoming  Jaded:</b> After  encountering  a </p>
<p>particular type of creature, the character shouldn’t </p>
<p>have to make Fear checks every time he sees </p>
<p>another one in that particular scenario. If the party </p>
<p>clears out a dungeon full of zombies, for example, </p>
<p>they should only have to roll the first time they </p>
<p>encounter them—not in every room. The Game </p>
<p>Master might require a roll if the heroes encounter </p>
<p>zombies in a particularly different or frightening </p>
<p>situation, however, such as if they fall into a pit </p>
<p>full of the ravenous creatures.</p>
<p>Fright.Table</p>
<p><b>  1d20* </b></p>
<p><b>Effect</b></p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>1-4 </b></p>
<p><b>Adrenaline Surge:</b> The hero’s “fight” </p>
<p>response takes over. He adds +2 to </p>
<p>all Trait and damage rolls on his next </p>
<p>action.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>5-8 </b></p>
<p><b>Shaken: </b>The character is Shaken.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>9-12 </b></p>
<p><b>Panicked:</b> The character immediately </p>
<p>moves his full Pace plus running die </p>
<p>away from the danger and is Shaken.</p>
<p><b>  13-16 </b></p>
<p><b>Minor  Phobia: </b> The  character  gains </p>
<p>a Minor Phobia Hindrance somehow </p>
<p>associated with the trauma.</p>
<p><b>  17-18 </b></p>
<p><b>Major Phobia: </b>The character gains a </p>
<p>Major Phobia Hindrance.</p>
<p><b>  19-20 </b></p>
<p><b>The Mark of Fear: </b>The hero is Shaken </p>
<p>and  also  suffers  some  cosmetic </p>
<p>physical  alteration—a  white  streak </p>
<p>forms  in  the  hero’s  hair,  his  eyes </p>
<p>twitch constantly, or some other minor </p>
<p>physical alteration. This reduces his </p>
<p>Charisma by 1.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>21+ </b></p>
<p><b>Heart  Attack:</b>  The  hero  is  so </p>
<p>overwhelmed with fear that his heart </p>
<p>stutters. He  becomes Incapacitated </p>
<p>and must make a Vigor roll at –2. If </p>
<p>successful,  he’s  Shaken  and  can’t </p>
<p>attempt  to  recover  for  1d4  rounds. </p>
<p>If he fails, he dies in 2d6 rounds. A </p>
<p>Healing roll at –4 saves the victim’s </p>
<p>life, but he remains Incapacitated.</p>
<p><i>*Add  the  creature’s  Fear  penalty  as  a </i></p>
<p><i>positive number to this roll.</i></p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--99</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p>Hazards</p>
<p>Heat,  cold,  hunger,  thirst,  lack  of  sleep,  and </p>
<p>other hazards can wear down even the hardiest </p>
<p>heroes,  sending  them  into  a  downward  spiral </p>
<p>that  can  lead  to  death  if  he  can’t  improve  his </p>
<p>situation.  Drowning,  fire,  and  falling  are  much </p>
<p>more immediate dangers, while others are more </p>
<p>insidious—such as poison and disease.</p>
<p>On the following pages are the most common </p>
<p>hazards, with details on when a character must </p>
<p>roll,  any  important  modifiers,  and  how  one </p>
<p>recovers from any effects he’s suffered from it. </p>
<p>Use these hazards mostly for dramatic purposes. </p>
<p>A quick trip to the outhouse through a blizzard isn’t </p>
<p>worth keeping up with, but a long trek through </p>
<p>the Mountains of Dread certainly is, especially if </p>
<p>it heightens the drama and makes the party think </p>
<p>about things they might otherwise ignore, such </p>
<p>as shelter, warm clothing, or eating their mounts </p>
<p>to stay alive.</p>
<p>Fatigue</p>
<p>Some hazards cause direct damage, but most </p>
<p>cause Fatigue—stress or weakness that makes </p>
<p>a victim less effective and can eventually lead to </p>
<p>Incapacitation or even death.</p>
<p>A character who falls victim to Fatigue passes </p>
<p>through  several  declining  states  before  finally </p>
<p>succumbing to his stress. Each of these states </p>
<p>and the penalties they incur are described below.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Fatigued: </b>The hero is tiring quickly. All of his </p>
<p>Trait checks suffer a –1 modifier. If he suffers </p>
<p>Fatigue again, he becomes Exhausted.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Exhausted:</b>  The  hero  is  fading  fast.  He </p>
<p>suffers –2 to all Trait rolls. If he suffers Fatigue </p>
<p>again, he’s Incapacitated unless the hazard </p>
<p>description says otherwise.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitated:</b> The  victim  collapses  and  is </p>
<p>Incapacitated.  See  Incapacitation  Effects </p>
<p>under each Hazard for what happens next. </p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b>  Recovering  from  Fatigue  varies </p>
<p>depending on its source. Severe hunger requires </p>
<p>food, cold requires warmth, and so on. Exactly </p>
<p>how Fatigue is relieved depends on how it was </p>
<p>acquired. This is explained under each Hazard.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Multiple  Hazards:</b>  It  doesn’t  matter  what </p>
<p>the source of Fatigue is. If a hero who’s already </p>
<p>Exhausted  from  hunger  then  suffers  a  level  of </p>
<p>Fatigue  from  cold,  he  becomes  Incapacitated. </p>
<p>A  character  suffering  from  multiple  sources  of </p>
<p>Fatigue must address <i>both</i> to recover.</p>
<p>Bumps.and.Bruises</p>
<p>Characters  who  suffer  minor  but  troubling </p>
<p>injuries,  such  as  stumbling  down  a  slope  or </p>
<p>running through a cavern in the dark, can suffer </p>
<p>from Fatigue rather than suffering actual wounds. </p>
<p>This is the hazard to use when characters should </p>
<p>be beat up a bit but not truly wounded.</p>
<p>When  a  character  suffers  injuries  like  these </p>
<p>he must make a Vigor roll. Those who fail gain </p>
<p>a Fatigue level from bumps, bruises, cuts, and </p>
<p>scrapes. The  Game  Master  may  occasionally </p>
<p>allow sure-footed characters to make Agility rolls </p>
<p>to avoid this damage instead. Fatigue gained in </p>
<p>this way can lead to Exhaustion, but not to being </p>
<p>Incapacitated.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery: </b> Fatigue  levels  from  Bumps  and </p>
<p>Bruises  automatically  improve  one  step  24 </p>
<p>hours after the original injuries were suffered.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation Effects:</b> None. A victim cannot </p>
<p>be Incapacitated from Bumps and Bruises.</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b> Two heroes race down a steep </i></p>
<p><i>slope to escape the clutches of some angry </i></p>
<p><i>crocodile cultists. The Game Master decides </i></p>
<p><i>they trip and suffer Bumps and Bruises if they </i></p>
<p><i>don’t make Agility rolls. The first succeeds but </i></p>
<p><i>the second fails and suffers Fatigue for the </i></p>
<p><i>next 24 hours.</i></p>
<p>Cold</p>
<p>Trudging through deep snow for hours on end, </p>
<p>or facing biting, bitter winds, can dehydrate and </p>
<p>fatigue a character as quickly as blazing deserts. </p>
<p>Every four hours spent in weather below freezing </p>
<p>(32° F), a character must make a Vigor roll. Failure </p>
<p>means the victim gains a Fatigue level. Subtract </p>
<p>1 from the victim’s Vigor roll for every 20 degrees </p>
<p>below freezing to a maximum of –3.</p>
<p>The roll assumes the character is wearing warm </p>
<p>clothing. If not, subtract 2 from the total. Modern </p>
<p>winter gear adds +2, and advanced gear (arctic </p>
<p>suits) add at least +4 or more depending on the </p>
<p>setting.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b>  Warmth  and  shelter  from  the </p>
<p>elements allows a hero to recover a Fatigue </p>
<p>level every 30 minutes.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation  Effects:</b>  Make  a  Vigor  roll </p>
<p>every hour or perish.</p>
<p>Disease</p>
<p>Diseases cover a wide range of maladies, from </p>
<p>long-term  debilitating  illnesses  to  those  which </p>
<p>might  cause  immediate  spasms  or  death.  To </p>
<p>handle such a diverse range of diseases, we’ve </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--100</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>broken them down into those most likely to come </p>
<p>into play in a typical game or campaign session. </p>
<p>If you’re trying to model a specific disease, you’ll </p>
<p>want to adjust the rules to better reflect that illness’ </p>
<p>symptoms.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b>  Unless  the  disease  description </p>
<p>says  otherwise,  it  can  only  be  treated  with </p>
<p>specific medicines or very powerful magic. If </p>
<p>the proper medicine is available, all the victim’s </p>
<p>ailments  vanish  in  2d6  days  minus  half  his </p>
<p>Vigor die type, to a minimum of one day. If not, </p>
<p>healers can make the victim more comfortable </p>
<p>but have no further effect on his condition. For </p>
<p>magical healing, see page 133.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation Effects:</b> Typically death unless </p>
<p>the text details a different result.</p>
<p>Contracting.Diseases</p>
<p>Diseases  may  be  acquired  in  one  of  the </p>
<p>following ways:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Airborne:</b> The toxin is in the air. If a character </p>
<p>is aware of the toxin he can hold his breath for </p>
<p>a number of rounds equal to 2 plus his Vigor </p>
<p>die, or half that if he wasn’t prepared. After that </p>
<p>he must breathe and automatically contracts </p>
