<p>the disease. If the victim is surprised by the </p>
<p>release of the disease (such as a creature with </p>
<p>exploding spores), he must make a Smarts </p>
<p>roll to hold his breath before the tainted air </p>
<p>enters his lungs.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Touch:</b> The  victim  must  make  a  Vigor  roll </p>
<p>immediately on being touched. If successful, </p>
<p>the disease didn’t manage to “take” and there </p>
<p>is no effect. Failure means the disease takes </p>
<p>immediate effect as described below.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Induction:</b>  The  disease  must  enter  the </p>
<p>bloodstream,  most  typically  by  an  animal’s </p>
<p>bite, or a cut by an infected weapon. In these </p>
<p>cases, if a victim is Shaken or wounded by </p>
<p>such an attack, he must make a Vigor roll. If </p>
<p>failed, he contracts the disease. If successful, </p>
<p>the  disease  didn’t  manage  to  get  into  the </p>
<p>bloodstream and there is no further effect.</p>
<p>Disease.Types</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Long-Term  Chronic,  Majorly  Debilitating:</b> </p>
<p>These  diseases  cause  constant  irritation </p>
<p>and exhaustion and eventually end in death. </p>
<p>Leprosy,  untreated  tuberculosis,  and  similar </p>
<p>fatal diseases fall into this category. Victims </p>
<p>have frequent spasms and coughing fits and </p>
<p>so  are  always  Exhausted.  If  they  suffer  an </p>
<p>additional Fatigue level, they are Incapacitated </p>
<p>but  do  not  die. At  the  start  of  every  game </p>
<p>session,  the  character  must  make  a  Vigor </p>
<p>roll. If his total is ever a 1 or less, he’s going </p>
<p>to pass away before the end of that session. </p>
<p>The  Game  Master  is  encouraged  to  let  the </p>
<p>hero go out with style if possible, but he will </p>
<p>perish before the game ends this time.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Long-Term  Chronic,  Minorly  Debilitating:</b> </p>
<p>This  works  exactly  as  above  except  the </p>
<p>unfortunate hero is constantly Fatigued rather </p>
<p>than  Exhausted.  Examples  of  this  kind  of </p>
<p>disease  include  malaria,  fantasy  diseases </p>
<p>such as the touch of certain undead, or living </p>
<p>in a foreign and slightly toxic environment.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Short Term, Debilitating:</b> These are extremely </p>
<p>rare in the real world but are very appropriate </p>
<p>for  fantasy  or  science-fiction  settings. They </p>
<p>are  typically  acquired  from  the  scratch  of  a </p>
<p>creature  or  breathing  air  laden  with  toxins </p>
<p>(see Airborne and Induction, above). A victim </p>
<p>who  fails  his  Vigor  roll  and  contracts  the </p>
<p>disease suffers Fatigue and is Shaken as he </p>
<p>begins  to  cough  and  wretch  uncontrollably. </p>
<p>Once he recovers from being Shaken he may </p>
<p>act normally but the Fatigue level (and thus </p>
<p>the disease) remains for 2d6 days while the </p>
<p>sickness works itself out.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Short  Term,  Lethal:</b>  Even  more  rare  are </p>
<p>diseases that can kill in seconds. Treat this </p>
<p>exactly  as  Lethal  Poison  (see  Poison).  If  a </p>
<p>character  survives  but  suffers  Fatigue  from </p>
<p>the poison, it lasts 2d6 hours.</p>
<p>Drowning</p>
<p>Water is deadly to those who aren’t prepared </p>
<p>for it. Here are some standard water hazards and </p>
<p>how often a character must make a Swimming roll, </p>
<p>with each failure adding a Fatigue level. </p>
<p>•  A character with at least a d4 in Swimming does </p>
<p>not have to roll when in calm water.</p>
<p>•  In  rough  water,  all  characters  must  make  a </p>
<p>Swimming roll every minute.</p>
<p>•  In white water, the hero is swept into eddies and </p>
<p>whirlpools (hydraulics) and rolls every round.</p>
<p>•  A hero forced to tread water for long periods </p>
<p>without a flotation device must roll once every </p>
<p>hour.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Modifiers:</b> Subtract –2 from a hero’s Swimming </p>
<p>rolls if he is trying to hold something up, including </p>
<p>another character. Add 2 to the roll if he’s wearing </p>
<p>a life vest.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b>  Once  a  character  is  out  of  the </p>
<p>water,  he  recovers  one  Fatigue  level  every </p>
<p>five minutes.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--101</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation Effects:</b> Death in a number </p>
<p>of rounds equal to half the victim’s Vigor die. </p>
<p>If someone can get to the victim within five </p>
<p>minutes  of  “death,”  he  can  be  resuscitated </p>
<p>with a Healing roll at –4.</p>
<p>Falling</p>
<p>Falling  damage  is  1d6+1  per  10  feet  fallen </p>
<p>(round up), to a maximum of 10d6+10. If you’re </p>
<p>playing on the table-top, treat every 2” as 10’ for </p>
<p>simplicity.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Snow:</b> Particularly soft ground, such as deep </p>
<p>snow, acts as a cushion. Every foot of soft snow </p>
<p>reduces damage by one point.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Water:</b> A fall into water reduces the number </p>
<p>of  dice  rolled  by  half  (rounded  down),  and  an </p>
<p>Agility roll means the character dives and takes no </p>
<p>damage at all, though he is automatically Shaken </p>
<p>if he dives from a height of greater than 50’. A fall </p>
<p>over 15” (30 yards) requires an Agility roll at –2 </p>
<p>to avoid damage.</p>
<p>Fire</p>
<p>Even giants impervious to the puny weapons of </p>
<p>sword-slingers are often afraid of flame and fire. </p>
<p>Roll the damage listed below when a character </p>
<p>is first burned and at the beginning of each round </p>
<p>until he is free of the flame. Only sealed, fireproof </p>
<p>armor  adds  to  a  character’s Toughness  when </p>
<p>resisting the effects of fire.</p>
<p>Fire.Damage</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>Damage </b></p>
<p><b>Description</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>Burning weapon</p>
<p> </p>
<p>1d10 </p>
<p>“Spot fire,” such as a burning arm </p>
<p>or leg</p>
<p> </p>
<p>2d10 </p>
<p>Flamethrower</p>
<p> </p>
<p>3d10 </p>
<p>Lava</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Spreading:</b> Anytime  something  flammable </p>
<p>is hit by fire, roll 1d6. On a 6, the target catches </p>
<p>fire. Very flammable targets, such as a scarecrow, </p>
<p>catch  fire  on  a  4-6.  Volatile  targets,  such  as  a </p>
<p>person soaked in gasoline, catch fire on anything </p>
<p><i>but </i>a 1.</p>
<p>Each round after a victim catches fire (at the </p>
<p>beginning of his action), roll as if checking to see </p>
<p>if the victim catches fire again. If he does, the fire </p>
<p>grows  in  intensity  and  does  the  damage  listed </p>
<p>above each round.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Smoke  Inhalation:</b>  Fires  in  confined  areas </p>
<p>produce deadly smoke. Every round a character </p>
<p>is in such an environment, he must make a Vigor </p>
<p>roll. A wet cloth over the face adds +2 to the roll, </p>
<p>and  a  gas  mask  negates  the  need  for  the  roll </p>
<p>entirely. If the roll is failed, the character gains a </p>
<p>Fatigue level.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--102</p>
<p>Heat</p>
<p>Intense  heat,  typically  that  over  90  degrees </p>
<p>Fahrenheit,  can  cause  heat  exhaustion  and </p>
<p>heat stroke, both of which are very dangerous. </p>
<p>The actual danger is from dehydration, so well-</p>
<p>supplied and conscientious characters can greatly </p>
<p>improve their chances in extreme heat simply by </p>
<p>carrying  a  good  amount  of  water  and  drinking </p>
<p>frequently.</p>
<p>When the temperature reaches 90 degrees or </p>
<p>more, the GM should pay attention to how much </p>
<p>water  characters  are  able  to  drink.  If  they  are </p>
<p>able to drink at least four quarts of water a day, </p>
<p>they may be tired and sunburned, but are in no </p>
<p>immediate danger.</p>
<p>If that amount of water isn’t available, characters </p>
<p>must make Vigor rolls every four hours. Subtract </p>
<p>two from the roll if the hero has half the water he </p>
<p>needs, and subtract 4 if he has less than half. </p>
<p>Failure means the character gains one level of </p>
<p>Fatigue.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Subtract 1 from the roll for each additional </p>
<p>5 degrees above 90 degrees Fahrenheit.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Add +1 if the hero stops all physical activity.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Add  +1  to  the  roll  if  the  character  has </p>
<p>substantial shade.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b> A  victim  who  receives  sufficient </p>
<p>water recovers one Fatigue level every hour.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation  Effects:</b> An  Incapacitated </p>
<p>character suffers heat stroke and may suffer </p>
<p>brain  damage.  Make  a  Vigor  roll.  If  the  roll </p>
<p>is  failed,  the  victim’s  Smarts  and  Strength </p>
<p>decrease  by  one  step  permanently  (to  a </p>
<p>minimum of d4).</p>
<p>Hunger</p>
<p>Humans  need  approximately  one  pound  of </p>
<p>food  every  24  hours.  If  sufficient  sustenance </p>
<p>isn’t available, a character begins to suffer from </p>
<p>severe hunger.</p>
<p>Starting the first day after the meal was missed, </p>
<p>the character must make a Vigor roll. Subtract 2 </p>
<p>if the hero has less than half the required amount </p>
<p>of  food.  Failure  means  the  character  gains  a </p>
<p>Fatigue level.</p>
<p>After  the  first  day,  the  character  must  roll  for </p>
<p>hunger every 12 hours, and thirst every 6 hours.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>The Survival Skill: </b>A successful Survival roll </p>
<p>each day provides enough food and water for one </p>
<p>person, or enough for five with a raise.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b> At least a pound of decent food </p>
<p>allows a character to recover a Fatigue level </p>
<p>every hour, or every 12 hours if he reached </p>
<p>Incapacitated.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation  Effects:</b>  A  character </p>
<p>Incapacitated by hunger dies 3d6 hours later.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--103</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p>Poison</p>
<p>Poisons  can  be  delivered  many  ways.  If </p>
<p>ingested, its effects occur automatically. If a victim </p>
<p>is Shaken or wounded by a bite or weapon coated </p>
<p>in poison, he must make an immediate Vigor roll </p>
<p>against the type of poison listed on the Poison </p>
<p>Effects Table below. If the poison has a modifier </p>
<p>associated with it (such as Venomous [–2]), the </p>
<p>victim’s Vigor roll is modified appropriately.</p>
<p>The Game Master must decide which category </p>
<p>is appropriate  based  on  the poison.  Most real-</p>
<p>world  animals  are  Venomous  with  only  a  few </p>
<p>falling in the Lethal category. Ghouls might have </p>
<p>paralyzing poison in their claws, while spies might </p>
<p>use Knockout poison in drinks or gases.</p>
<p>Characters who suffer Paralysis or a Knockout </p>
<p>are Incapacitated until they recover.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Treatment: </b> To  treat  a  poisoned  victim,  the </p>
<p>healer can try a Healing roll minus the strength </p>
<p>of  the  poison  itself  (found  in  the  poison’s </p>
<p>description).  If  successful,  the  victim’s  life  is </p>
<p>saved and the poison no longer has any effect—</p>
<p>paralyzed victims can move and those rendered </p>
<p>unconscious wake.</p>
<p>Each character may only attempt one Healing </p>
<p>roll per incident to cure the poison, but another </p>
<p>character  with  Healing  may  make  a  second </p>
<p>attempt, and so on. </p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b> Fatigue leves from poison recover </p>
<p>in 24 hours.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation:</b>  Characters  Incapacitated </p>
<p>by accrued Fatigue recover as above. Other </p>
<p>effects  from  the  table  below  are  handled </p>
<p>separately.</p>
<p>Poison.Effects.Table</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Lethal  Poison:</b>  Failure  results  in  death  in </p>
<p>2d6 rounds. Success results in 1 wound and </p>
<p>Exhaustion.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Venomous Poison:</b> Failure causes death in </p>
<p>2d6 minutes. Success causes 1 wound and </p>
<p>Exhaustion.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Paralysis Poison:</b> Paralyzed for 2d6 minutes </p>
<p>if failed, or 2d6 rounds with success.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Knockout Poison:</b> Knocked out for 2d6 hours </p>
<p>with failure, or 2d6 minutes with success.</p>
<p>Radiation</p>
<p>Characters in a radioactive environment must </p>
<p>make a Vigor roll every hour spent in low radiation, </p>
<p>and every minute in high radiation. Each failure </p>
<p>results in a Fatigue level.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b> Fatigue from radiation fades at the </p>
<p>rate of one level every 24 hours, or half that if </p>
<p>the victim can shower or scrub away lingering </p>
<p>dust and other contaminants.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation Effects:</b> The victim contracts </p>
<p>radiation  sickness,  a  Long-Term  Chronic, </p>
<p>Minorly  Debilitating  disease,  as  explained </p>
<p>under <b>Disease</b>.</p>
<p>Sleep</p>
<p>Most  people  need  a  minimum  of  six  hours </p>
<p>sleep  out  of  every  24. A  character  who  goes </p>
<p>without this amount of sleep must make a Vigor </p>
<p>roll at a cumulative –2 every 12 hours thereafter </p>
<p>(maximum  penalty  of  –6). A  large  amount  of </p>
<p>coffee, soda, or other stimulant adds +2 to the roll.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b> Every four hours of restful sleep </p>
<p>removes a level of Fatigue.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation  Effects:</b> A  character  who </p>
<p>suffers this much Fatigue simply falls into a </p>
<p>deep sleep for 2d10 hours.</p>
<p>Thirst</p>
<p>An average-sized man requires two quarts of </p>
<p>water a day. This requirement is doubled in both </p>
<p>very dry conditions (such as the desert) or areas </p>
<p>of  high  humidity  (the  jungle)  as  the  character </p>
<p>perspires constantly and begins to dehydrate.</p>
<p>If enough water isn’t available, the hero begins </p>
<p>to suffer from dehydration. Starting the first day </p>
<p>after the water runs out, he must make a Vigor </p>
<p>roll. Subtract 2 if the hero has less than half the </p>
<p>required  amount.  Failure  means  the  character </p>
<p>gains  a  Fatigue  level. After  the  first  day,  the </p>
<p>character must make the required Vigor roll every </p>
<p>6 hours.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>The Survival Skill: </b>A successful Survival roll </p>
<p>each day provides enough water (and food) for </p>
<p>one person, or enough for five with a raise.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Recovery:</b>  Two  quarts  of  water  allows  a </p>
<p>character to recover a Fatigue level every hour.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Incapacitation Effects:</b> Death in 2d6 hours.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--104</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--105</p>
<p>Interludes</p>
<p>In  most  heroic  tales,  characters  engage  in </p>
<p>small  talk  that  reveals  something  about  their </p>
<p>past or further develops their personality. Such </p>
<p>“Interludes”  are  rare  in  roleplaying  games </p>
<p>where we focus primarily on action and the next </p>
<p>encounter.</p>
<p>The system below formulizes these scenes and </p>
<p>rewards players for roleplaying their character and </p>
<p>revealing their backstory to the rest of the group.</p>
<p>Running.an.Interlude.</p>
<p>The Game Master should run an Interlude during </p>
<p>natural down-time such as when healing up after </p>
<p>a fight, performing research, or between legs of </p>
<p>a long trip.</p>
<p>To start, pick a player and have her draw a card. </p>
<p>The Suit determines the general topic as shown on </p>
<p>the Interlude Table below. Each entry requires the </p>
<p>player to tell a story in the voice of her character. </p>
<p>The tale should be substantial enough to take a </p>
<p>few minutes and may draw in other characters’ </p>
<p>participation as well.</p>
<p>The  next  time  the  Game  Master  feels  an </p>
<p>Interlude  is  appropriate,  he  should  pick  a </p>
<p>different player so that everyone has a chance to </p>
<p>participate and reap the rewards.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b> Rewards:</b> After a player completes her tale, </p>
<p>award her a Benny or an Adventure Card—her </p>
<p>choice.</p>
<p>Interlude.Table</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Clubs—Tragedy:</b> Describe a tale of tragedy </p>
<p>or misfortune from your hero’s past, featuring </p>
<p>one of his Hindrances if possible. If the teller </p>
<p>has a dark secret of some kind, hint strongly </p>
<p>at it, drop clues, or otherwise give the rest of </p>
<p>the group a glimpse into your hero’s dark side </p>
<p>during your narrative.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Spades—Victory:</b> Tell the group about a great </p>
<p>victory or personal triumph in your adventurer’s </p>
<p>past.  How  did  it  affect  him  afterward?  Was </p>
<p>there a reward?</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Hearts—Love:</b> Speak fondly of the character’s </p>
<p>greatest love—lost, found, or waiting on him </p>
<p>back home. What is her name? Where does </p>
<p>she live? Why is the traveler not with her now?</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Diamonds—Desire:</b>  Tell  a  tale  about </p>
<p>something your hero wants (or already has). </p>
<p>It might be a material possession, recognition, </p>
<p>a political goal, or even a trip he wishes to take </p>
<p>to some amazing destination.</p>
<p>Example:.Crossing.Mars</p>
<p>Colonel Green of the Martian 24th Highlanders, </p>
<p>his  guide  Sanjay  of  the  Gurkhas,  Lady  Emily, </p>
<p>and  big  game  hunter  extraordinaire  Sir  John </p>
<p>Hobbsworth cross the vast Martian desert. They </p>
<p>face sandstorms, intense heat by day, and bone-</p>
<p>numbing cold at night.</p>
<p>The Game Master decides to call for an Interlude </p>
<p>after each encounter along the way.</p>
<p>Colonel Green goes first and draws a Spade. </p>
<p>He describes with great vigor his travails against </p>
<p>the wild Martians at Zimkangaroon and the tense </p>
<p>battle he fought there. “It’s how I got this injury,” </p>
<p>he says as he points to his Lame leg.</p>
<p>Later, after surviving a terrible sandstorm, Lady </p>
<p>Emily draws a Heart. “I will withstand any trauma,” </p>
<p>she begins, “if only it will help me discover what </p>
<p>happened  to  my  beloved  Nathaniel,  lost  in  the </p>
<p>mountains I have asked you all to escort me to.” </p>
<p>She then goes on about how she and Nathaniel </p>
<p>met, traveled to Mars, and began a new life far </p>
<p>away from the grime and corruption of New York </p>
<p>City.</p>
<p>A day later, the party is ambushed by Martian </p>
<p>raiders. Sanjay draws the next card—a Diamond. </p>
<p>“See this bandit blade?” he says proudly. “It is a </p>
<p>poor substitute for my kukri, awarded ten years </p>
<p>ago when I joined the regiment.” Sanjay goes on </p>
<p>to describe his training and some of the terrible </p>
<p>battles he fought in the 6th Gurkha </p>
<p>regiment  before  volunteering  for </p>
<p>service on faraway Mars.</p>
<p>Sir  Hobbsworth  is  next,  with  a </p>
<p>Club.  “I  have  hunted  every </p>
<p>creature on earth. Now I’ve </p>
<p>come  to  Mars  to  bag  the </p>
<p>biggest,  most  ferocious </p>
<p>beast  on  all  the  known </p>
<p>worlds—the crask. I’ll kill </p>
<p>the thing and mount its </p>
<p>head  on  my  wall. And </p>
<p><i>nothing</i> will stop me.” </p>
<p>Hobbsworth  then </p>
<p>hints that he hopes </p>
<p>the  monster  slays </p>
<p>him as he slays it—</p>
<p>revealing  his  Death </p>
<p>Wish  and  the  chronic </p>
<p>disease  he  picked  up </p>
<p>during  the  campaign  in </p>
<p>the process.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--106</p>
<p>Mass.Battles</p>
<p>Truly  savage  worlds  often  feature  wars  with </p>
<p>massive and bloody battles. The system below </p>
<p>rewards players who not only take part in these </p>
<p>battles, but are instrumental in gathering political </p>
<p>support, recruiting troops, and deciding strategies </p>
<p>that win them.</p>
<p>These rules allow the Game Master to handle </p>
<p>everything from a small warband holding a fort </p>
<p>against an undead horde to full divisions of troops </p>
<p>fighting  a  massive  field  battle  or  a  planetary </p>
<p>assault  from  a  space-based  invasion  fleet.  It’s </p>
<p>abstract, but provides a narrative base for heroes </p>
<p>to plan, get involved, and even become involved </p>
<p>in the fight themselves.</p>
<p>Setup</p>
<p>To start, give the larger or more powerful army </p>
<p>10 tokens. Give the opposing army a proportional </p>
<p>number of tokens. If one army has 10,000 men, </p>
<p>for example, and the other has 7,000, give the </p>
<p>smaller army seven tokens.</p>
<p>The Game Master will need to adjust for special </p>
<p>or elite troops, better equipment, and so on. It’s </p>
<p>not important to count every point of armor and </p>
<p>damage  point—just  get  close  enough  to  give </p>
<p>a  reasonable  approximation  of  strength,  adjust </p>
<p>where  needed,  and  trust  in  the  dice  and  the </p>
<p>player’s actions to handle the rest.</p>
<p>Knowledge.(Battle)</p>
<p>Once the odds are determined, both sides make </p>
<p>opposed Knowledge (Battle) rolls, modified by any </p>
<p>of the circumstances below:</p>
<p>Battle.Modifiers</p>
<p><b>Modifier </b></p>
<p><b>Circumstance</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>+1 </p>
<p>The side with more tokens adds +1 for </p>
<p>every token it has more than the foe </p>
<p>each round</p>
<p><b>Artillery or Air Support</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>+1 </p>
<p>Light</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>Medium</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+3 </p>
<p>Heavy</p>
<p><b>Terrain</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>–1 </p>
<p>Foe has slight advantage</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Foe has minor advantage</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–3 </p>
<p>Foe has major advantage</p>
<p><b>Battle Plan—GM’s Call determined each round</b></p>
<p>  +/–? </p>
<p>The army gains a penalty or a bonus </p>
<p>depending  on  the  tactical  decisions  made  by </p>
<p>the leader each round. Springing a hidden flank </p>
<p>attack, sending reserves to a crucial front, and </p>
<p>so on, all add to the Battle roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Casualties:</b> Each success and raise causes </p>
<p>the other side to lose one token. Casualties are </p>
<p>generally distributed evenly throughout the force.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Morale:</b> Each round in which an army loses </p>
<p>a token, its leader must check morale. This is a </p>
<p>Spirit roll modified by the circumstances below. </p>
<p>With  a  success,  the  army  fights  on  and  both </p>
<p>sides  make  another  Knowledge  (Battle)  roll  as </p>
<p>described above.</p>
<p>Failure  means  the  army  is  defeated  but  the </p>
<p>troops conduct an orderly retreat. Make one more </p>
<p>Battle Roll and then end the fight. (A leader may </p>
<p>voluntarily choose this option as well.) </p>
<p>On a result of 1 or less, the troops rout and the </p>
<p>battle is over immediately.</p>
<p>Morale.Modifiers</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>Mod </b></p>
<p><b>Situation</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>–1 </p>
<p>For each token lost in battle so far</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>The army is made up predominantly </p>
<p>(75% or more) of undead or other </p>
<p>fearless drones</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>The army is within major fortifications, </p>
<p>such  as  a  fortress  or  prepared </p>
<p>positions</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>The army cannot retreat</p>
<p>Characters.in.Mass.Battles</p>
<p>The heroes of your campaign aren’t likely to sit </p>
<p>idly by while war rages around them. Fighters may </p>
<p>want to charge the gates, wizards unleash fireballs </p>
<p>into the masses, and a gallant knight may ride his </p>
<p>trusty warhorse directly into the enemy formations.</p>
<p>Those who want to get involved can dramatically </p>
<p>affect  the  results  of  the  battle.  Have  each </p>
<p>character make a Fighting, Shooting, or arcane </p>
<p>skill roll (their choice) each action and consult the </p>
<p>Character Results below.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Modifiers: </b> Add  or  subtract  the  number  of </p>
<p>tokens difference between the heroes’ side and </p>
<p>their opponent. If the enemy has 8 tokens and </p>
<p>the heroes have 5, for example, the heroes must </p>
<p>subtract –3 from their attack rolls.</p>
<p>Also add +1 to the roll for each Rank a hero has </p>
<p>above Novice to account for his various Edges and </p>
<p>abilities that aren’t reflected in a straight skill roll.</p>
<p>Character.Battle.Results</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Failure:</b> The hero is stopped by overwhelming </p>
<p>numbers, an unfortunate tactical development, </p>
<p>or bad fortune. He suffers 4d6 damage.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Success:</b> The hero fights well and adds +1 </p>
<p>to  his  side’s  Battle  Roll,  though  he  suffers </p>
<p>3d6 damage.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--107</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Raise: </b>The hero wreaks havoc, slaying enemy </p>
<p>leaders and destroying important assets. He </p>
<p>suffers 2d6 damage, but adds +2 to his side’s </p>
<p>Battle Roll.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Two  Raises:</b> The  warrior  covers  himself  in </p>
<p>glory! Scores of foes fall before him and his </p>
<p>success inspires his allies to fight like demons. </p>
<p>His efforts add +2 to his side’s Battle Roll and </p>
<p>he emerges from the fight unscathed.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Ammo:</b> If it’s important to track, each round a </p>
<p>hero enters the fray and uses his Shooting or an </p>
<p>arcane skill (such as Spellcasting), he expends </p>
<p>some of his ammunition or Power Points.</p>
<p>Arcane types use 2d6 Power Points per round. </p>
<p>Characters with ranged weapons use 3d6 shots </p>
<p>for ranged weapons (triple that for weapons that </p>
<p>usually fire bursts or full-auto). If the hero winds </p>
<p>up without any ammo or Power Points she’ll have </p>
<p>to change tactics for the next round.</p>
<p>Battle.Aftermath</p>
<p>When  one  side  routs,  retreats,  or  runs  out </p>
<p>of  tokens,  the  battle  ends  and  casualties  are </p>
<p>removed. Some of the troops lost in the battle can </p>
<p>be recovered as they regroup or receive first aid.</p>
<p>If it’s important to know how things stand after </p>
<p>the fight, roll 1d6 for each token lost. The victor </p>
<p>recovers a lost token on a roll of 4-6. The loser </p>
<p>recovers a lost token on a roll of 5-6. A routed army </p>
<p>recovers tokens only on a roll of 6.</p>
<p>After the battle and once both sides have figured </p>
<p>out how many tokens they lost, remove the actual </p>
<p>casualties from their armies. The side that started </p>
<p>with the larger force simply multiplies the number </p>
<p>of  tokens  lost  by  10%.  Losing  four  tokens,  for </p>
<p>example,  means  the  entire  army  suffered  40% </p>
<p>casualties. The side with the smaller force must </p>
<p>figure out what percentage of his men are left. If </p>
<p>he started with 8 tokens and ends up with only 2, </p>
<p>for example, he’s lost 75% of his force.</p>
<p>Once  you’ve  figured  the  total  percentage  of </p>
<p>casualties  to  your  army,  distribute  the  losses </p>
<p>among specific units as evenly as possible.</p>
<p>Example:.The.Invasion.of.Earth</p>
<p>The insectoid Gorram Empire is conducting a </p>
<p>planetary assault of Earth. The Gorram have 10 </p>
<p>tokens that represent their massive invasion fleet </p>
<p>and armored walkers with fiery death rays. Earth </p>
<p>has  all  the  world’s  militaries  at  their  disposal. </p>
<p>Given the superiority of Gorram technology and </p>
<p>greater  numbers,  the  GM  decides  the  Earth </p>
<p>Defense Force (EDF) gets 8 tokens.</p>
<p>Both sides have heavy artillery and air support </p>
<p>(+3  to  both  sides),  but  there  are  no  terrain </p>
<p>advantages since it’s a world-wide battle.</p>
<p>The  Gorram  Empire  makes  a  Knowledge </p>
<p>(Battle) roll and gets a 13. The player characters </p>
<p>load up in stratospheric fighters and lend a hand. </p>
<p>Collectively, they suffer some damage but also </p>
<p>add  a  grand  total  of  +3  to  the  roll. The  EDF’s </p>
<p>Knowledge (Battle) final total is 17—Earth wins </p>
<p>by 4. That destroys two of the invaders’ tokens </p>
<p>and 20% of their force.</p>
<p>The Game Master describes the first few hours </p>
<p>of the invasion and a spirited defense by earth’s </p>
<p>militaries. The  evil  insectoids  are  dealt  heavy </p>
<p>losses, but are far from defeated, and the battle </p>
<p>goes on.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--108</p>
<p>Setting.Rules</p>
<p>There are many Savage Worlds with settings </p>
<p>ranging  from  dark  and  gritty  detective  tales  to </p>
<p>cinematic epics to political thrillers. These rules </p>
<p>provide a great framework for anything you want </p>
<p>to do but adding the right setting rules really bring </p>
<p>the world and the action to life.</p>
<p>Blood.&amp;.Guts</p>
<p>Characters  can  spend  Bennies  on  damage </p>
<p>rolls!  Use  this rule  when  you  really  want to up </p>
<p>the carnage.</p>
<p>Born.A.Hero</p>
<p>During character creation, heroes may ignore </p>
<p>the  Rank  qualifications  for  Edges. They  must </p>
<p>still have any other requirements as usual. The </p>
<p>usual rules for Rank requirements apply after the </p>
<p>character is created.</p>
<p>Critical.Failures</p>
<p>This  rule  works  well  for  very  dark  or  very </p>
<p>humorous games. When a character rolls double </p>
<p>1’s on a Trait roll, he can’t spend a Benny—he’s </p>
<p>stuck with the critical failure.</p>
<p>Fanatics</p>
<p>Use this rule in pulp-style games where villains </p>
<p>are  larger  than  life.  When  a  Wild  Card  enemy </p>
<p>character is hit by a successful attack, any of his </p>
<p>henchmen, goons, or other allies jumps in front of </p>
<p>his master and suffers the attack instead.</p>
<p>Gritty.Damage</p>
<p>This variation on damage works well for settings </p>
<p>such  as  gritty  detective  scenarios  or  “realistic” </p>
<p>military adventures. It can be very lethal so use </p>
<p>it cautiously.</p>
<p>Treat Extras’ wounds normally.</p>
<p>For Wild Cards, count wounds as usual and go </p>
<p>through the normal steps for Incapacitation should </p>
<p>he accumulate more than three wounds.</p>
<p>In addition, every time the hero suffers a wound, </p>
<p>roll  on  the  Injury  Table  and  apply  the  results </p>
<p>immediately  (but  roll  only  once  per  incident </p>
<p>regardless  of  how  many  wounds  are  actually </p>
<p>caused). A  hero  who  takes  2  wounds  from  an </p>
<p>attack, for example, still only suffers one roll on </p>
<p>the Injury Table.</p>
<p>Injuries sustained in this way are cured when </p>
<p>the  wound  is  healed.  (Injuries  sustained  via </p>
<p>Incapacitation may be temporary or permanent </p>
<p>as usual.)</p>
<p>A  Shaken  character  who’s  Shaken  a  second </p>
<p>time from a damaging attack receives a wound as </p>
<p>usual but does <i>not</i> have to roll on the Injury Table.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--109</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p><i><b>Example:</b>  Sergeant  Trotter  is  a  hero  in </i></p>
<p><i>Weird  Wars.  He  takes  a  wound  from  a </i></p>
<p><i>German sniper, doesn’t Soak it, and so has </i></p>
<p><i>to roll on the Injury Table. He rolls a 10—Leg. </i></p>
<p><i>The GM rolls a die and decides it’s the left </i></p>
<p><i>leg. Trotter is now Lame. If he takes another </i></p>
<p><i>wound to that leg, he’d have the One Leg </i></p>
<p><i>Hindrance. Further wounds to the same leg </i></p>
<p><i>add to his wound total as usual but have no </i></p>
<p><i>further effect as there’s nothing beyond the </i></p>
<p><i>One Leg Hindrance.</i></p>
<p><i>Later,  the  unfortunate  Sarge  takes  two </i></p>
<p><i>wounds to the Guts. The Game Master rolls </i></p>
<p><i>once and gets the Battered result, reducing </i></p>
<p><i>the Sarge’s Vigor from d8 to d6. Note that </i></p>
<p><i>while  the  attack  caused  two  wounds,  the </i></p>
<p><i>Game Master only rolled once on the Injury </i></p>
<p><i>Table since it was one attack.</i></p>
<p>Heroes.Never.Die</p>
<p>Heroes in movies very rarely die. And when they </p>
<p>do, they go down fighting or perform one last, epic </p>
<p>act of heroism. With this rule in play, heroes who </p>
<p>would normally die are simply knocked out for a </p>
<p>dramatically appropriate time instead. Only if the </p>
<p>situation is particularly heroic, or if it serves as </p>
<p>a major story point can characters actually die.</p>
<p>An  adventurer  who  falls  into  a  volcano,  for </p>
<p>example, might land on a floating piece of rock and </p>
<p>leapfrog to safety. Conversely, if a hero confronts a </p>
<p>massive demon on a crumbling bridge and suffers </p>
<p>what would normally be a mortal wound, perhaps </p>
<p>he does perish but the Game Master decides with </p>
<p>his final blow he takes the demon into the boiling </p>
<p>lava with him, allowing his companions to escape.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Villains:</b> Of course the reverse of this rule is </p>
<p>also  true—villains  never  die  either! The  Game </p>
<p>Master  will  need  to  explain  the  spirit  of  these </p>
<p>rules to the group so their characters don’t take </p>
<p>advantage of it by doing something gruesome and </p>
<p>fatal to a fallen villain. In Heroes Never Die, the </p>
<p>party is assumed to capture their rivals and turn </p>
<p>them over to the authorities—where of course they </p>
<p>eventually escape to return again.</p>
<p>High.Adventure</p>
<p>  Characters  can  spend  a  Benny  to  gain </p>
<p>a  one-time  use  of  a  Combat  Edge.  Player </p>
<p>Characters have to meet the Rank and any Edge </p>
<p>requirements, but can ignore Trait requirements </p>
<p>for  this  one-time  use.  Multiple  bennies  can  be </p>
<p>spent in one round for multiple Edges, either for </p>
<p>different  effects  or  in  order  to  meet  a  needed </p>
<p>requirement to gain another Edge.</p>
<p>Joker’s.Wild</p>
<p>This is a great rule to add to your game if you’re </p>
<p>the kind of Game Master who’s a little stingy in </p>
<p>awarding Bennies. It slightly ups the heroics and </p>
<p>is a lot of fun for the group.</p>
<p>When a player character draws a Joker during </p>
<p>combat, he receives his normal +2 bonus to Trait </p>
<p>and damage rolls. In addition, all player characters </p>
<p>receive a Benny!</p>
<p>Multiple.Languages</p>
<p>Some settings feature characters and cultures </p>
<p>who typically speak many different languages. If </p>
<p>this Setting Rule is in play, your hero knows his </p>
<p>cultural or national language plus an additional </p>
<p>number  of  languages  equal  to  half  his  Smarts </p>
<p>die. An elf with a d8 Smarts in a swords &amp; sorcery </p>
<p>campaign,  for  example,  knows  Elvish  and  four </p>
<p>other languages—perhaps human, dwarven, and </p>
<p>two others of her choosing.</p>
<p>No.Power.Points</p>
<p>Instead of using Power Points, characters with </p>
<p>Arcane  Backgrounds  simply  choose  the  power </p>
<p>they want to activate and make an arcane skill roll. </p>
<p>The penalty to the roll is half the power’s usual </p>
<p>Power  Point  cost  (rounded  down).  Casting  the </p>
<p><i>armor</i> power, for example, which costs 2 Power </p>
<p>Points, is an arcane skill roll at –1.</p>
<p>Once cast, check the results below:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Success:</b> The power activates as usual.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Raise:</b> A raise on the roll grants any additional </p>
<p>bonuses to the power stated in its description. </p>
<p><i>Armor, </i> for  example,  grants  a  +4  bonus  to </p>
<p>Toughness with a raise.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Failure:</b> All currently maintained powers are </p>
<p>cancelled and the caster is Shaken.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Backlash:</b>  Channeling  magic,  spiritual,  or </p>
<p>psionic energy, tinkering with unstable technology, </p>
<p>and even using superpowers all come with certain </p>
<p>risks.</p>
<p>If a caster rolls a 1 on the arcane skill die when </p>
<p>using one of his powers it automatically fails and </p>
<p>he suffers 2d6 damage.</p>
<p>Replace each Arcane Background’s specialized </p>
<p>backlash rules with these:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Magic:</b> Use the above Backlash results.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Miracles:</b> As  above,  but  the  damage  is </p>
<p>reduced by half the priest’s Faith skill.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Psionics:</b> Use the above Backlash rules, but </p>
<p>the damage is applied to all sentient creatures </p>
<p>within a Large Burst Template centered on the </p>
<p>psionicist. </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--110</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Super  Powers:</b>  The  hero  does  not  suffer </p>
<p>damage, but cannot use the power for the rest </p>
<p>of this encounter or until he spends a Benny </p>
<p>to negate the Backlash.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Weird Science:</b> As above and the “device” is </p>
<p>destroyed and must be rebuilt. This typically </p>
<p>takes 1d3+1 hours, minus one hour for every </p>
<p>success and raise on the Repair roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Maintaining  Powers:</b>  Characters  can </p>
<p>maintain  powers  as  long  as  desired,  but  each </p>
<p>power maintained inflicts a –1 to cast any new </p>
<p>powers. Thus  an  invisible  mage  can  keep  the </p>
<p>power going indefinitely, but suffers a –1 penalty </p>
<p>if he then attempts to hurl a <i>bolt</i>.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Interrupting Powers:</b> If a character with an </p>
<p>activated  power  is  Shaken  or  suffers  a  wound </p>
<p>or  Fatigue  level,  he  must  make  a  Smarts  roll </p>
<p>to  maintain  all  his  powers.  If  the  roll  is  failed, </p>
<p>all powers are instantly dropped. A wizard with </p>
<p><i>armor</i> who suffers a Shaken result, for example, </p>
<p>must make a Smarts roll. If the wizard suffers two </p>
<p>wounds from an attack, he must make a Smarts </p>
<p>roll at –2.</p>
<p>Powers shut down automatically if the caster </p>
<p>sleeps or is rendered unconscious.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Power Preparation:</b> A caster may prepare a </p>
<p>spell by concentrating for a round (no movement </p>
<p>or  other  actions  and  avoid  interruption,  as </p>
<p>described  above).  If  successful,  he  ignores  2 </p>
<p>points  of  penalties  on  all  powers  cast  with  his </p>
<p>next action. If he does not enact any powers on </p>
<p>his next action, the preparation is lost.</p>
<p>Skill.Specialization</p>
<p><i>Savage Worlds</i> skills are intended to be broad, </p>
<p>allowing characters to focus primarily on Edges </p>
<p>for customization rather than multiple iterations </p>
<p>of  something  like  Fighting  for  edged  weapons, </p>
<p>Fighting for blunt weapons, etc.</p>
<p>If  it’s  important  to  your  setting  to  have  more </p>
<p>detail, a character uses his normal skill for one </p>
<p>device of choice. When using any other variation </p>
<p>he suffers a –2 penalty.</p>
<p>Gaining  an  additional  specialization  counts </p>
<p>as raising a skill below its linked Attribute. So a </p>
<p>character can gain two new specializations with </p>
<p>an Advance, or gain a specialization and increase </p>
<p>a skill below its linked Attribute.</p>
<p>Below are skills appropriate for this extra detail </p>
<p>and some example specializations:</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Boating:</b> Powered, Sailed, Steam</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Driving:</b> Hover, Tracked, Wheeled</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Fighting:</b> Axe,  Blunt  Weapon,  Exotic  (such </p>
<p>as nunchaku; each is separate), Long blade, </p>
<p>Pole arm, Short blade.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Piloting:</b> Fixed Wing, Rotary, Space</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Riding:</b> Camel, Horse, etc.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Shooting:</b>  Bow,  Crossbow,  Pistol,  Rifle, </p>
<p>Shotgun, etc.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Survival:</b> Arctic, Desert, Temperate</p>
<p>Social.Conflict</p>
<p>Not every conflict is won by the blade. Successful </p>
<p>oratory can topple nations. </p>
<p>The following system works well for protracted </p>
<p>and dramatic negotiations, such as convincing a </p>
<p>council to send troops to a noble’s lands, winning </p>
<p>a court case, or tricking a guard into opening the </p>
<p>gates to the space station.</p>
<p>The conflict is broken down into three rounds </p>
<p>of conversation, each focusing on one particular </p>
<p>point (or a few highly connected points). Further </p>
<p>rounds  represent  the  turn  of  conversation  to </p>
<p>additional points.</p>
<p>Each  round,  the  player  character  roleplays </p>
<p>his  argument  and  makes  a  Persuasion  roll  (or </p>
<p>an opposed Persuasion roll if a rival is arguing </p>
<p>against him).</p>
<p>A  speaker  accumulates  a  success  for  each </p>
<p>success and raise on the Persuasion roll (it’s best </p>
<p>to keep track of this with tokens).</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--111</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p>At the end of the third round of conflict, the side </p>
<p>with the most successes “wins” the argument. The </p>
<p>more successes, the more convinced the target to </p>
<p>be persuaded is, as shown on the Social Conflict </p>
<p>Table below.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Modifiers:</b> The  Game  Master  should  grant </p>
<p>a +2 bonus to a side that makes a particularly </p>
<p>brilliant  or  undeniable  point;  or  a  –2  penalty  if </p>
<p>the speaker commits a faux pas, such as making </p>
<p>a  provably  untrue  statement  or  insulting  the </p>
<p>audience he hopes to convince.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Knowledge Is Power:</b> If the characters are </p>
<p>arguing technical points, such as a legal battle </p>
<p>or  the  best  plan  for  a  kingdom’s  defense,  the </p>
<p>character must roll the lowest of his Persuasion </p>
<p>or appropriate Knowledge roll. So for example a </p>
<p>lawyer with a Persuasion of d10 and Knowledge </p>
<p>(Law) of d4, would roll d4. It always pays to know </p>
<p>what you’re talking about.</p>
<p>Social.Conflict.Results</p>
<p> <b>Margin of Victory </b></p>
<p><b>Result</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>Tie </p>
<p>The issue is unsettled and no action is </p>
<p>taken until new and more compelling </p>
<p>evidence can be presented to reopen </p>
<p>the  negotiation.  In  a  court  case,  the </p>
<p>defense  would  win  as  the  burden  of </p>
<p>proof is on the prosecution.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>1-2 </p>
<p>The  target  isn’t  truly  convinced  but </p>
<p>decides it’s better to be safe than sorry. </p>
<p>He  provides  the  minimum  amount  of </p>
<p>support possible. In a court case, the </p>
<p>jury  barely  finds  reasonable  doubt  if </p>
<p>the defense wins, or assigns minimal </p>
<p>sentencing if the prosecution wins.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>3-4 </p>
<p>The target is reasonably convinced. He </p>
<p>grants  the  help  requested,  more  or </p>
<p>less, but may have conditions or ask </p>
<p>favors  in  return.  In  a  court  case,  the </p>
<p>judge invokes severe sentencing if the </p>
<p>prosecution wins. If the defense wins </p>
<p>the defendant is cleared of all charges.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>5+ </p>
<p>The  target  is  sure  of  his  decision.  He </p>
<p>provides more support than requested. </p>
<p>If the prosecution wins in a court case, </p>
<p>the defendant receives the maximum </p>
<p>penalty.</p>
<p>.Example:.To.Arms!</p>
<p>Orcs have invaded multiple baronies across the </p>
<p>kingdom. The hero is a young noble attempting </p>
<p>to convince the king to send royal troops to his </p>
<p>aid. The king is disinclined to send his troops as </p>
<p>a rebellion is also near-at-hand.</p>
<p>The young noble begins by telling the king of his </p>
<p>own battles against the orcs and the depredations </p>
<p>they  have  committed  across  the  kingdom.  He </p>
<p>makes  his  Persuasion  roll  and  gets  a  7.  One </p>
<p>success.</p>
<p>The noble next presents his scouts who tell the </p>
<p>king which baronies the orcs have been spotted </p>
<p>in. The noble rolls again and gets a 9 for a total </p>
<p>of three successes.</p>
<p>Finally, the young warrior reminds the king that </p>
<p>it was his family that supported his coronation, </p>
<p>subtly  implying  that  a  debt  is  owed. This  time, </p>
<p>the noble fails.</p>
<p>With three total successes, the king agrees to </p>
<p>send a token force of infantry, but will not send his </p>
<p>more loyal mounted knights. One of the infantry </p>
<p>commanders, an old friend of noble’s family, is </p>
<p>likely to rebel in the coming revolt, so the king </p>
<p>not  only  sends  him  to  the  noble’s  aid  but  also </p>
<p>instructs an assassin to ensure he doesn’t return! </p>
<p>The Game Master now has a new subplot to work </p>
<p>into his adventure as the heroes try to prevent the </p>
<p>assassination of their friend—and the commander </p>
<p>of their allied troops.</p>
<p>Travel</p>
<p>Most  epic  tales  feature  trips  across  great </p>
<p>expanses—whether  it’s  the  Hobbits’  quest  to </p>
<p>Mordor in <i>The Lord of the Rings</i> books or warping </p>
<p>across space in a hard science-fiction tale. This </p>
<p>section  helps  you  figure  out  how  long  these </p>
<p>journeys  might  take  and  what  might  dramatic </p>
<p>events might happen along the way.</p>
<p>Time.and.Distance</p>
<p>Land.Travel.by.Foot.or.Mount</p>
<p>A creature or character’s Base Speed is half its </p>
<p>Pace in miles per hour, with a minimum of ½ mile </p>
<p>per hour. A human with a Pace of 6, for example, </p>
<p>walks about 3 miles per hour.</p>
<p>Groups  move  at  the  speed  of  their  slowest </p>
<p>member.  Fatigue  and  Encumbrance  penalties </p>
<p>don’t normally modify Pace, but the Game Master </p>
<p>may  choose  to  apply  them  when  calculating </p>
<p>overland  movement  rates  if  it’s  dramatically </p>
<p>appropriate.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Flying Speeds:</b> Travelers with the luxury of </p>
<p>flying mounts or some other means of leaving the </p>
<p>ground behind may ignore the modifiers below and </p>
<p>may be able to take shorter routes than the land-</p>
<p>bound. Game Masters may adjust airspeeds for </p>
<p>headwinds, thin air, smoke, or other aeronautical </p>
<p>phenomena.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--112</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Overland.Speed.Modifiers</p>
<p><b>Ground </b></p>
<p><b>Speed </b></p>
<p><b>Terrain Type</b></p>
<p>Easy </p>
<p>– 0 mph </p>
<p>Plains, road</p>
<p>Average </p>
<p>– 1 mph </p>
<p>Rocky desert, light </p>
<p>forest, low hills</p>
<p>Hard </p>
<p>– 2 mph </p>
<p>Steep hills, sand, </p>
<p>medium forest</p>
<p>Difficult </p>
<p>– 3 mph </p>
<p>Mountains, heavy </p>
<p>forest, marsh</p>
<p>Vehicular.Travel</p>
<p>For  reasonably  paced  travel  over  the  course </p>
<p>of one or more days, just use the vehicle’s Top </p>
<p>Speed as its average miles per hour.</p>
<p>Multiply Top  Speed  by  the  number  of  hours </p>
<p>traveled (eight per day being typical) to see how </p>
<p>far a vehicle can travel in one day. For example, </p>
<p>a vehicle with a Top Speed of 40 could travel 320 </p>
<p>miles in 8 hours. </p>
<p>Multiply  the  mileage  by  1.5  for  interstate  or </p>
<p>autobahn travel, or by .5 if traveling over difficult </p>
<p>terrain  such  as  clogged  highways  in  a  post-</p>
<p>apocalyptic aftermath, lack of roads, etc.</p>
<p>Sailing.Ships</p>
<p>Powered  vessels  use  the  same  system  as </p>
<p>Vehicles, above. Sailing ships require more work </p>
<p>on  the  part  of  the  crew.  Each  day,  the  ship’s </p>
<p>captain makes a Boating roll, plus or minus any </p>
<p>modifiers the Game Master feels are appropriate </p>
<p>for  strong  or  weak  winds,  currents,  traveling </p>
<p>upriver,  and  so  on. These  rates  are  based  on </p>
<p>eight-hour stretches, but sailing ships with enough </p>
<p>crew don’t usually stop, so multiply the results by </p>
<p>three if the vessel sails the entire 24-hour period.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Total  of  1  or  Less:</b> The  craft  is  becalmed, </p>
<p>goes in circles, or is effectively lost. It makes no </p>
<p>progress that day and must make a Knowledge </p>
<p>(Navigation) roll the following day or sail in a </p>
<p>direction other than intended.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Failure:</b> 3 x Top Speed in miles traveled</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Success:</b> 10 x Top Speed in miles traveled</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Raise:</b> 15 x Top Speed in miles traveled</p>
<p>Encounters</p>
<p>Unless  the  area  traveled  is  patrolled,  draw </p>
<p>a  card  from  the Action  Deck  once  per  day  (or </p>
<p>whatever  period  the  Game  Master  feels  is </p>
<p>dramatically appropriate for the current scene). A </p>
<p>face card or higher represents an encounter, and </p>
<p>the card suit can be used to determine the type.</p>
<p>Game  Masters  are  highly  encouraged  to </p>
<p>customize encounters based on their setting.</p>
<p>Encounter.Table</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Clubs—Obstacle:</b> The heroes encounter an </p>
<p>obstacle  of  some  kind  and  must  figure  out </p>
<p>how  to  circumvent  it.  Some  examples  are </p>
<p>a flooded river, minefields, a decaying rope </p>
<p>bridge, whirlpool, etc. The obstacle might also </p>
<p>be defended by creatures or enemies as well.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Hearts—NPCs:</b>  The  group  comes  upon </p>
<p>neutral or friendly nonplayer characters such </p>
<p>as merchants, lost travelers, a guide, or even </p>
<p>other adventurers.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Diamonds—Fortune:</b>  Somewhere  along </p>
<p>the  way  is  something  of  value—the  hulk  of </p>
<p>a spaceship full of supplies, a small treasure </p>
<p>cache, a vein of useful or valuable minerals, </p>
<p>or a minor magic item in the clutches of some </p>
<p>unfortunate corpse. </p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Spades—Enemies:</b>  Monsters,  enemies,  or </p>
<p>hostile beasts bar the way. Perhaps they lie </p>
<p>in ambush if it’s known the heroes are coming </p>
<p>this way.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--113</p>
<p>Vehicles</p>
<p>From armored combat in World War II to ship-</p>
<p>to-ship battles on the high seas, vehicles often </p>
<p>play a key role in the furious action of our Savage </p>
<p>Settings. The following section contains rules for </p>
<p>handling  vehicle  combat  on  the  table-top.  For </p>
<p>more abstract Chases that take place over larger </p>
<p>areas, see page 94.</p>
<p>Movement</p>
<p>A  vehicle’s Acceleration  value  is  how  many </p>
<p>inches per turn it can increase its current speed. </p>
<p>A pickup with an Acceleration of 5, for instance, </p>
<p>can  increase its movement by  5”  every action, </p>
<p>up to its Top Speed. A vehicle can decrease its </p>
<p>speed by twice its Acceleration (or more in a Hard </p>
<p>Brake—see Maneuvers).</p>
<p>The player controlling the vehicle decides his </p>
<p>speed at the beginning of his action, and moves </p>
<p>it that many inches. The vehicle remains at that </p>
<p>speed until the driver’s next action, so record the </p>
<p>current speed somewhere for the next round and </p>
<p>in case it hits something.</p>
<p>Vehicles have some restrictions on maneuvering, </p>
<p>particularly  in  how  they  turn.  Use  the Turning </p>
<p>Template for normal turns, or perform a maneuver </p>
<p>(see below) for tighter moves.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Action Cards:</b> Vehicles move on their driver’s </p>
<p>Action Card. Driving is a free action unless the </p>
<p>driver attempts a maneuver. Passengers act on </p>
<p>their own initiative as usual.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Speed Kills:</b> It’s much easier to maneuver a </p>
<p>vehicle slowly than it is at high speed. A vehicle </p>
<p>moving over 15” a round on the tabletop inflicts a </p>
<p>–2 handling penalty on its driver. A vehicle moving </p>
<p>over 30” inflicts a –4 penalty.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Difficult  Ground:</b>  Slippery  gravel,  uneven </p>
<p>ground, and mud are all difficult ground and work </p>
<p>just as for characters on foot. Count each inch </p>
<p>moved through such rough terrain as two, and </p>
<p>apply a –2 penalty to all Driving rolls made while </p>
<p>in  rough  terrain.  Driving  through  rough  terrain </p>
<p>at over half of Top Speed requires a Driving roll </p>
<p>every round.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Reverse: </b>A vehicle can move up to half its </p>
<p>Top Speed in reverse. Driving rolls made while in </p>
<p>reverse suffer a –2 modifier.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--114</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>1</p>
<p>Turning</p>
<p>To perform turns when using miniatures on the table-top, use the Turning Template found at the </p>
<p>back of this book. Simply line your vehicle up with the template and move it along the outside, as </p>
<p>shown in the Turning diagram. Vehicles can perform tighter turns as a Maneuver (see Maneuvers).</p>
<p>The car here is performing a turn to the left. This is the </p>
<p>most it can turn without performing a maneuver.</p>
<p>The car has </p>
<p>moved 5”. This is </p>
<p>its position at the </p>
<p>end of its move.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Aircraft: </b> Aircraft  ignore  terrain.  Unless  the </p>
<p>aircraft has the ability to hover, it must move at </p>
<p>least half its Top Speed each action. Climb isn’t </p>
<p>used in table-top battles—only in Chases.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Animals  and  Conveyances:</b> Animals  don’t </p>
<p>have to worry about Acceleration or Top Speed—</p>
<p>they simply move their Pace (plus any running </p>
<p>roll) each round. Animal-drawn transportation has </p>
<p>an Acceleration equal to half the animal’s basic </p>
<p>Pace. Carts and wagons don’t have a Top Speed </p>
<p>because  the  animal  simply  moves  its  Pace  as </p>
<p>usual (plus a running die if it runs).</p>
<p>Maneuvers</p>
<p>Below are some common maneuvers with any </p>
<p>penalty to the Driving roll listed in parentheses. </p>
<p>If the maneuver is failed, move the vehicle to the </p>
<p>point  of  the  maneuver,  then  roll  on  the  Out  of </p>
<p>Control Table to see where it actually ends up.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Bootlegger Reverse (–4):</b> The vehicle moves </p>
<p>forward at half its current speed and then turns </p>
<p>between 90 and 180 degrees (player’s choice). </p>
<p>The vehicle instantly decelerates to a complete </p>
<p>stop and cannot move further this round.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Hard Brake (0):</b> The driver decelerates up to </p>
<p>three times the vehicle’s Acceleration.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Jump  (0):</b>  With  a  ramp,  ground  and  water </p>
<p>vehicles can jump a distance equal to one-</p>
<p>quarter of their current speed, plus 1d10” with </p>
<p>a raise on the Driving roll. They descend one </p>
<p>inch for every two inches jumped forward, so </p>
<p>a vehicle jumping 20” falls 10” by the end of </p>
<p>its jump.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Maneuver (–1 to –4):</b> This one covers most </p>
<p>everything else a driver, rider, or pilot might try </p>
<p>to do, such as riding a horse down a steep or </p>
<p>slippery hill, avoiding a manhole cover or other </p>
<p>obstacle, or driving through an oil slick at high </p>
<p>speed. The GM sets the modifier. If the roll is </p>
<p>failed, the driver goes out of control as usual.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Obstacle  (–2  or  more):</b>  Driving  through  a </p>
<p>really  tight  obstacle  looks  easy  enough  on </p>
<p>the tabletop since a vehicle is simply moved </p>
<p>however the player wants. But in “reality,” the </p>
<p>car is shifting around and is much more difficult </p>
<p>to hold steady than the table-top shows. For </p>
<p>this reason, drivers trying to pass through tight </p>
<p>obstacles—narrow  alleys,  other  cars,  flying </p>
<p>under power lines—must make Driving rolls. </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--115</p>
<p>Situational.Rules</p>
<p>The standard difficulty is –2, but really tight </p>
<p>spots might call for a –4 or greater penalty. If </p>
<p>the roll is failed, the vehicle hits the obstacle </p>
<p>and suffers collision damage as usual.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Ram (Opposed):</b> Though we have to move </p>
<p>vehicles  in  turns  on  the  table-top,  they’re </p>
<p>actually  moving  simultaneously  in  the  “real </p>
<p>world.” For that reason, when one car rams </p>
<p>another,  we  give  the  defender  a  chance  to </p>
<p>get out of the way—even if it’s not his turn. </p>
<p>When  this  happens,  the  two  drivers  make </p>
<p>opposed  Driving  rolls.  If  the  attacker  wins, </p>
<p>he’s  managed  to  ram  his  foe  and  damage </p>
<p>is calculated normally. If the defender wins, </p>
<p>he must move his vehicle just out of the way, </p>
<p>whether  backwards,  forwards,  or  sideways. </p>
<p>Sometimes  cars  run  into  people  as  well.  In </p>
<p>this case, the driver makes an opposed roll </p>
<p>against the target’s Agility instead.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Tight Turn (0): </b>The vehicle can turn up to 90 </p>
<p>degrees.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Two  Wheels  (–4): </b> Sometimes  a  character </p>
<p>needs to put a wheeled vehicle on its side, </p>
<p>maybe to squeeze through a narrow alley or </p>
<p>avoid  running  over  some  deadly  obstacle. </p>
<p>Turning a vehicle up on two wheels requires </p>
<p>a ramp of some kind—even an embankment </p>
<p>can do the trick. The effect is to decrease the </p>
<p>width of the car—usually by about 25%. The </p>
<p>driver must make a Driving roll at the beginning </p>
<p>of each turn he wants to keep the car up on </p>
<p>two wheels.</p>
<p>Collisions</p>
<p>Collisions are very deadly in <i>Savage Worlds.</i> </p>
<p>The damage to the vehicle and its passengers </p>
<p>is  1d6  for  every  5”  of  its  current  speed  (round </p>
<p>normally). If a motorcycle moving at 16” per turn </p>
<p>hits a tree, for instance, it suffers 3d6 damage, as </p>
<p>does its rider and anything it rams into.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Relative  Speeds:</b>  Increase  the  damage  if </p>
<p>a  vehicle  hits  another  object  moving  toward  it. </p>
<p>This is called “relative speed.” If two tanks ram </p>
<p>into  each other  at  a  speed of 12,  for instance, </p>
<p>their relative speed is actually 24 and each tank </p>
<p>suffers 4d6 damage. Similarly, a vehicle moving </p>
<p>away from an attacker subtracts its speed from </p>
<p>the ramming vehicle’s.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Vehicular Armor: </b>Vehicles with Heavy Armor </p>
<p>add  their Armor  rating  as  an AP  value  to  their </p>
<p>damage.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Safety  Harnesses:</b> A  seat  belt  protects </p>
<p>anyone wearing it. Roll half the normal damage </p>
<p>dice (round down) for these characters. Do the </p>
<p>same for air bags, but subtract one additional die </p>
<p>of damage as well.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Soft Obstacles:</b> The collision rules assume </p>
<p>the vehicle has hit something hard, like another </p>
<p>vehicle, rock, wall, and so on. If the obstacle was </p>
<p>soft, like a person, the damage is halved for the </p>
<p>vehicle. The person takes damage normally, of </p>
<p>course.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Falling  or  Jumping  From  Vehicles:</b> </p>
<p>Characters  who  fall  from  a  moving  vehicle </p>
<p>suffer damage just as if they were in a collision. </p>
<p>Characters  who  jump  and  make  an Agility  roll </p>
<p>suffer half the usual damage dice (round down). </p>
<p>If a character jumps out of a boat, treat this as </p>
<p>nonlethal damage.</p>
<p>Losing.Control</p>
<p>Failing  a  Driving  roll  causes  a  vehicle  to  go </p>
<p>“out of control.” When this happens, roll 2d6 on </p>
<p>the Out of Control Table. Resolve any additional </p>
<p>movement  (such  as  slips  or  flips)  immediately. </p>
<p>Anything  in  the  way  gets  smashed—check  out </p>
<p>the collision rules above if that happens.</p>
<p>Vehicular.Attacks</p>
<p>Combat between vehicles and their crews works </p>
<p>just like regular combat but with two additional </p>
<p>modifiers.</p>
<p>Attack.Modifiers</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>Penalty</b> </p>
<p><b>Situation</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Unstable Platform</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–1 per 10” of Speed </p>
<p>Fast Targets</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Unstable  Platform: </b> Any  character  on  an </p>
<p>unstable  platform,  including  a  moving  vehicle, </p>
<p>suffers a –2 penalty to any “fine” physical skills </p>
<p>such as Fighting and Shooting. This applies to </p>
<p>characters who are firing vehicular weapons as </p>
<p>well. Note that the Steady Hands Edge eliminates </p>
<p>this penalty.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Fast  Target: </b> A  fast-moving  vehicle  is  more </p>
<p>difficult to hit, and subtracts 1 from any attacks </p>
<p>directed at it for every full 10” of its speed. This </p>
<p>is relative, so a vehicle heading directly toward a </p>
<p>character doesn’t get the modifier, nor does the </p>
<p>modifier apply if the hero is shooting at a vehicle </p>
<p>driving parallel to his own.</p>
<p>Damage</p>
<p>Damage that equals or exceeds the vehicle’s </p>
<p>Toughness forces  the  driver  to make  a  Driving </p>
<p>roll or go Out of Control (roll on the Out of Control </p>
<p>Table).</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--116</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Each  raise  on  the  damage  roll  also  inflicts  a </p>
<p>“wound.” Each wound caused to the vehicle inflicts </p>
<p>a –1 penalty to the driver’s Driving skill rolls until </p>
<p>someone repairs the damage. The attacker also </p>
<p>scores a critical hit for each wound inflicted, and </p>
<p>rolls on the Critical Hit Table to find out exactly </p>
<p>what happened.</p>
<p>When  a  vehicle  takes  its  fourth  wound,  it  is </p>
<p>automatically wrecked and the driver must make </p>
<p>a Driving roll or go out of control.</p>
<p>A wrecked vehicle won’t run anymore, though </p>
<p>its weapons may still be able to fire if they aren’t </p>
<p>powered by the vehicle’s propulsion system.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Wrecked Aircraft:</b> If an aircraft goes Out of </p>
<p>Control, don’t use the usual table. Instead, the </p>
<p>pilot makes a Piloting roll. If he fails, the aircraft </p>
<p>crashes and is destroyed. If the pilot can eject, he </p>
<p>gets one chance to do so by making an Agility roll.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Wrecked  Watercraft:</b>  Unless  the  vessel  is </p>
<p>cracked open and sinks instantly, a wrecked boat </p>
<p>sinks at the following rate:</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Small craft (rowboats, canoes) sink at the </p>
<p>end of the round in which they’re wrecked.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Medium craft, such as a fishing boat, sink in </p>
<p>1d6 minutes.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Large craft, such as a private yacht, sink in </p>
<p>4d6 minutes.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Ships, such as oil tankers, cruise ships, or </p>
<p>naval vessels, sink in 2d6 hours.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>A wrecked boat that hasn’t sunk drifts with </p>
<p>the current (if any). This is typically 2d6” per </p>
<p>round  for  fast-moving  rivers,  and  1d6”  for </p>
<p>most other rivers or in strong seas. In the </p>
<p>ocean, the GM can roll 1d12 to determine </p>
<p>which direction a vessel drifts.</p>
<p>Repairs</p>
<p>Characters may repair vehicles given sufficient </p>
<p>time and at least some basic tools. This requires </p>
<p>a Repair roll that takes two hours per wound the </p>
<p>vehicle has (regardless of how many are actually </p>
<p>repaired). Attempting  to  fix  up  a  tank  that  has </p>
<p>suffered  two  wounds,  for  example,  takes  four </p>
<p>hours regardless of the result of the Repair roll.</p>
<p>Field work requires at least a toolbox and basic </p>
<p>supplies,  and  subtracts  2  from  the  Repair  roll. </p>
<p>An average garage negates this penalty, and an </p>
<p>excellent or dedicated facility adds +2.</p>
<p>Each success and raise on the Repair roll fixes </p>
<p>one of the vehicle’s wounds. With an additional </p>
<p>raise above and beyond that required to fix all its </p>
<p>wounds, the repair time is halved.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Wrecks:</b>  Wrecked  vehicles  can  also  be </p>
<p>repaired, but it takes much more time and decent </p>
<p>spare parts. The time is equal to 1d6 x 10 hours </p>
<p>per wound level repaired.</p>
<p><i><b>Example: </b>Corporal Tunny has been tasked </i></p>
<p><i>with  patching  the  holes  in  a  B-17  bomber </i></p>
<p><i>with  two  wounds.  He’s  at  the  airbase  with </i></p>
<p><i>a dedicated garage and thus adds +2 to his </i></p>
<p><i>rolls.  Each  attempt  takes  four  hours,  and </i></p>
<p><i>each success and raise removes one wound.</i></p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--117</p>
<p>Out.of.Control</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>2d6 </b></p>
<p><b>Effect</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>2</b> </p>
<p><b>Roll Over:</b> The vehicle performs a Slip and rolls over 1d6 times in that direction. Roll </p>
<p>collision damage for the vehicle and everyone inside. Any exterior-mounted weapons </p>
<p>or accessories are ruined.</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>3–4</b> </p>
<p><b>Spin:</b> Move the vehicle 1d6” in the direction of the maneuver, or 1d6” away from a damaging </p>
<p>blow. Roll a d12, read it like a clock facing, and point the vehicle in that direction. </p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>5-9</b> </p>
<p><b>Skid:</b> Move the vehicle 1d4” left or right (in the direction of a failed maneuver, or away </p>
<p>from a damaging attack).</p>
<p> <b> 10–11</b> </p>
<p><b>Slip:</b> Move the vehicle 1d6” left or right (in the direction of a failed maneuver, or away from </p>
<p>a damaging attack).</p>
<p> </p>
<p><b>12</b> </p>
<p><b>Flip: </b>The vehicle flips end over end 1d4 times. Move it forward that many increments of </p>
<p>its own length. Roll collision damage for the vehicle, its passengers, and anything it hits. </p>
<p>Slow and heavy vehicles such as tanks (GM’s discretion) don’t flip but suffer a Slip or </p>
<p>Skid instead.</p>
<p>Critical.Hits</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>2d6 </b></p>
<p><b>Effect</b></p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>2</b> </p>
<p><b>Scratch and Dent:</b> The attack merely scratches the paint. There’s no permanent damage.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>3</b> </p>
<p><b>Engine:</b> The engine is hit. Oil leaks, pistons misfire, etc. Acceleration is halved (round </p>
<p>down). This does not affect deceleration, however.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>4</b> </p>
<p><b>Locomotion:</b> The wheels, tracks, or whatever have been hit. Halve the vehicle’s Top Speed </p>
<p>immediately. If the vehicle is pulled by animals, the shot hits one of them instead.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>5</b> </p>
<p><b>Controls:</b> The control system is hit. Until a Repair roll is made, the vehicle can only perform </p>
<p>turns to one side (1–3 left, 4–6 right). This may prohibit certain maneuvers as well.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>6–8</b> </p>
<p><b>Chassis:</b> The vehicle suffers a hit in the body with no special effects.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>9–10</b> </p>
<p><b>Crew:</b> A random crew member is hit. The damage from the attack is rerolled. If the character </p>
<p>is inside the vehicle, subtract the vehicle’s Armor from the damage. Damage caused by </p>
<p>an explosion affects all passengers in the vehicle.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>11</b> </p>
<p><b>Weapon:</b> A random weapon on the side of the vehicle that was hit is destroyed and may </p>
<p>no longer be used. If there is no weapon, this is a Chassis hit instead.</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>12</b> </p>
<p><b>Wrecked:</b> The vehicle is wrecked and automatically goes Out of Control.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--118</p>
<p>Most roleplaying games feature “magic” in one </p>
<p>form or another. Whether it’s hidden occult lore </p>
<p>practiced only by dark cultists, voodoo rituals, the </p>
<p>eldritch sorcery of powerful wizards, weird gadgets </p>
<p>created by mad scientists, superpowers, or the </p>
<p>psionic powers of the mind, these rules handle it </p>
<p>all in one simple system.</p>
<p>For  ease  of  use,  we  call  all  of  these  effects </p>
<p>“powers.”  Best  of  all,  powers  work  the  same </p>
<p>from  game  to  game,  but  the  particular  use </p>
<p>and Trappings  give  the  same  powers  endless </p>
<p>variations. That means you can create wizards, </p>
<p>mad scientists, superheroes, and even creatures </p>
<p>with one simple set of easy-to-remember rules.</p>
<p>Making.Arcane.Characters</p>
<p>Before  we go any  further,  you need to make </p>
<p>sure  your  GM  will  allow  arcane  characters  in </p>
<p>his game. You can’t make a wizard in a realistic </p>
<p>military campaign, and mad scientists might not </p>
<p>fit in every fantasy setting.</p>
<p>Now that that’s out of the way, you need to buy </p>
<p>the Arcane Background Edge and choose which </p>
<p>type of supernatural power your hero is blessed </p>
<p>with.</p>
<p>Five different types of powers are presented in </p>
<p>this rulebook: Magic, Miracles, Psionics, Super </p>
<p>Powers, and Weird Science. All types of powers </p>
<p>use  the  same  basic  mechanics  but  with  a  few </p>
<p>important differences in the details.</p>
<p>Let’s talk about what’s similar before we get into </p>
<p>what’s different.</p>
<p>Arcane.Skill</p>
<p>Each  type  of  power  has  a  particular  arcane </p>
<p>skill:  Faith  for  miracles,  Psionics  for  psionics, </p>
<p>Spellcasting  for  magic,  and  Weird  Science  for </p>
<p>weird  science.  Super  powers  use  skills  a  little </p>
<p>differently, as you’ll see on the following pages.</p>
<p>You  need  to  take  the  listed  skill  for  your </p>
<p>character’s  particular Arcane  Background  and </p>
<p>put points into it as usual. You’ll find the attribute </p>
<p>the  skill  is  linked  to  in  parentheses  beside  the </p>
<p>skill itself.</p>
<p>Power.Points</p>
<p>Arcane characters energize their powers with </p>
<p>“Power Points.” As soon as you buy an Arcane </p>
<p>Background  Edge,  your  hero  gets  the  listed </p>
<p>number of Power Points. </p>
<p>Using a power requires that you spend a number </p>
<p>of these points. Some powers allow you to pay </p>
<p>additional points for better effects, and some allow </p>
<p>you  to  maintain  the  effect  by  spending  Power </p>
<p>Points each round.</p>
<p>Heroes recover 1 Power Point per hour.</p>
<p>Starting.Powers</p>
<p>Arcane characters start with a number of powers </p>
<p>dictated by their particular Arcane Background. </p>
<p>See the Arcane Background list for specifics.</p>
<p>Learning.New.Powers</p>
<p>An arcane character can learn a new power by </p>
<p>selecting the New Power Edge. As soon as he </p>
<p>levels up and chooses this Edge, he can instantly </p>
<p>begin using whichever power he chooses.</p>
<p>Using.Powers</p>
<p>As  an  action,  a  character  may  use  a  single </p>
<p>power by declaring the power he’s using, spending </p>
<p>the desired number of Power Points, and making </p>
<p>an arcane skill roll.</p>
<p>If the roll is failed, there’s no effect but the Power </p>
<p>Points are lost. If successful, consult the particular </p>
<p>power to determine the results.</p>
<p>Some powers have variable effects depending </p>
<p>on how many Power Points are used to cast them. </p>
<p>The player must spend the desired Power Points </p>
<p><i>before</i> rolling his character’s arcane skill to see </p>
<p>if he’s successful.</p>
<p>Maintaining.Powers</p>
<p>Some powers may be maintained, as listed in </p>
<p>the power’s Duration description. This is a free </p>
<p>action. The number following the duration is the </p>
<p>cost in Power Points to keep the power going. </p>
<p>No new skill roll is needed to maintain a power.</p>
<p>For each power currently being maintained, the </p>
<p>Chapter.Five:.</p>
<p>Powers</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--119</p>
<p>caster suffers a –1 to future arcane skill rolls (but </p>
<p>not other Trait tests). A wizard maintaining <i>armor</i> </p>
<p>and <i> charm</i>,  for  example,  suffers  a  –2  penalty </p>
<p>to  his  Spellcasting  rolls  until  he  allows  those </p>
<p>powers to lapse. He does <i>not</i> suffer penalties to </p>
<p>his Fighting (or other) rolls while these spells are </p>
<p>being maintained.</p>
<p>Disruption</p>
<p>A  character  who  is  actively  maintaining  a </p>
<p>power may be disrupted if he suffers damage. To </p>
<p>maintain concentration for <i>all</i> of his powers, the </p>
<p>hero makes one opposed arcane skill roll versus </p>
<p>the damage he suffered. If his roll is higher, he </p>
<p>maintains all of his spells. If he fails, he instantly </p>
<p>drops all of his powers <i>after</i> the attack that caused </p>
<p>the disruption is resolved.</p>
<p>A character who is Shaken by non-damaging </p>
<p>means  (such  as  a Test  of  Wills)  must  make  a </p>
<p>simple Smarts roll to maintain his powers.</p>
<p>Arcane.Backgrounds</p>
<p>Below  are  the  five  different  types  of Arcane </p>
<p>Backgrounds.  Each  type  describes  the Arcane </p>
<p>Skill a character with that background uses, his </p>
<p>starting Power Points, the number of powers he </p>
<p>starts play with, and any potential drawbacks to </p>
<p>the power (such as Backlash or Malfunctions).</p>
<p>Arcane.Background.(Magic)</p>
<p><b>Arcane Skill:</b> Spellcasting (Smarts)</p>
<p><b>Starting Power Points: </b>10</p>
<p><b>Starting Powers:</b> 3</p>
<p>Magicians range from powerful wizards to vile </p>
<p>cultists. They draw on raw supernatural energy to </p>
<p>fuel their eldritch fires. This energy often infuses </p>
<p>the worlds in which they live, and is drawn forth </p>
<p>with elaborate rituals, words of power, runes, or </p>
<p>perhaps even dark sacrifices.</p>
<p>Wizards  are  often  quite  weak  early  in  their </p>
<p>careers, but are forces to be reckoned with as </p>
<p>they become powerful sorcerers.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Backlash:</b>  When  a  wizard  rolls  a  1  on  his </p>
<p>Spellcasting die (regardless of his Wild Die), he </p>
<p>is automatically Shaken. This can cause a wound.</p>
<p>Arcane.Background.(Miracles)</p>
<p><b>Arcane Skill:</b> Faith (Spirit)</p>
<p><b>Starting Power Points:</b> 10</p>
<p><b>Starting Powers:</b> 2</p>
<p>Those who draw on miracles are priestly types </p>
<p>or  holy  champions. Their  power  comes  from  a </p>
<p>divine  presence  of  some  sort,  including  gods, </p>
<p>nature, or spirits. Their powers are usually invoked </p>
<p>with  a  few  words  of  prayer  or  by  performing </p>
<p>established rituals.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Protector:</b>  Those  who  cast  miracles  are </p>
<p>champions  of  their  particular  religions.  Good </p>
<p>priests vow to protect the innocent, fight evil, and </p>
<p>obey all other tenets of their particular religion. Evil </p>
<p>priests typically vow to defeat those who oppose </p>
<p>their religion, or simply to cause as much misery </p>
<p>and  strife  as  possible. The  player  and  Game </p>
<p>Master should come up with a simple list of what </p>
<p>is important to the character’s religion and use </p>
<p>this as a guide.</p>
<p>Champions  who  violate  their  beliefs  are </p>
<p>temporarily  or  permanently  forsaken  by  their </p>
<p>chosen deity. Minor sins give the character a –2 to </p>
<p>his Faith rolls for one week. Major sins rob him of </p>
<p>all arcane powers for one week. Mortal sins cause </p>
<p>the character to be forsaken until the penitent hero </p>
<p>completes some great quest or task of atonement </p>
<p>to regain his lost powers.</p>
<p>Arcane.Background.(Psionics)</p>
<p><b>Arcane Skill:</b> Psionics (Smarts)</p>
<p><b>Starting Power Points:</b> 10</p>
<p><b>Starting Powers:</b> 3</p>
<p>Psionicists  have  discovered  how  to  tap  into </p>
<p>their own psychic powers. They can manipulate </p>
<p>matter, create fire, or control their own bodies with </p>
<p>but a thought.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Brainburn:</b> When a psionic character rolls a </p>
<p>1 on his Psionics die (regardless of his Wild Die), </p>
<p>he is automatically Shaken. On a critical failure, </p>
<p>the psi lets out a psychic scream that causes him </p>
<p>to be Shaken along with all allies in a Large Burst </p>
<p>Template who fail a Spirit roll. This can cause a </p>
<p>wound.</p>
<p>Arcane.Background.(Super.Powers)</p>
<p><b>Arcane Skill: </b>Special (None)</p>
<p><b>Starting Power Points:</b> 20</p>
<p><b>Starting Powers:</b> 1</p>
<p>Characters with super powers gain their abilities </p>
<p>through  strange  circumstances,  such  as  being </p>
<p>bitten by irradiated creatures, exposure to strange </p>
<p>chemicals, or perhaps by finding alien artifacts. </p>
<p>This  particular  level  of  power  is  intended  for </p>
<p>relatively low-level “pulp” heroes. More powerful </p>
<p>super  types  are  dealt  with  in  specific  Savage </p>
<p>Settings, and you’ll find an alternate and far more </p>
<p>detailed system in our <i>Super Powers Companion.</i></p>
<p>Super powers work a little differently from most </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--120</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>other Arcane Backgrounds—each power is its own </p>
<p>skill and has no linked attribute (and thus counts </p>
<p>as “lower” than its linked Attribute for purposes </p>
<p>of Advancement). A hero with the <i>armor</i> and <i>bolt </i></p>
<p>powers, for example, also has an Armor and a Bolt </p>
<p>skill he uses to enable it. It’s more expensive for </p>
<p>a character to improve his powers, but he starts </p>
<p>with more Power Points than other arcane types </p>
<p>so he can use his abilities more often.</p>
<p>Best of all, there are no drawbacks for super </p>
<p>powers as there are with other types of arcane </p>
<p>powers—the power either works or it doesn’t.</p>
<p>Arcane.Background.(Weird.Science)</p>
<p><b>Arcane  Skill: </b> Weird  Science  (Smarts—</p>
<p>see below)</p>
<p><b>Starting  Power  Points: </b> 10  (but  see </p>
<p>below)</p>
<p><b>Starting Powers:</b> 1</p>
<p>Weird Science is the creation of strange and </p>
<p>powerful devices. It differs from regular science </p>
<p>in that some element of the arcane is involved. </p>
<p>Maybe it’s just generic “super-science,” or perhaps </p>
<p>it’s divinely (or demonically) inspired. Maybe the </p>
<p>science  itself  is  relatively  sound,  but  it  derives </p>
<p>power from an arcane source, such as ghost rock </p>
<p>in <i>Deadlands</i>, or some other magical mineral or </p>
<p>essence in a steampunk fantasy game.</p>
<p>Weird  Science  is  different  in  that  each  new </p>
<p>power is actually a new “gizmo.” The player must </p>
<p>write down exactly what the device is when he </p>
<p>gains the power. An inventor with the <i>invisibility</i> </p>
<p>power,  for  instance,  actually  has  an <i> invisibility </i></p>
<p>belt, cloak, etc. Players are encouraged to give </p>
<p>their devices pseudo-scientific names as well (“Dr. </p>
<p>Zee’s Chromatic Disfibulating Device!”).</p>
<p>Weird Science is the skill an inventor uses when </p>
<p>activating most devices, and raises increase the </p>
<p>effects of the power as usual. If a device uses </p>
<p>a  different  skill,  such  as  Driving,  Fighting,  or </p>
<p>Shooting, the inventor uses  that  instead. A ray </p>
<p>gun, for example, uses the Shooting skill, while </p>
<p>a “vibroknife” (a knife with the <i>smite</i> power) uses </p>
<p>Fighting. </p>
<p>Powers that require an opposed roll, such as </p>
<p><i>puppet,</i>  also  use  the  scientist’s  Weird  Science </p>
<p>skill.</p>
<p>Each device comes with its own Power Points </p>
<p>equal to the inventor’s Power Points. An inventor </p>
