<p>everyone adjacent to them as a single action.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Size +3:</b> Sentinels are 8–9 feet tall and very </p>
<p>dense.</p>
<p>Mirror.Self</p>
<p>This is an ally that appears identical to the caster </p>
<p>but with the following differences. The ally is an </p>
<p>Extra as normal for the power. The duplicate has </p>
<p>half the total Power Points of the caster, and all of </p>
<p>the duplicate’s Traits are one die type less than the </p>
<p>caster’s Traits (to a minimum of d4). The duplicate </p>
<p>has identical mundane equipment, but none of it </p>
<p>has any magical qualities.</p>
<p>Telekinesis</p>
<p><b>Rank:</b> Seasoned</p>
<p><b>Power Points:</b> 5</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--138</p>
<p><b>Range:</b> Smarts</p>
<p><b>Duration:</b> 3 (1/round)</p>
<p><b>Trappings:</b> A  wave  of  the  hand,  magic  wand, </p>
<p>steely gaze.</p>
<p><i>Telekinesis</i> is the ability to move a single object </p>
<p>or creature (including one’s self) with arcane will. </p>
<p>The weight a caster can lift is equal to 10 pounds </p>
<p>times his Spirit die type, 50 pounds times his Spirit </p>
<p>with a raise.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Lifting Creatures:</b> Living targets may resist </p>
<p>with an opposed Spirit roll. If the roll is greater than </p>
<p>the caster’s skill total, the victim is unaffected. If the </p>
<p>creature loses, however, it is lifted as usual and </p>
<p>does not get another attempt to break free.</p>
<p>Occasionally  a  victim  might  manage  to  grab </p>
<p>onto something solid to prevent itself from being </p>
<p>lifted. When this happens, the victim may make an </p>
<p>opposed Strength roll versus the caster’s arcane </p>
<p>skill. If the victim is successful, he manages to </p>
<p>grab  onto  whatever  was  available  and  is  not </p>
<p>moved, bashed, or otherwise affected that round.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Telekinetic  Weapons:</b> A  caster  can  use </p>
<p><i>telekinesis</i> to wield a weapon. When this occurs, </p>
<p>the  weapon’s  Fighting  is  equal  to  his  arcane </p>
<p>skill,  and  its  damage  is  based  on  the  caster’s </p>
<p>Spirit instead of his Strength. A sword that does </p>
<p>Strength+d6 damage, for example, does Spirit+d6 </p>
<p>when  wielded  by <i> telekinesis</i>.  The  weapon </p>
<p>otherwise functions normally, including granting </p>
<p>bonus damage when it strikes with a raise.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Dropping  Things:</b>  Particularly  ruthless </p>
<p>characters often use <i>telekinesis</i> to drop their foes </p>
<p>or bash them into walls and the like. A creature </p>
<p>affected by this power can be moved up to the </p>
<p>caster’s Smarts in inches per turn in any direction. </p>
<p>Dropped creatures suffer falling damage as usual.</p>
<p>Victims who are bashed into walls or other solid </p>
<p>objects suffer the caster’s Spirit+d6 as damage. If </p>
<p>a caster with a d12 Spirit smashes an orc into a </p>
<p>wall, for example, the orc suffers d12+d6 damage.</p>
<p>Teleport</p>
<p><b>Rank:</b> Seasoned</p>
<p><b>Power Points:</b> 3+</p>
<p><b>Range:</b> Special</p>
<p><b>Duration:</b> Instant</p>
<p><b>Trappings:</b> A  cloud  of  smoke,  “phasing”  out, </p>
<p>change into a bolt of lightning.</p>
<p><i>Teleport</i>  allows  a  character  to  disappear  and </p>
<p>instantly  reappear  up  to  10”  distant  for  each  3 </p>
<p>Power  Points  spent,  or  15”  with  a  raise. This </p>
<p>counts as his movement for the round. Adjacent </p>
<p>opponents do not get a free attack against the </p>
<p>teleporting character. If the hero wishes to <i>teleport</i> </p>
<p>somewhere he can’t see, he must make a Smarts </p>
<p>roll at –2. If it is an unknown area he has never </p>
<p>seen, the roll is at a –4 penalty.</p>
<p>Failure of either roll means the teleporter hit an </p>
<p>object of some sort. He returns where he came </p>
<p>from and is Shaken. A roll of 1 on the casting die </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--139</p>
<p>Powers</p>
<p>(regardless  of  the  Wild  Die)  indicates  a  more </p>
<p>serious disaster — in addition to being Shaken he </p>
<p>also suffers 2d6 damage.</p>
<p> The teleporter can never enter a solid space </p>
<p>even if he tries. The power instantly returns him </p>
<p>to his starting location as above.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Carrying Others:</b> The hero can carry other </p>
<p>beings with him at the cost of a level of Fatigue per </p>
<p>additional “rider.” More than two may be carried </p>
<p>at once, but causes instant Incapacitation. One </p>
<p>Fatigue level is regained for each full hour of rest.</p>
<p>Wall.Walker</p>
<p><b>Rank:</b> Novice</p>
<p><b>Power Points:</b> 2</p>
<p><b>Range:</b> Touch</p>
<p><b>Duration:</b> 3 (1/round)</p>
<p><b>Trappings:</b> A crushed spider, bit of web, piece </p>
<p>of tentacle.</p>
<p>Spellcasters are frequently targeted in combat </p>
<p>because of their arcane prowess and high-utility </p>
<p>spells  such  as  this  are  great  for  getting  the </p>
<p>caster safely out of harm’s way. Of course, it has </p>
<p>countless other uses too.</p>
<p><i>Wall walker</i> allows the recipient to function much </p>
<p>like a human spider. He can stick to any surface, </p>
<p>allowing him to climb walls and even hang from the </p>
<p>ceiling. With a success, the character can move </p>
<p>along such surfaces at half his normal Pace. With </p>
<p>a raise, he may move at full Pace and even run.</p>
<p> </p>
<p>►</p>
<p><b>Additional Targets:</b> The character may affect </p>
<p>up to five targets by spending a like amount of </p>
<p>additional Power Points. </p>
<p>Warrior’s.Gift</p>
<p><b>Rank:</b> Seasoned</p>
<p><b>Power Points:</b> 4</p>
<p><b>Range:</b> Touch</p>
<p><b>Duration:</b> 3 (1/round)</p>
<p><b>Trappings:</b> Gestures, prayer, whispered words, </p>
<p>concentration.</p>
<p>Even  combat  mages  cannot  afford  to  spend </p>
<p>all  their  time  learning  new  combat  maneuvers </p>
<p>and martial skills. For those who enjoy the thrill </p>
<p>of melee or want to improve their companions’ </p>
<p>skills,  this  spell  provides  a  quick  solution  to  a </p>
<p>lack of training.</p>
<p>With a successful arcane skill roll, the recipient </p>
<p>gains the benefits of a single Combat Edge chosen </p>
<p>by the caster. The caster (not the recipient) must </p>
<p>be one Rank higher than the Rank requirement </p>
<p>of the Edge but ignores other requirements, even </p>
<p>those requiring other Edges. For the duration of </p>
<p>the spell, the recipient gains all the benefits of </p>
<p>the Edge.</p>
<p>Edges  gained  through  this  power  provide  no </p>
<p>additional  benefit  if  the  character  already  has </p>
<p>the Edge.</p>
<p>Zombie</p>
<p><b>Rank:</b> Veteran</p>
<p><b>Power Points:</b> 3/corpse</p>
<p><b>Range:</b> Smarts</p>
<p><b>Duration:</b> Special</p>
<p><b>Trappings:</b> Carving symbols on corpses, throwing </p>
<p>bones, graveyards, “leather” books.</p>
<p>This power is considered evil in most settings, </p>
<p>and  so  is  typically  used  only  by  villainous </p>
<p>nonplayer characters such as necromancers, evil </p>
<p>scientists, dark cultists, and the like.</p>
<p>When cast, <i>zombie</i> raises a number of dead </p>
<p>specified  by  the  character  when  he  spent  his </p>
<p>Power  Points.  The  undead  are  immediately </p>
<p>obedient, though perhaps a bit mischievous and </p>
<p>literal-minded in their duties.</p>
<p>Corpses  aren’t  summoned  by  this  ability,  so </p>
<p>there must actually be a supply of bodies available </p>
<p>for the power to have any effect. The bodies don’t </p>
<p>have  to  be  fresh — <i>zombie</i>  can  raise  servants </p>
<p>that  have  been  waiting  patiently  for  centuries. </p>
<p>Graveyards,  morgues,  and  battlefields  can  all </p>
<p>serve this purpose.</p>
<p>With a success, the dead remain animated for 1 </p>
<p>hour. With a raise, they remain animated for 1d6 </p>
<p>hours. With two raises, they remain animated for </p>
<p>an entire day.</p>
<p>Certain  powerful  necromancers  may  have </p>
<p>improved versions of this power that are cheaper </p>
<p>to cast and create permanent undead.</p>
<p>Zombie</p>
<p>These walking dead are typical groaning fiends </p>
<p>looking for fresh meat.</p>
<p><b>Attributes:</b> Agility  d6,  Smarts  d4,  Spirit  d4, </p>
<p>Strength d6, Vigor d6</p>
<p><b>Skills:</b>  Fighting  d6,  Intimidation  d6,  Notice  d4, </p>
<p>Shooting d6</p>
<p><b>Pace: </b>4; <b>Parry:</b> 5;<b> Toughness:</b> 7</p>
<p><b>Special Abilities</b></p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Claws:</b> Str.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Fearless:</b> Zombies are immune to Fear and </p>
<p>Intimidation.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Undead: </b>+2 Toughness; +2 to recover from </p>
<p>being  Shaken;  called  shots  do  no  extra </p>
<p>damage (except to the head).</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Weakness (Head):</b> Shots to a zombie’s head </p>
<p>are +2 damage.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--140</p>
<p>A group of heroes emarks upon an epic quest. </p>
<p>Terrible monsters and bitter rivals oppose them. </p>
<p>The elements are against them. Mysteries must </p>
<p>be solved, artifacts found, innocents saved.</p>
<p>It’s your job as Game Master to bring all these </p>
<p>fantastic elements to life, to challenge your players </p>
<p>with adventure beyond their wildest imaginations, </p>
<p>and provide a framework to find out if they succeed </p>
<p>in their ultimate goal—or fail.</p>
<p>This is the fun and excitement of being a Game </p>
<p>Master—of creating, managing, and presenting </p>
<p>an entire world of excitement and adventure to </p>
<p>your closest friends. It can be one of the most </p>
<p>satisfying  entertainment  experiences  there  is, </p>
<p>and <i>Savage Worlds</i> is designed to help you wring </p>
<p>every tension-filled moment and gut-busting laugh </p>
<p>from it.</p>
<p>You’ve read the rules and probably have more </p>
<p>ideas for a new campaign than you know what </p>
<p>to do with. Before you do, let’s take a moment to </p>
<p>talk about the art of being a good Game Master, </p>
<p>creating worlds, and putting together memorable </p>
<p>and exciting adventures.</p>
<p>Learning.the.Rules</p>
<p>You need to know how to make Trait rolls, use </p>
<p>Wild Dice, resolve attacks, and handle wounds. </p>
<p>Everything  else  in  this  book,  from  combat </p>
<p>maneuvers  to  Interludes,  can  be  ignored  until </p>
<p>you need it.</p>
<p>A great way to do this is to run a very simple </p>
<p>fight on your own first. Put a Wild Card fighter and </p>
<p>three orcs on the table in front of you and have at </p>
<p>it. Your only goal is to get a feel for rolling the dice, </p>
<p>figuring totals from Aces and modifiers, rolling and </p>
<p>applying  damage,  Soaking  wounds.  If  you  can </p>
<p>handle that, you can run a game.</p>
<p>Your.Game</p>
<p>You might think that the first step in starting a </p>
<p>new game is finding a group of people to play </p>
<p>with. That is important, but that’s your next step. </p>
<p>Your first step is to get <i>yourself</i> excited. Do that </p>
<p>and you’ll likely get all your friends excited as well.</p>
<p>Start  by figuring out  what  kind  of setting  you </p>
<p>want to run. What kind of characters might people </p>
<p>play? What might a typical adventure be like? Jot </p>
<p>down a few notes about what makes your game </p>
<p>cool, who the bad guys are, what kinds of magic </p>
<p>or other supernatural aspects are present, and </p>
<p>what  your  basic  storyline  is.  If  you  have  these </p>
<p>elements, you’ve got enough information to “sell” </p>
<p>your game to your friends and get them to play.</p>
<p>Game.Night</p>
<p>Now  it’s  time  to  recruit.  You’ve  got  a  great </p>
<p>campaign idea and enough information about it to </p>
<p>explain it to your friends. The next step is to find </p>
<p>out who wants to play, and when they can do it.</p>
<p>When  you’re  finding  out  who  wants  to  play, </p>
<p>first ask <i>when</i> each person can make it. It’s very </p>
<p>important that you set both a time and a regular </p>
<p>day to play. If you rely on a fluctuating week-to-</p>
<p>week schedule, you’re almost certainly doomed </p>
<p>to failure. People have busy lives, and as much </p>
<p>as everyone involved might love to play, they’ve </p>
<p>still  got  to  study  for  classes,  take  care  of  their </p>
<p>children, and otherwise live their lives. If you have </p>
<p>a set night every week, it’s much easier for your </p>
<p>friends to schedule most of their activities around </p>
<p>game night. It also helps you know when you’ve </p>
<p>got to be ready to run.</p>
<p>Don’t rule out playing on weeknights. Friday and </p>
<p>Saturday are great if you’re single or in college, </p>
<p>but difficult for players who need weekend nights </p>
<p>to be with their families. If you start at 6 p.m., your </p>
<p>players have time to get off work, grab some food </p>
<p>(or share pizza with the group), and get deep into </p>
<p>the game by 7 or so.</p>
<p>For most groups, you should wrap things up by </p>
<p>11 p.m. or so. Most people tend to get a little tired </p>
<p>by then and you don’t want game night to be a </p>
<p>stressful experience. Talk it over with the group </p>
<p>and see what works for them. Setting some basic </p>
<p>rules will help everyone schedule and makes it </p>
<p>far more likely everyone will show up week after </p>
<p>week.</p>
<p>Chapter.Six:.</p>
<p>Game.Mastering</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--141</p>
<p>A  wise  Game  Master  tries  to  end  each  night </p>
<p>with a bit of a cliffhanger as well. If your players </p>
<p>are talking during the week about what’s going to </p>
<p>happen next, you’ve done your job well.</p>
<p>Character.Types</p>
<p>Once  you’ve  got  a  few  friends  interested, </p>
<p>it’s  great  if  you  can  give  them  a  little  primer </p>
<p>information and find out what kind of characters </p>
<p>they  want  to  play. You  don’t  need  everyone  to </p>
<p>make characters at this point—character creation </p>
<p>is  fast  enough  that  they  can  do  it  at  your  first </p>
<p>session if you want. But if some of your friends </p>
<p>have neat ideas as to their background or basic </p>
<p>type (fighter, investigator, etc.), you can start doing </p>
<p>more detail work on your story. If you’re going to </p>
<p>run <i>Evernight</i>, for example, and all your friends </p>
<p>make  combat  types,  you  know  they’re  looking </p>
<p>for  a  high-action,  “hack  and  slash”  type  game. </p>
<p>That doesn’t mean you can’t mix in lots of other </p>
<p>elements—exploration, horror, deep roleplaying, </p>
<p>and so on—but every now and then you’ll want </p>
<p>to have a big nasty combat as well. </p>
<p>Getting.the.Party.Together</p>
<p>The  first  thing  you  need  to  do  in  any  new </p>
<p>adventure  or  campaign  is  figure  out  why  the </p>
<p>characters are together. There are two common </p>
<p>ways to handle this.</p>
<p>The.Mission</p>
<p>The most common way of building a party is for </p>
<p>an employer to offer a reward for adventurers to </p>
<p>complete some task. Perhaps they answer a want </p>
<p>ad, are hired in a smoky tavern, or are called on </p>
<p>by connections or friends. Either way, the heroes </p>
<p>are then thrown together by fate and must learn </p>
<p>to work as a team.</p>
<p>There’s a problem that sometimes arises from </p>
<p>this  approach.  Say  you’re  running  a  fantasy </p>
<p>campaign and the mission is to deliver a message </p>
<p>to a distant city. But one of the characters in your </p>
<p>group is a witch hunter. His player is all excited </p>
<p>about making such a unique character, but you </p>
<p>need  him  (and  the  others)  to  be  a  courier  for </p>
<p>now and accept the assignment to kick off your </p>
<p>campaign.</p>
<p>Fortunately,  there’s  an  easy  way  to  fix  this. </p>
<p>Instead  of  arguing  with  the  player  about  his </p>
<p>character’s  motivations,  let  the <i> player</i>  figure  it </p>
<p>out. Be honest and tell him that this is what you </p>
<p>need to get things started. Maybe the witch hunter </p>
<p>is simply between jobs. Or maybe he’s working </p>
<p>under cover to root out some evil sorceress, or </p>
<p>needs to earn some gold to buy better arms and </p>
<p>equipment for his task. Or maybe the employer or </p>
<p>the recipient is an old friend (or enemy).</p>
<p>This approach not only helps get things moving, </p>
<p>but may establish an entirely new and interesting </p>
<p>subplot for your campaign!</p>
<p>Former.Acquaintances</p>
<p>You  can  also  start  a  campaign  with  all  the </p>
<p>characters  already  knowing  each  other.  This </p>
<p>works great for getting the game moving, and is </p>
<p>very appropriate for certain campaign types. <i>Weird </i></p>
<p><i>Wars</i> games in particular, for example, are often </p>
<p>best run where the soldiers have served together </p>
<p>for a bit and know at least the basic faults and </p>
<p>vices of their companions. This is also a great way </p>
<p>to kick off a convention game where your group </p>
<p>only has a few hours to play.</p>
<p>The downside of starting a campaign like this </p>
<p>is that some players may feel cheated if they put </p>
<p>a lot of work into their characters’ backgrounds. </p>
<p>Players  often  come  up  with  deep  backstories </p>
<p>for  their  heroes. This  shows  great  imagination </p>
<p>and  enthusiasm  for  your  game  and  should  be </p>
<p>encouraged.</p>
<p>Unfortunately, unless you’ve set the character </p>
<p>up as an important person in your setting and his </p>
<p>background figures prominently in the events that </p>
<p>are to come, it’s very likely all that work will never </p>
<p>come to light. The reason why is that individual </p>
<p>backgrounds likely aren’t part of your overall plot, </p>
<p>and characters don’t engage in revealing small </p>
<p>talk like real people do. (But see Interludes on </p>
<p>page 105!)</p>
<p>Staying.Together</p>
<p>After the first adventure, the players may wonder </p>
<p>exactly why their characters might stay together. </p>
<p>This is easy if they’re employed  by a common </p>
<p>benefactor, or if similar  groups are  common  to </p>
<p>the  setting  (adventuring  crews,  military  teams, </p>
<p>and so on).</p>
<p>Staying together might be more difficult if the </p>
<p>campaign goal isn’t very clear, or if some of the </p>
<p>characters don’t get along that well. How to fix this </p>
<p>depends a lot on the type of game you’re running. </p>
<p>If there’s a clear, overall objective or over-arching </p>
<p>storyline, the party might stay together to defeat </p>
<p>the  greater  evil,  even  if  they  aren’t  the  best  of </p>
<p>friends.</p>
<p>If the campaign goal is more ambiguous, the </p>
<p>best answer may be to <i>not</i> force the heroes to </p>
<p>stay together. Consider a modern horror game. </p>
<p>The  players  are  thrust  together  in  a  haunted </p>
<p>mansion for a weekend. Terrible things occur, but </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--142</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>eventually at least some of the player characters </p>
<p>survive and stagger back into daylight. Why would </p>
<p>they then go out hunting vampires or searching </p>
<p>for zombies the following week just because they </p>
<p>had one incredible encounter?</p>
<p>Maybe  they  don’t.  Maybe  they  all  go  home </p>
<p>and try to forget what happened just like normal </p>
<p>folks. Let a few days or weeks of nothing happen </p>
<p>(narratively, of course). Later on though, one of </p>
<p>the  characters  gets  involved  in  another  creepy </p>
<p>encounter. This time out, however, he knows at </p>
<p>least a few other people who won’t laugh at him </p>
<p>or throw him in the loony bin, so <i>he</i> calls and asks </p>
<p>the other player characters for help.</p>
<p>Friction</p>
<p>So  what  happens  if  your  group  doesn’t  get </p>
<p>along? That depends on whether it’s the players </p>
<p>or the characters who are arguing.</p>
<p>We  won’t  give  you  advice  on  how  to  handle </p>
<p>conflicts with your friends. You know them best, </p>
<p>and  will  have  to  figure  out  if  there  are  certain </p>
<p>friends  who  just  don’t  fit  in  your  campaign. </p>
<p>Remember though that friends are more important </p>
<p>than games—even ours. If you and the rest of </p>
<p>the  group  can’t  talk  out  your  differences,  find </p>
<p>something else to do for a while that doesn’t cause </p>
<p>such friction. Maybe you can come back to the </p>
<p>game once you’ve worked things out.</p>
<p>Friction between characters, on the other hand, </p>
<p>is not only fine, it’s actually encouraged to some </p>
<p>degree. Any  interesting  group  of  personalities </p>
<p>squabble and argue on occasion. As long as this </p>
<p>is  done  in  character,  it  adds  to  the  roleplaying </p>
<p>experience and the depth of your campaign. Quiet </p>
<p>fights between the heroes can often take on a life </p>
<p>of their own, encouraging players to take you, the </p>
<p>Game Master, aside or write you private notes </p>
<p>about actions their heroes perform away from the </p>
<p>prying eyes of others.</p>
<p>Campaign.Types</p>
<p>Different groups like different types of games. </p>
<p>Some like lots of combat, others prefer to run fast </p>
<p>and loose with more roleplaying and less “hack </p>
<p>and slash.” Most people mix all these elements </p>
<p>together. When you cut to the chase, there are </p>
<p>basically three campaign types: Hack and Slash, </p>
<p>Roleplaying, and Exploration.</p>
<p>Hack.and.Slash</p>
<p>A Hack and Slash game features lots of combat. </p>
<p>With <i>Savage Worlds,</i> you can do more than pit </p>
<p>your five player characters against a couple of </p>
<p>orcs and an ogre. You can throw a whole horde at </p>
<p>your heroes, and give them a few staunch allies </p>
<p>or henchmen to help as well!</p>
<p>The best part is that players who just want to </p>
<p>wade into the forces of darkness with two blades </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--143</p>
<p>Game.Mastering</p>
<p>slashing can do it. More tactically-minded players </p>
<p>can load up on Leadership Edges and direct the </p>
<p>actions of hirelings and allies.</p>
<p>Players who like to think on their feet can take </p>
<p>advantage of acrobatic maneuvers and the like to </p>
<p>describe their heroes’ actions down to the most </p>
<p>minute details.</p>
<p>Exploration</p>
<p>Exploring lost cities, finding forgotten treasures, </p>
<p>or reclaiming fallen civilizations is always exciting </p>
<p>for the players. The Great Unknown lurks behind </p>
<p>every  pile  of  toppled  columns,  and  Incredible </p>
<p>Treasures await those brave enough to take them </p>
<p>from their mysterious guardians.</p>
<p>The trouble with exploration games is that they’re </p>
<p>often difficult for you, the Game Master, to create. </p>
<p>All those incredible surprises, fearsome beasts, </p>
<p>and awesome treasures have to be created by </p>
<p>you. Fortunately, <i>Savage Worlds</i> makes it easy </p>
<p>to quickly create just about any creature, magic </p>
<p>item, or other surprise you can dream up.</p>
<p>One important tip here. The players don’t have </p>
<p>access to your creatures’ statistics unless you give </p>
<p>it to them. If you describe mysterious creatures of </p>
<p>different shapes and sizes, don’t kill yourself trying </p>
<p>to make their statistics vastly different.</p>
<p>Roleplaying</p>
<p>Perhaps the trickiest type of campaign to run is </p>
<p>one that involves deep roleplaying. It’s very easy </p>
<p>to handle rules-wise—there likely won’t be much </p>
<p>die rolling besides a few Persuasion rolls here and </p>
<p>there. The tricky part is handling all the different </p>
<p>Extras and interacting with the player characters. </p>
<p>Again, being able to create characters on the fly </p>
<p>helps tremendously here. You can jot down a few </p>
<p>notes about a character’s most important skills </p>
<p>and then move on. That way you can concentrate </p>
<p>on giving your Extras more personality and worry </p>
<p>less about how good they are at obscure skills.</p>
<p>Another thing to be careful of when running a </p>
<p>game that’s heavy on roleplaying is making sure </p>
<p>there are at least a few events scheduled to give </p>
<p>the group something to talk about. If the majority </p>
<p>of your adventure is a bunch of people standing in </p>
<p>a room, you’re likely in for a slow night. If they’re </p>
<p>in a room trying to find out which one of them is a </p>
<p>murderer, the action will likely be quite exciting. </p>
<p>And if the lights occasionally go out and another </p>
<p>victim  turns  up  dead,  the  interaction  between </p>
<p>characters can easily get as exciting as the most </p>
<p>knock-down, drag-out dungeon crawl.</p>
<p>Horror</p>
<p>Most every game has an element of horror to </p>
<p>it. New Game Masters often ask how to handle </p>
<p>horror in their games. They have visions in their </p>
<p>heads  of  their  friends  sitting  around  shivering </p>
<p>in  utter  terror  as  gruesome  fiends  stalk  their </p>
<p>underpowered investigators.</p>
<p>Unfortunately, that’s not too likely. There may </p>
<p>certainly be moments like that, but more likely, </p>
<p>your group will be sitting around eating nachos </p>
<p>and making bad jokes to each other most of the </p>
<p>night. The worst thing you can do is try and stop </p>
<p>them.  Remember  that  they’re  there  to  have  a </p>
<p>good  time  and  socialize  while  exercising  their </p>
<p>own  imaginations.  Let  them  enjoy  themselves </p>
<p>and  don’t  try  to  be  overly  oppressive  with  the </p>
<p>spooky stuff.</p>
<p>When the time comes and the weirdness begins, </p>
<p>subtly change the tone a bit. Smile and dim the </p>
<p>lights, then turn on some creepy music—just loud </p>
<p>enough for them to hear it without being too overt. </p>
<p>If what you’re running is genuinely creepy, your </p>
<p>group will be relaxed and willing to go with the flow </p>
<p>and let the heebie-jeebies take over. Try and <i>force</i> </p>
<p>them and it will almost certainly backfire.</p>
<p>Whatever  you  do,  when  the  group  finally </p>
<p>encounters some horrid monster, make sure to </p>
<p>describe it rather than refer to it by name. A “large, </p>
<p>lanky, green-skinned creature with drool dripping </p>
<p>from its fangs and beady black eyes” is much more </p>
<p>frightening than “a troll.”</p>
<p>Running.the.Game</p>
<p><i>Savage  Worlds</i>  has  been  designed  from  the </p>
<p>ground up to make the job of the Game Master as </p>
<p>easy as possible. The designers and playtesters </p>
<p>of  this  game  want  to  concentrate  on  playing </p>
<p>the  game  and  making  incredible  and  exciting </p>
<p>memories. We aren’t interested in spending hours </p>
<p>before the game creating statistics for Karlos the </p>
<p>Innkeeper.</p>
<p>That means that when preparing for your game, </p>
<p>you can concentrate on creating intricate plots, </p>
<p>tricky  puzzles,  and  interesting  characters. You </p>
<p>don’t  have  to  do  complex  math  to  make  your </p>
<p>nonplayer  characters  and  monsters,  and  you </p>
<p>certainly don’t need to spend an hour on some </p>
<p>computer program just to make up a few bandits.</p>
<p>Your job is kept easy during the game as well </p>
<p>since there’s very little bookkeeping. You might </p>
<p>have to track a few Wild Card villains’ wounds, but </p>
<p>other than that, the bad guys are up, Shaken, or </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--144</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>removed from play. You can focus on describing </p>
<p>the action instead of trying to record “2 hit points </p>
<p>of damage on the skeleton figure with the chipped </p>
<p>paint on his sword.”</p>
<p>Take advantage of these things to give yourself </p>
<p>a  break  and  run  a  game  like  you  never  have </p>
<p>before. If you <i>want </i>to spend a lot of time on your </p>
<p>campaign, make some cool props or develop your </p>
<p>nonplayer characters’ personalities and plots—not </p>
<p>their game statistics.</p>
<p>Introducing.New.Players</p>
<p>Getting  your  friends  to  try  a  new  game, </p>
<p>especially if they’re used to one system and don’t </p>
<p>like to try many others, can be pretty difficult. We </p>
<p>recommend  downloading  the Test  Drive  rules </p>
<p>from our website and giving them to each of your </p>
<p>friends. This will show them what <i>Savage Worlds</i> </p>
<p>is all about and encourage them to try it at least </p>
<p>once. If you run one of the free adventures from </p>
<p>our site, complete with pregenerated characters, </p>
<p>it should be very easy for everyone to jump in, </p>
<p>play  a  short  session,  and  figure  out  if  it’s  for </p>
<p>them. We hope it is of course. If so, you can then </p>
<p>try  something  with  a  little  more  meat,  such  as </p>
<p><i>Deadlands, Necessary Evil, Slipstream,</i> or even a </p>
<p>world of your own creation. If you like these rules </p>
<p>but have a favorite game world, you can convert </p>
<p>it over (see page 148).</p>
<p>Balance</p>
<p>Some games have very strict rules for how to </p>
<p>balance encounters so that every fight is “fair.” The </p>
<p>battle might be a tough one, but it’s still expected </p>
<p>the heroes can win if they play smart and have </p>
<p>decent luck.</p>
<p>In <i>Savage Worlds</i>, most setpiece encounters </p>
<p>should be reasonably balanced for the expected </p>
<p>power  level  of  the  group.  But  this  isn’t  always </p>
<p>the case.  Sometimes  it’s just  as interesting  for </p>
<p>the  players  to  evaluate  a  situation  and  realize </p>
<p>they probably can’t win—at least not by charging </p>
<p>in  guns  blazing. This  is  desirable  on  occasion </p>
<p>as  it  encourages  the  group  to  think,  talk,  plan, </p>
<p>and come up with clever solutions to the most </p>
<p>challenging situations.</p>
<p>Game Masters should never be afraid to let the </p>
<p>opposition flex their muscles. These are <i>savage</i> </p>
<p>worlds, after all, and triumphing against these foes </p>
<p>should be a major accomplishment—not a given.</p>
<p>Combat.Ratings</p>
<p>Once you’ve played <i>Savage Worlds</i> for a while </p>
<p>you should have a good feel for how many bad </p>
<p>guys to throw at your adventurers. New Game </p>
<p>Masters might want a little guidance. The system </p>
<p>below gives you a baseline to start from, but make </p>
<p>sure  to  also  think  about  additional  advantages </p>
<p>either side might have (such as traps, support, </p>
<p>powerful magic items, or favorable terrain).</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Start  your  estimate  by  getting  a  Combat </p>
<p>Rating  for  each  player  character  equal  to </p>
<p>half the maximum damage he does with his </p>
<p>typical attack. So a fighter with a d8 Strength </p>
<p>and a short sword (Str+d6 damage) has a </p>
<p>Damage Rating of 7 (14/2). A wizard who </p>
<p>typically  uses  the <i> bolt</i>  power  averages  a </p>
<p>standard bolt attack of 2d6, or 6 (12/2).</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Add  +1  for  each  Combat  Edge  or  special </p>
<p>ability,  and  another  +1  for  each  point  of </p>
<p>Toughness over 5.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Do the same for any allies. If a character isn’t </p>
<p>a Wild Card, his Combat Rating is halved.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Now add all the player characters’ Combat </p>
<p>Ratings together to find the party’s Combat </p>
<p>Rating.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Use  the  same  system  for  the  villains </p>
<p>(counting combat special abilities as Combat </p>
<p>Edges).</p>
<p>Now compare each side’s values. If the party’s </p>
<p>Combat Rating is 50, for example, your opposition </p>
<p>should be a little less for an easy encounter, about </p>
<p>even for a moderate encounter, and a little more </p>
<p>for a tough fight.</p>
<p>If  the  villains’  Combat  Rating  is  two  or  more </p>
<p>times the heroes’ total they’re likely going to learn </p>
<p>how to retreat.</p>
<p>Experience</p>
<p>You should usually award two experience points </p>
<p>per game session. That means your players get to </p>
<p>upgrade their characters every two or three game.</p>
<p>When  you  end  longer  adventures,  story  arcs </p>
<p>that  take  four  to  six  sessions  or  so,  you  might </p>
<p>want to award three points, but any more should </p>
<p>be reserved for really big events. Keeping your </p>
<p>average  award  to  two  means  your  characters </p>
<p>progress  at  a  more  natural  rate,  and  won’t  be </p>
<p>Legendary  warriors  after  only  a  few  months  of </p>
<p>play.</p>
<p>Starting.With.Experienced.Characters</p>
<p>In  general,  player  characters  at  the  start  of </p>
<p><i>Savage Worlds</i> campaigns have a little training </p>
<p>and talent, but haven’t had many adventures on </p>
<p>their own yet.</p>
<p>You may occasionally want to start a campaign </p>
<p>with  more  experienced  characters.  That’s </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--145</p>
<p>Game.Mastering</p>
<p>encouraged for really difficult worlds, or for shorter </p>
<p>campaigns where the heroes need to get to the </p>
<p>heart of the action a little quicker. A commando </p>
<p>raid in World War Two, an assault on a lich’s lair, </p>
<p>or super spies infiltrating a diabolical villain’s base </p>
<p>are not adventures for the inexperienced.</p>
<p>Start experienced characters as Seasoned, or </p>
<p>very rarely, Veterans, when first trying this out. </p>
<p>Once you have a good feel for more experienced </p>
<p>characters, you can go as high as you want.</p>
<p>It’s important to the balancing process to make </p>
<p>your players create their characters as Novices </p>
<p>and  then  “bump”  them  up  through  their  four </p>
<p>progressions per Rank. That maintains a more </p>
<p>realistic progression of attributes and skills, and </p>
<p>ensures they can’t take more advanced Edges </p>
<p>than they would otherwise be entitled to.</p>
<p>It’s also easier to do it this way from a player’s </p>
<p>perspective.  It’s  a  little  tricky  to  add  up  all  the </p>
<p>“points” one would have at Seasoned rank. Bump </p>
<p>up a character from a Novice however and the </p>
<p>advancement system is very simple.</p>
<p>Bennies</p>
<p>Experience is very limited—we encourage you </p>
<p>to  give  out  two  points  just  about  every  game </p>
<p>session.  Bennies  are  much  more  flexible,  and </p>
<p>allow you to reward creative players on the spot </p>
<p>for their actions.</p>
<p>You should hand out a Benny anytime a player </p>
<p>does something particularly clever, finds a very </p>
<p>important  clue,  or  generally  advances  the  plot. </p>
<p>You  should  also  hand  out  Bennies  for  great </p>
<p>roleplaying,  particularly  as  it  concerns  their </p>
<p>Hindrances. If a Loyal character jeopardizes his </p>
<p>life to save his comrade, he definitely deserves </p>
<p>a Benny for his efforts. It never hurts to reward a </p>
<p>player for a great line, side-splitting in-game joke, </p>
<p>or even a rare serious and dramatic moment.</p>
<p>Average  players should  get one  or  two extra </p>
<p>Bennies per night. Really good roleplayers may </p>
<p>wind up with two to three.</p>
<p>Interpret.the.Die.Rolls</p>
<p><i>Savage Worlds</i> frequently features wild and </p>
<p>unpredictable die rolls. When this happens, </p>
<p>go  with  it!  If  a  hero  has  to  jump  from </p>
<p>a  wrecked  car  as  it  careens  out  of </p>
<p>control  and  makes  an  amazing </p>
<p>Agility roll, describe how he leaps </p>
<p>into the air, tucks and rolls on </p>
<p>the  ground,  and </p>
<p>comes  up  on </p>
<p>his feet with </p>
<p>perfect balance. Similarly, don’t be afraid to throw </p>
<p>some bad luck at them when they get a really poor </p>
<p>roll. Maybe while using the Chase rules a player </p>
<p>is skiing down a mountain attempting to escape </p>
<p>a rampaging yeti and rolls snake eyes! Describe </p>
<p>how he tumbles in the snow, rolling and tumbling </p>
<p>as the beast bounds after him and prepares to </p>
<p>make its attack!</p>
<p>Your players will love the extra embellishment, </p>
<p>and they’ll feel powerful and cool when the dice </p>
<p>are nice, and feel danger and excitement when </p>
<p>the dice betray them.</p>
<p>Pacing</p>
<p>A good Game Master must pay attention to the </p>
<p>pace  of  the  game.  Sometimes  your  group  will </p>
<p>want to take their time roleplaying their characters, </p>
<p>interacting with the world, or even just joking out </p>
<p>of character and enjoying the social aspect of the </p>
<p>game. If this goes on too long, however, your party </p>
<p>likely needs a little guidance.</p>
<p>This can be especially true in open adventures </p>
<p>such as a murder mystery that requires the players </p>
<p>to figure out where their characters go and what </p>
<p>they want to do (as opposed to a dungeon crawl or </p>
<p>similar adventure where they </p>
<p>really  only </p>
<p>need  to  decide  if  they’re </p>
<p>pressing forward or not).</p>
<p>In  general,  if  most </p>
<p>or all of the group is </p>
<p>roleplaying, smiling, </p>
<p>and having a good </p>
<p>t i m e — l e a v e </p>
<p>them  alone. </p>
<p>Let  them </p>
<p>s e t </p>
<p>the </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--146</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>pace. If you notice some of your players are left </p>
<p>out or looking a little bored, prod them a bit. If </p>
<p>they seem a little lost as to what to do next, ask </p>
<p>one of the group to recount the clues or situation </p>
<p>as they understand it. This will often be enough </p>
<p>to spark an idea or lead they want to follow up </p>
<p>on. You might also introduce new information via </p>
<p>a call or visit from a nonplayer character. Finally, </p>
<p>don’t be afraid to contrive an encounter that gives </p>
<p>the group a new path to follow. Maybe the team </p>
<p>is attacked by the bad guys, and in the aftermath </p>
<p>are able to gather a new clue from the survivors.</p>
<p>Combat.Pacing</p>
<p>Perhaps even more important than pacing at </p>
<p>the  plot  level  is  making  sure  combats  are  fast </p>
<p>and furious. As you count down the Action Cards, </p>
<p>make each player tell you what his character is </p>
<p>doing fairly quickly. If he needs a moment, put his </p>
<p>character on Hold and move on to the next player </p>
<p>(but probably wait if the next actor is a villain).</p>
<p>If  you  feel  the  scene  should  be  particularly </p>
<p>dramatic  and  a  player  doesn’t  announce  his </p>
<p>character’s  intentions  quickly  enough,  start </p>
<p>counting down… “What do you do? 5, 4, 3…!” </p>
<p>That will get all the players’ blood pumping and </p>
<p>reinforce the notion that their characters are in a </p>
<p>dangerous situation that requires quick decisions </p>
<p>and big heroics.</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.The.Countdown</p>
<p><i>The  countdown  is  a  fantastic  tool  for </i></p>
<p><i>instilling  a  sense  of  urgency  into  your </i></p>
<p><i>combats. We’ve run hundreds of games and </i></p>
<p><i>when a player takes too long to figure out </i></p>
<p><i>what they want to do on their turn everyone </i></p>
<p><i>suffers. That’s not to say there aren’t times </i></p>
<p><i>when you want to give them some breathing </i></p>
<p><i>room—particularly if they have a big plan or </i></p>
<p><i>need to look up something really important. </i></p>
<p><i>But most of the time, rush things along. Make </i></p>
<p><i>them feel the urgency of combat. Make them </i></p>
<p><i>a little nervous. Make them realize things are </i></p>
<p><i>desperate and that next die roll better be a </i></p>
<p><i>good one.</i></p>
<p><i>And  of  course,  don’t  be  afraid  to  back </i></p>
<p><i>off  and  give  the  group  time  to  plan  and </i></p>
<p><i>strategize  when  that  makes  sense.  Feel </i></p>
<p><i>the moment and play up the drama befitting </i></p>
<p><i>your  scenario,  the  environment,  and  the </i></p>
<p><i>party’s mood.</i></p>
<p>Extras</p>
<p>The backbone of any good game is the world </p>
<p>that  surrounds  the  player  characters,  and </p>
<p>nonplayer characters are a big part of that. This </p>
<p>section  shows  you  how  to  breathe  life  into  the </p>
<p>supporting cast of your world.</p>
<p>Creating.Extras</p>
<p>Consider this Game Master’s Rule #1 when it </p>
<p>comes to Extras: Don’t “build” them!</p>
<p>Don’t  create  your  Extras  with  the  character </p>
<p>creation rules. Just give them what you think they </p>
<p>ought to have in their various skills and attributes </p>
<p>and move on. Remember this game is supposed </p>
<p>to be easy for you to set up, run, and play. Don’t </p>
<p>sit around adding up skill points for Extras when </p>
<p>you could be designing fiendish traps and thinking </p>
<p>up nasty special abilities for your monsters!</p>
<p>Personality</p>
<p>Far  more  important  than  most  nonplayer </p>
<p>character’s statistics are their personalities. Jot </p>
<p>down a note or two about any Extras the party is </p>
<p>likely to come across so you’ll have some idea how </p>
<p>to run them. Some Game Masters find it useful to </p>
<p>identify prominent Extras with actors or characters </p>
<p>from film, television, books, or comics.</p>
<p>Knowing that the Captain of the City Guard is </p>
<p>“played by Sam Elliot,” for instance, gives you a </p>
<p>good handle on how to handle interactions with </p>
<p>him. He’s likely to be gruff, to the point, and have </p>
<p>a deep, throaty voice.</p>
<p>Adding these extra touches to the characters can </p>
<p>really make them stand out and be remembered </p>
<p>by  your  players  as  well. That  way  the  captain </p>
<p>becomes a memorable character they may call on </p>
<p>in the future rather than just a one-shot resource </p>
<p>they forget about by the next scene. Not every </p>
<p>Extra needs this kind of depth of course, but those </p>
<p>that do add a level of realism and continuity to </p>
<p>your game.</p>
<p>Allies</p>
<p>Though it’s rarely written, most games assume </p>
<p>that  the  Game  Master  controls  the  nonplayer </p>
<p>characters,  both  when  they’re  being  talked </p>
<p>to  and  when  they  fight  alongside  the  player </p>
<p>characters  in  combat.  Most  of  the  time,  this </p>
<p>means the overworked GM simply forgets about </p>
<p>the additional characters during a fight, or shoves </p>
<p>them off to the side and narratively describes what </p>
<p>happens to them. This goes for hirelings as well as </p>
<p>animal companions, sidekicks, or love interests. </p>
<p>The simple fact is that in most games, allies are </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--147</p>
<p>Game.Mastering</p>
<p>a cumbersome complication.</p>
<p><i>Savage Worlds</i> takes a very different approach—</p>
<p>we  turn  control  of  allies  over  to  the  player </p>
<p>characters. The GM acts out these allies when </p>
<p>they’re spoken to, of course, but he should very </p>
<p>rarely, if ever, take them over in combat.</p>
<p>If  you  allow  your  heroes  to  have  allies,  you </p>
<p>can include all the minions your villains should </p>
<p>have  as  well.  Picture  an  ancient  lich  cornered </p>
<p>in his unholy “throne” room. Would he be sitting </p>
<p>there alone? No, he’d be surrounded by scores </p>
<p>of ghoulish undead. These lesser minions make </p>
<p>great  complications  for  your  battles,  and  your </p>
<p>players will have fun bashing through them with </p>
<p>the Extras while their heroes battle the lich and </p>
<p>his more capable lieutenants.</p>
<p>This can take a little getting used to. If you’ve </p>
<p>been Game Mastering other games for a long time </p>
<p>and have a hard time letting go of the nonplayer </p>
<p>characters,  we  suggest  you  try  it  for  a  bit  and </p>
<p>see how it works out. You can always change it if </p>
<p>doesn’t make sense for your group.</p>
<p>Artificial.Inflation</p>
<p>When running <i>Savage Worlds</i>, a lot of Game </p>
<p>Masters become extremely enamored with the ally </p>
<p>aspect of the game. That’s great and it’s what we </p>
<p>intend, but it also sometimes leads to very large </p>
<p>parties of player characters and Extras, which then </p>
<p>demand very large parties of opponents.</p>
<p>It’s okay if you do this—the system can handle </p>
<p>it—but  be  warned  that  a  combat  with  50+ </p>
<p>combatants will take a bit, even with a fast and </p>
<p>furious system. To avoid this kind of “inflation,” pay </p>
<p>attention to the size of your party and its nonplayer </p>
<p>characters, and then remember that you’ll need a </p>
<p>lot of enemies to challenge a large group.</p>
<p>Creating.Worlds</p>
<p>Pinnacle  makes  many  incredible  and  award-</p>
<p>winning settings that we hope you’ll check out, but </p>
<p>it’s also fun to create your own worlds to play in. </p>
<p>The game rules and statistics won’t give you any </p>
<p>trouble after you’ve played even a single game, </p>
<p>and you probably don’t <i>need</i> to create a single </p>
<p>Edge, Hindrance, or power (though you may want </p>
<p>to). That means you can concentrate on what your </p>
<p>world is about, what the heroes do there, what </p>
<p>kinds of fantastic treasures they might find, and </p>
<p>who their opponents are.</p>
<p>The.Name</p>
<p>It’s not necessarily the most important part of the </p>
<p>game, but a good name can really help you nail </p>
<p>down the theme. <i>Evernight,</i> for example, is about </p>
<p>a world of perpetual darkness. <i>Deadlands</i> has a </p>
<p>Western feel, and hints at the underlying horror. </p>
<p>You can just guess what <i>Hell on Earth</i> is all about.</p>
<p>If you can come up with a good name for your </p>
<p>game, it may help everyone instantly realize just </p>
<p>what kind of setting it is.</p>
<p>Iconic.Adventures</p>
<p>Perhaps  the  single  strongest  thing  you  can </p>
<p>think of for your new world is what you expect </p>
<p>a typical adventure to be about. If it’s a fantasy </p>
<p>“dungeon crawler,” you expect the heroes to delve </p>
<p>into  multi-leveled  dungeons  fighting  monsters </p>
<p>and gathering treasure. If it’s  an  anti-corporate </p>
<p>cyberpunk  setting,  you  expect  the  team  to  run </p>
<p>missions against secure buildings and battle in </p>
<p>cyberspace.</p>
<p>This doesn’t mean all your adventures need to </p>
<p>follow this pattern, but if you set this as a baseline </p>
<p>expectation  players  will  quickly  understand  the </p>
<p>world, what it’s about, and most importantly, their </p>
<p>role in it. Knowing the game will mostly be about </p>
<p>Texas Rangers roaming the frontier fighting crime, </p>
<p>for example, gives a player a very strong guideline </p>
<p>for what kind of character he could make.</p>
<p>Scripted.Adventures</p>
<p>Scripted  adventures  are  more  like  interactive </p>
<p>stories. The players can make choices along the </p>
<p>way, but the overall plot advances more or less </p>
<p>intact regardless of what they do. Epic tales must </p>
<p>sometimes follow this path—it’s hard to tell a story </p>
<p>if you don’t know what chapters are to come.</p>
<p>When running a scripted adventure, try not to </p>
<p>make  your  scenarios <i> feel</i>  scripted. The  group </p>
<p>should never feel like they’re just observers, going </p>
<p>along for the ride no matter what they do. Instead, </p>
<p>use  the  situation,  overwhelming  opponents,  or </p>
<p>“down times” to give the group the illusion they </p>
<p>control  the  story  more  than  they  really  do.  In </p>
<p><i>Evernight</i>, for example, there’s a point where the </p>
<p>heroes  can  do  whatever  they  want  for  a  time. </p>
<p>Eventually,  however,  they  are  captured  by  the </p>
<p>villains of the tale, and begin the next episode </p>
<p>of the story.</p>
<p>Situational.Adventures</p>
<p>Situational adventures are much easier to run if </p>
<p>you’re able to think on your feet as you won’t have </p>
<p>quite as much preparation. In these epic tales, you </p>
<p>present a situation of some sort and then just let </p>
<p>the heroes deal with it however they choose. Say </p>
<p>an evil lich has risen and is creating an army of </p>
<p>undead to destroy the living. What do the heroes </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--148</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>do about it? Do they hire on with the local militia? </p>
<p>Do they try and sneak into the cursed lands to </p>
<p>strike down the necromancer himself?</p>
<p>You will need to prepare a few locations, Extras, </p>
<p>and perhaps a few staged encounters ahead of </p>
<p>time. You don’t want to have to figure out what the </p>
<p>lich’s lair looks like on the fly, for example. And </p>
<p>you might want a few “random” encounters to fill </p>
<p>in the gaps between the heroes’ actions.</p>
<p>The.Genre</p>
<p>Your  setting  should  hint  at  what  genre  it’s </p>
<p>in—you  shouldn’t  pick  a  genre  and  then  try  to </p>
<p>shoehorn  your  setting  into  it.  Maybe  you  want </p>
<p>to make a pulp fantasy game that harkens back </p>
<p>to  the  old  Robert  E.  Howard <i> Conan®</i>  stories. </p>
<p>You  could  call  that  pulp,  but  most  people  will </p>
<p>think you’re talking about Indiana Jones® or <i>The </i></p>
<p><i>Shadow®.</i> Call it fantasy and they’ll think you’re </p>
<p>talking about Tolkien or <i>Dungeons &amp; Dragons®.</i></p>
<p>So how about “pulp  fantasy?” That tells  your </p>
<p>friends that there’s not likely to be lots of shining </p>
<p>knights  on white horses  saving princesses. It’s </p>
<p>more likely to feature cunning rogues outwitting </p>
<p>incredibly powerful sorcerers, lost races, forgotten </p>
<p>ruins, and savage combat.</p>
<p>Having a genre description in your head can </p>
<p>go a long way toward helping you figure out what </p>
<p>kinds  of  villains  should  be  present,  and  what </p>
<p>typical adventures your characters might go on.</p>
<p>The.World</p>
<p>Now it’s time to design the world itself. Start with </p>
<p>the area you expect the heroes to adventure in </p>
<p>most of the time. If there’s a city that serves as </p>
<p>their  home  base,  describe  it  in  a  paragraph  or </p>
<p>two. Is it a shining example of law and order? Or </p>
<p>is it a wretched hive of scum and villainy? Now </p>
<p>sketch out some of the surrounding areas. Are </p>
<p>the “Mountains of Dread” just a few miles away? </p>
<p>Or are such places relatively far away from the </p>
<p>centers of population?</p>
<p>There are several software programs available </p>
<p>to  help  you  do  this  if  you  like. The  nice  thing </p>
<p>about using a computer program is that it’s much </p>
<p>easier to make changes should you later decide </p>
<p>the Mountains of Dread were just too close. Or </p>
<p>perhaps the players themselves can add to the </p>
<p>map in exploration campaigns as they discover </p>
<p>new areas and lost cities!</p>
<p>Original.Settings</p>
<p>When creating your own original setting, start </p>
<p>by  identifying  its  core  elements.  Spend  some </p>
<p>time writing down just what makes it so special. </p>
<p>What are the themes? Is it fantasy? Is it science </p>
<p>fiction? Is it science fantasy? Is there a hook? If </p>
<p>so, what is it? (And we highly recommend you </p>
<p>have  a  hook—there  are  already  solid  swords </p>
<p>and sorcery realms with elves and dwarves. Add </p>
<p>something new, like an alien invasion—as we did </p>
<p>in <i>Evernight</i>.) </p>
<p>Once you have clearly identified the thematic </p>
<p>elements that identify your setting, figure out who </p>
<p>the heroes are. These “archetypes” are usually </p>
<p>the best identifiers of new worlds. </p>
<p>Next  figure  out  what  kind  of  adventures  take </p>
<p>place. There’s  no  point  detailing  the  undersea </p>
<p>world  of  Caribdus,  for  example,  if  99%  of  the </p>
<p>action in <i>50 Fathoms</i> takes place above water and </p>
<p>on ships. Focus your efforts where it’s important, </p>
<p>then fill in the extra details when they come up. </p>
<p>The last step you should take is creating new </p>
<p>powers,  Edges,  and  Hindrances.  For  the  most </p>
<p>part,  we’ve  found  you  really  want  to  keep  the </p>
<p>selection to less than a dozen powers, and half </p>
<p>that number of new Edges or Hindrances. A lot of </p>
<p>new Game Masters go crazy creating scores of </p>
<p>each, but at the end of the day find that most of </p>
<p>the stuff people actually take is already covered </p>
<p>in the main <i>Savage Worlds</i> rule book. That’s not </p>
<p>to say you shouldn’t have some cool new powers, </p>
<p>Edges, or Hindrances—just that you should think </p>
<p>them through very carefully, and add them mostly </p>
<p>for flavor or to cover some very unique feature </p>
<p>of the setting that the rules don’t currently cover. </p>
<p>Converted.Settings.</p>
<p>There are literally thousands of great roleplaying </p>
<p>settings  out  there  made  by  other  companies </p>
<p>besides Pinnacle. We’re fans of many of these </p>
<p>brilliant worlds as well. But we usually like playing </p>
<p>them  with <i>Savage Worlds.</i>  Here  are  some tips </p>
<p>we’ve learned after a few years of watching folks </p>
<p>“savage” other games. </p>
<p>Don’t  reinvent  the  wheel. <i> Savage  Worlds</i> </p>
<p>was  designed  for  quick  play  with  minimal  rule </p>
<p>interference. All  too  often  conversions  of  other </p>
<p>games try to account for every element —massive </p>
<p>skill lists, minute details, and hundreds of powers. </p>
<p>This isn’t <i>Savage Worlds</i>, and bringing in those </p>
<p>elements is likely to burden the rules to the point </p>
<p>where the game play is no longer fast, furious, </p>
<p>or fun. </p>
<p>Like  an  original  setting,  identify  the  themes </p>
<p>of the other game and try to adapt with a very </p>
<p>few key world rules. Literal translations of game </p>
<p>mechanics from other systems usually just result </p>
<p>in cumbersome sub-systems that don’t add one </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--149</p>
<p>Game.Mastering</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--150</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>minute of fun to the Savage version. </p>
<p>There is a often a strong desire to create lots of </p>
<p>new skills, Edges, and powers to fit your setting. </p>
<p>That’s  understandable,  but  it  isn’t  always  the </p>
<p>best path.</p>
<p>Remember  that  more  skills  dilutes  the  pool </p>
<p>from which the players can choose. It weakens </p>
<p>the overall character, because now their 15 skill </p>
<p>points have to be spread further. Creating a list of </p>
<p>20 different firearm skills might be more realistic, </p>
<p>but the system wasn’t designed that way and it </p>
<p>becomes a major burden to the players. Creating </p>
<p>one new Edge that allows a person to specialize </p>
<p>in a specific gun is a better way to go if that’s </p>
<p>particularly  important  to  the  feel  of  your  game </p>
<p>(as it might be in a high-tech military campaign). </p>
<p>Remember too that a lot of what you’re looking </p>
<p>for might already be in the rules. Do you need </p>
<p>a sniper Edge? That’s Marksman. Do you need </p>
<p>a Mechanic skill? Why not use Repair? Do you </p>
<p>need a magic missile power? Why not use <i>bolt</i>?</p>
<p>When you do decide to add a new skill, try to </p>
<p>let it cover as broad a range of related topics as </p>
<p>possible. There’s  no  need  to  create  Dancing, </p>
<p>Oratory, Acting,  and  Singing  Skills  in  most </p>
<p>campaigns when a single Perform skill does the </p>
<p>trick.</p>
<p>When converting monsters and Extras, compare </p>
<p>them to existing creatures in this rule book. That’s </p>
<p>much  easier—and  much  less  confining—than </p>
<p>trying to convert every attribute, skill, and hit point. </p>
<p>Design.Note.-.The.Elevator.Pitch</p>
<p><i>There’s a term used in Hollywood called </i></p>
<p><i>“the Elevator Pitch.” The idea is that you’re </i></p>
<p><i>a  young  and  hungry  screenwriter  trying  to </i></p>
<p><i>pitch an idea to an executive. You can’t get </i></p>
<p><i>in to see him, but one day he’s in the elevator </i></p>
<p><i>and you have about 30 seconds to make him </i></p>
<p><i>understand your idea, why it’s cool, and why </i></p>
<p><i>he’d want to make it.</i></p>
<p><i>The  idea  of  the  elevator  pitch  is  strong </i></p>
<p><i>for your campaign as well. If it takes many </i></p>
<p><i>minutes to explain what the game is about </i></p>
<p><i>to your potential players, it’s too much. You </i></p>
<p><i>can certainly do all the things you’ve talked </i></p>
<p><i>about or have them as part of your backstory, </i></p>
<p><i>but they need to be revealed during play as </i></p>
<p><i>they’re important to the characters.</i></p>
<p><i>If your game can be summed up succinctly, </i></p>
<p><i>the players will get it quickly as well. You’ll </i></p>
<p><i>also know very quickly if they’re excited about </i></p>
<p><i>it and then can ask you for the details they’re </i></p>
<p><i>particularly interested in.</i></p>
<p><i>Here are the elevator pitches for some of </i></p>
<p><i>Pinnacle’s games:</i></p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Deadlands:</b>  Weird  Western  horror  where </p>
<p>heroes  are  sometimes  so  tough  they  come </p>
<p>back from the dead.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>50 Fathoms:</b> A fantastic and colorful world is </p>
<p>drowning from a witch’s curse. Heroes from </p>
<p>that  world  and  our  own  earth  must  travel </p>
<p>the seas to stop the flooding and defeat the </p>
<p>witches.</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Necessary Evil:</b> When all the superheroes are </p>
<p>gone, the only ones left to save the world from </p>
<p>an evil alien invasion are the supervillains!</p>
<p>•	</p>
<p><b>Weird Wars: </b>The violence of war gives rise </p>
<p>to dark things. Soldiers, sailors, and airmen of </p>
<p>every nation must battle their enemies as well </p>
<p>as the horrible things that grow in the shadows.</p>
<p>Races</p>
<p>A race of super-intelligent titans are simply going </p>
<p>to be more powerful in game terms than humans. </p>
<p>That’s  fine  if  everyone  is  playing  a  titan,  but  if </p>
<p>they’re not, the GM needs to do a little balancing.</p>
<p>Guidelines  for  creating  new  player  character </p>
<p>races can be found on page 22, but if the entire </p>
<p>campaign world is more difficult (or less difficult), </p>
<p>don’t  be  afraid  to  amp  up  (or  tone  down)  the </p>
<p>heroes.</p>
<p>Perhaps  the  player  characters  are  all  giant </p>
<p>monsters, as are their foes. Or perhaps the heroes </p>
<p>are  intelligent  rats.  Both  campaigns  could  be </p>
<p>extremely  interesting—and  require  stronger  or </p>
<p>weaker starting statistics.</p>
<p>New.Edges.&amp;.Hindrances</p>
<p>Now  comes  one  of  the  trickiest  parts  of  the </p>
<p>game. You may want to create some new Edges </p>
<p>&amp; Hindrances for your setting. First, realize that </p>
<p>you <i>probably</i> don’t have to. What’s in this book </p>
<p>covers an awful lot of character types.</p>
<p>What  you  really  may  want  to  look  at  are </p>
<p>Professional Edges. These help you create the </p>
<p>archetypal  characters  of  your  world. A <i> Savage </i></p>
<p><i>Worlds</i> character with the Woodsman Edge, for </p>
<p>example, is a “ranger” in most swords and sorcery </p>
<p>games.</p>
<p>If there’s a particular character type common to </p>
<p>your world, this is the way to encourage your group </p>
<p>to play them. Let’s say you’re creating a far-future </p>
<p><i>Matrix</i>-type  world  with  vampires,  for  example, </p>
<p>and you want to create one or more Professional </p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--151</p>
<p>Game.Mastering</p>
<p>Edges for vampire hunters. You could start with </p>
<p>something useful but fairly low-powered, let’s call </p>
<p>it Hunter. These are guys who know the vampires </p>
<p>exist and have fought them before. Maybe their </p>
<p>special ability is that they never make Fear checks </p>
<p>when confronted by vampires. To reflect the fact </p>
<p>that they’ve had to face them before, you set the </p>
<p>requirement as Novice, Fighting d8+, and Spirit </p>
<p>of d8+.</p>
<p>Maybe  later  on,  you  create  Vampire  Hunter. </p>
<p>These  guys  have  learned  how  to  stake  the </p>
<p>bloodsuckers  in  the  heart,  and  halve  penalties </p>
<p>for such called shots. Maybe an improved version </p>
<p>negates the penalty altogether.</p>
<p>If  there’s  a  cardinal  rule  to  Edges,  it’s  that </p>
<p>you don’t want to grant flat bonuses to combat </p>
<p>or  arcane  skills.  It’s  okay  if  they  only  apply  in </p>
<p>certain situations, but don’t give gunslingers +2 </p>
<p>to Shooting all the time, or martial artists +2 to </p>
<p>Fighting  all  the  time. You’ll  really  throw  off  the </p>
<p>scale of the game if you do that, particularly as </p>
<p>they reach higher Ranks. You can give them flat </p>
<p>bonuses to noncombat skills, but be careful not </p>
<p>to stack them too high with those already found </p>
<p>in this book.</p>
<p>Finally, don’t feel you need a ton of new Edges </p>
<p>&amp; Hindrances. What you’re looking for is those </p>
<p>few bits that make the world stand out, or enable </p>
<p>a particular character archetype to function more </p>
<p>effectively than he could if the new rules weren’t </p>
<p>there. It’s not a good idea to go scouring every </p>
<p>other book we’ve done and import all the Combat </p>
<p>Edges into your world—it’s overwhelming to the </p>
<p>players, unnecessary, and moves the game away </p>
<p>from what it’s supposed to be: streamlined fun.</p>
<p>Design.Note.-.Less.Is.More</p>
<p><i>We’ve seen hundreds of new settings now, </i></p>
<p><i>both  those  which  have  been  published  by </i></p>
<p><i>fans and licensees as well as submissions </i></p>
<p><i>that have never gone public. One thing we </i></p>
<p><i>see way too much is the “everything and the </i></p>
<p><i>kitchen  sink”  approach.  It’s  overwhelming </i></p>
<p><i>and it just doesn’t make sense. Does your </i></p>
<p><i>Cthulhu-inspired 1920s campaign really need </i></p>
<p><i>all  the  Combat  Edges  from  Weird  Wars? </i></p>
<p><i>Does your expedition to Mars really need all </i></p>
<p><i>the Professional Edges from 50 Fathoms?</i></p>
<p><i>Sometimes less is more. Use the core book </i></p>
<p><i>and anything you just can’t live without. For </i></p>
<p><i>everything else, let the players decide if they </i></p>
<p><i>want it. Then you can allow it, or not.</i></p>
<p>Trim.the.Fat!</p>
<p>Now  that  you  have  your  theme  and  your </p>
<p>mechanics are in place, it’s time to go back and </p>
<p>get rid of the stuff that doesn’t fit or isn’t needed. </p>
<p>Does your fantasy setting really need elves and </p>
<p>dwarves?  Or  did  you  include  them  because </p>
<p>every  fantasy  setting  since <i> The  Hobbit®</i>  has </p>
<p>them? Would anything be lost by their removal? </p>
<p>Could something else take their place? Do you </p>
<p>really need those extensive computer rules in a </p>
<p>sci-fi setting? <i>Star Trek®</i> might need extensive </p>
<p>computer rules—<i>Star Wars®</i> does not. Even then, </p>
<p>a Knowledge (Computer Use) skill probably does </p>
<p>everything you need. </p>
<p>Final.Note</p>
<p>At all times, remember to keep it “FFF”—or Fast, </p>
<p>Furious, and Fun! All progress in creating a setting </p>
<p>should further the setting’s feel, but never at the </p>
<p>expense of FFF. These are some of the lessons </p>
<p>we think work for most people and most settings, </p>
<p>and have been echoed on our forums and listservs </p>
<p>since <i>Savage Worlds</i> came out in March of 2003. </p>
<p>But it’s your game, and you need to decide what </p>
<p>works best for you.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--152</p>
<p>We’ve  talked  about  heroes  and  their  antics. </p>
<p>Now it’s time to deal with the monsters, villains, </p>
<p>and bad guys. Below are some abilities common </p>
<p>to many monsters and villains.</p>
<p>Nonplayer characters and monsters can have </p>
<p>any regular Edges or Hindrances the GM feels </p>
<p>are  appropriate  and  are  not  made  like  player </p>
<p>characters. Just give them the abilities you want </p>
<p>them to have and spend your time and mental </p>
<p>energy on better things, like the plot of the game </p>
<p>or how best to entertain your group.</p>
<p>Monstrous.Abilities</p>
<p>Aquatic</p>
<p>The creature is native to the water. It is a natural </p>
<p>swimmer and cannot drown. While in the water, </p>
<p>its Pace is generally equal to its Swimming skill, </p>
<p>but some creatures (usually fish) may have much </p>
<p>higher movement rates.</p>
<p>Armor</p>
<p>A  creature’s Armor  adds  to  the  creature’s </p>
<p>Toughness  (already  added  in  to  its  statistics), </p>
<p>usually  in  all  locations.  Thick,  leathery  hide </p>
<p>generally  offers  2  points  of Armor.  “Armored” </p>
<p>creatures like a stegosaurus generally have 4 or </p>
<p>more points of protection. Supernatural creatures </p>
<p>may  have  much  higher Armor  values. A  living </p>
<p>statue, for example, might have 8 points of Armor </p>
<p>or more.</p>
<p>Burrowing</p>
<p>From  massive  worms  to  sand-dwelling </p>
<p>humanoids, many creatures are able to burrow </p>
<p>beneath  the  earth  and  move  within  it.  These </p>
<p>creatures can tunnel underground and reappear </p>
<p>elsewhere for devastating surprise attacks against </p>
<p>their foes. The distance a creature can burrow in a </p>
<p>turn is written immediately after its Burrow ability. </p>
<p>A  burrowing  creature  may  tunnel  on  its  action, </p>
<p>and may erupt from the ground at any point within </p>
<p>its burrowing Pace the same round if desired. It </p>
<p>cannot be attacked while beneath the earth unless </p>
<p>the attacker has some special means of detecting </p>
<p>it and penetrating the intervening dirt.</p>
<p>Burrowing  creatures  strike  by  erupting  from </p>
<p>beneath  their  opponents  and  taking  them  by </p>
<p>surprise.  When  this  occurs,  the  Burrowing </p>
<p>creature makes an opposed Stealth roll versus </p>
<p>the target’s Notice. If the creature wins, it gains +2 </p>
<p>to attack and damage that round, or +4 if it gets a </p>
<p>raise. If the victim wins and was on Hold, he may </p>
<p>try to interrupt the burrower’s attack as usual.</p>
<p>Construct</p>
<p>Robots,  golems,  and  other  animated  objects </p>
<p>are  collectively  called  “constructs.”  Some  are </p>
<p>sentient beings while others are mere automatons </p>
<p>following the will of a hidden master.</p>
<p>Whatever their origin or material, such beings </p>
<p>have several inherent advantages over creatures </p>
<p>of flesh and blood:</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Constructs  add  +2  when  attempting  to </p>
<p>recover from being Shaken.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Constructs do not suffer additional damage </p>
<p>from called shots (unless otherwise specified </p>
<p>in their description).</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Construct  Wild  Cards  never  suffer  from </p>
<p>Wound Modifiers.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Constructs  do  not  suffer  from  disease  or </p>
<p>poison.</p>
<p>Elemental</p>
<p>Air,  earth,  fire,  and  water  form  the  basis  of </p>
<p>the  elemental  realms,  wherein  dwell  strange, </p>
<p>unfathomable creatures.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Elementals suffer no additional damage from </p>
<p>called shots.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Elementals are Fearless.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Elemental  Wild  Cards  never  suffer  from </p>
<p>Wound Modifiers.</p>
<p>• </p>
<p>Elementals  do  not  suffer  from  disease  or </p>
<p>poison.</p>
<p>Ethereal</p>
<p>Ghosts, shadows, will-o’-the-wisps, and similar </p>
<p>intangible creatures have no form in the physical </p>
<p>world  (or  can  turn  it  on  and  off  at  will). They </p>
<p>cannot be harmed by physical attacks, and cannot </p>
<p>Chapter.Seven:.</p>
<p>Bestiary</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--153</p>
<p>even be seen unless they desire to be. Ethereal </p>
<p>creatures are always affected by magical items, </p>
<p>weapons, and supernatural powers.</p>
<p>Most ethereal creatures can still affect things </p>
<p>in the physical world—throwing objects, wielding </p>
<p>ghostly  swords,  or  even  pushing  heroes  down </p>
<p>long, dark stairs.</p>
<p>Fear</p>
<p>Particularly  frightening  monsters  cause  Fear </p>
<p>checks to all who see them. Some truly terrifying </p>
<p>monsters may inflict penalties on Fear checks as </p>
<p>well. A creature with Fear –2, for instance, causes </p>
<p>those who see it to make their Fear checks at –2. </p>
<p>See the <b>Fear </b>rules on page 97 for effects.</p>
<p>Fearless</p>
<p>Mindless creatures, some undead, robots, and </p>
<p>the like don’t suffer from the weaknesses of the </p>
<p>mortal mind. Fearless creatures never suffer from </p>
<p>Fear effects and cannot be affected by Intimidation </p>
<p>(though Taunt may still work).</p>
<p>Flight</p>
<p>The  creature  can  fly  at  the  listed  Pace  and </p>
<p>ignores the effects of difficult ground. The flyer </p>
<p>also  has  a  Climb  score  that  reflects  its  in-air </p>
<p>maneuverability. The number is entirely relative </p>
<p>and is mostly used for Chases to determine if the </p>
<p>beast is more maneuverable than its foes (see </p>
<p>page 94).</p>
<p>Gargantuan</p>
<p>Gargantuan  creatures  are  those  that  are  at </p>
<p>least  size  9  or  better.  Classic  movie  monsters </p>
<p>like <i>Godzilla</i> or <i>King Kong</i> fit into this category. </p>
<p>Gargantuans have Heavy Armor, so they can only </p>
<p>be hurt by Heavy Weapons, and all their attacks </p>
<p>count as Heavy Weapons as well.</p>
<p>Gargantuans suffer the penalty for being Huge: </p>
<p>+4 to attack rolls from man-sized creatures.</p>
<p>When a Gargantuan creature makes a stomp </p>
<p>attack (Game Master’s call), add its Size to its </p>
<p>damage roll, but subtract the Size of the foe as </p>
<p>well. Don’t add the size of inanimate obstacles </p>
<p>such  as  vehicles,  buildings,  or  ships—that’s </p>
<p>already figured into their Toughness.</p>
<p>Hardy</p>
<p>Very  tough  and  resilient  creatures  do  not  fall </p>
<p>from lesser wounds, no matter how many they </p>
<p>suffer. A decisive blow is needed to put one of </p>
<p>these tenacious creatures down.</p>
<p>If the beast is Shaken, further Shaken results </p>
<p>have  no  further  effect—they  do  not  cause  a </p>
<p>wound.</p>
<p>Infection</p>
<p>A vampire’s bite, a horrid spider-like creature </p>
<p>that  injects  eggs  into  its  victim’s  wounds,  or </p>
<p>even the disease-born scratching of rats are all </p>
<p>examples of Infection.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--154</p>
<p>Savage.Worlds</p>
<p>Whenever a character is Shaken or wounded </p>
<p>by a creature with Infection, the victim must make </p>
<p>a Vigor roll. Modifiers to the roll are listed in the </p>
<p>creature’s description, as are the effects of failure.</p>
<p>Infravision</p>
<p>Nocturnal  beasts  often  see  in  the  infrared </p>
<p>spectrum—meaning they can “see” by detecting </p>
<p>heat. Creatures with Infravision halve penalties </p>
<p>(round  down)  for  bad  lighting  when  attacking </p>
<p>living targets.</p>
<p> Clever characters may figure out ways to mask </p>
<p>their heat from such creatures. Smearing cold mud </p>
<p>over one’s body or wearing special heat-filtering </p>
<p>suits  generally  obscures  the  target  from  those </p>
<p>with Infravision. Creatures with Infravision almost </p>
<p>always have normal sight as well.</p>
<p>Immunity</p>
<p>Creatures born in fire aren’t affected by heat, </p>
<p>and a horror made of pure lightning won’t suffer </p>
<p>from a <i>bolt</i> attack with an electrical trapping.</p>
<p>Immunities  are  always  to  specific  types  of </p>
<p>attacks, such as fire, cold, electricity, and so on. </p>
<p>Such  creatures  aren’t  Invulnerable,  they  just </p>
<p>ignore  damage  from  the  specific  attack  types </p>
<p>named.</p>
<p>Invulnerability</p>
<p>Savage Settings<b> </b>are filled with violent combat, </p>
<p>but many often feature desperate puzzle-solving </p>
<p>or dark research into unholy horrors as well. To </p>
<p>defeat  an  Invulnerable  creature,  you’ll  need  a </p>
<p>little of both.</p>
<p>Invulnerable creatures can be Shaken, but they </p>
<p>can’t be wounded by anything but their Weakness </p>
<p>(all such creatures have at least one if not more). </p>
<p>An  ancient  dark  god  given  life  by  misguided </p>
<p>cultists, for example, might be immune to mortal </p>
<p>weapons, but is vulnerable to shards of stained </p>
<p>glass gathered from a church.</p>
<p>Low.Light.Vision</p>
<p>Many monsters, and even fantasy races such as </p>
<p>elves and dwarves, are typically able to see in all </p>
<p>but the blackest darkness. Low light vision ignores </p>
<p>penalties for Dim and Dark lighting, allowing the </p>
<p>creature to see in all but pitch black conditions.</p>
<p>Paralysis</p>
<p>Certain  creatures  and  poisons  can  instantly </p>
<p>paralyze  a  foe,  rendering  the  victim  easy  prey </p>
<p>for the thing’s dark designs or ravenous appetite. </p>
<p>A target who suffers damage or a Shaken result </p>
<p>from such a creature must make a Vigor roll or </p>
<p>be paralyzed and incapable of any action—even </p>
<p>speech—for 2d6 rounds or longer.</p>
<p>Poison</p>
<p>Snakes,  venom-coated  daggers,  and  so  on </p>
<p>afflict their victims with dangerous poison. Poisons </p>
<p>are  described  in  more  detail  in  the <b> Hazards</b> </p>
<p>section.</p>
<p>A creature with the Poison ability typically injects </p>
<p>it via a bite or scratch. To do so, the thing must </p>
<p>cause at least a Shaken result to the victim, who </p>
<p>then makes a Vigor roll modified by the strength </p>
<p>of  the  poison  (listed  in  parentheses  after  the </p>
<p>creature’s Poison ability). The effects of failure are </p>
<p>described in each creature’s description.</p>
<p>Regeneration</p>
<p>Legend  has  it  that  trolls,  vampires,  and </p>
<p>certain  other  types  of  legendary  creatures  can </p>
<p>Regenerate damage caused to them.</p>
<p>Regeneration  comes  in  two  types:  Fast  and </p>
<p>Slow.</p>
<p>• Fast  Regeneration  lets  a  wounded  creature </p>
<p>make  a  Vigor  roll  every  round  to  heal  any </p>
<p>damage it has sustained—even after it has been </p>
<p>“killed.” A success heals one wound (or removes </p>
<p>Incapacitated  status),  and  a  raise  heals  an </p>
<p>additional wound. Most creatures with this ability </p>
<p>also have a Weakness or Vulnerability, such as </p>
<p>fire. Wounds from the creature’s Weakness or </p>
<p>Vulnerability  do  not  regenerate,  but  may  still </p>
<p>heal naturally. Creatures with Fast Regeneration </p>
<p>also add +2 to Spirit rolls made to recover from </p>
<p>being Shaken.</p>
<p>• Slow Regeneration means the creature won’t be </p>
<p>suddenly healing itself during a fight, but may </p>
<p>recover its wounds quickly between encounters. </p>
<p>Slow regenerators make a natural Healing roll </p>
<p>once per day.</p>
<p>Size</p>
<p>A creature’s size has a lot to do with how much </p>
<p>damage it can take, so we add a modifier to its </p>
<p>Toughness to reflect its tremendous mass. Note </p>
<p>that a beast’s size has nothing to do with Vigor—</p>
<p>even a mighty kraken can catch a cold or tire out.</p>
<p>The Toughness  modifiers  on  the  table  below </p>
<p>represent average specimens of particular species </p>
<p>for comparison, but there are always exceptions. </p>
<p>Not every great white has a +4 Toughness bonus. </p>
<p>A young specimen might have a +3, while a larger </p>
<p>fish might be +5 or even +6. The same is true for </p>
<p>humans. Small humans suffer a –1 penalty, while </p>
<p>those  with  the  Brawny  Edge  gain  a  +1  bonus. </p>
<p>Use the table as a baseline when creating your </p>
<p>own creatures then adjust for particularly large or </p>
<p>smaller versions.</p>
<h1 style="page-break-before:always; "></h1>
<p>--Page--155</p>
<p>Bestiary</p>
<p>Toughness.Modifiers</p>
<p><b> </b></p>
<p><b>Mod </b></p>
<p><b>Size of a...</b></p>
<p> </p>
<p>–2 </p>
<p>Cat, fairy, pixie, large rat, dog</p>
<p> </p>
<p>–1 </p>
<p>Large dog, bobcat, half-folk, goblin, </p>
<p>small human</p>
<p> </p>
<p>0 </p>
<p>Human</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+1 </p>
<p>Orc</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+2 </p>
<p>Bull, gorilla, bear, horse</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+3 </p>
<p>Ogre, kodiak bear</p>
<p> </p>
<p>+4 </p>
<p>Rhino, great white shark</p>
<p> </p>

<p>Small elephant</p>
